PEOPLE · Modern Wisdom

纳瓦尔一个赢完游戏、转身教你"想要得更少"的硅谷哲学家

不想要一样东西，和拥有它一样好。
Chris × 纳瓦尔 · 全长 3 小时 16 分 · 中英对照

① 他是谁

纳瓦尔·拉维坎特（Naval Ravikant），1974 年生，AngelList 创始人、硅谷顶级天使投资人——早年早早投中过 Twitter、Uber、Stack Overflow 等一批公司。但让他真正"出圈"的，不是投资战绩，而是 2018 年那条叫《How to Get Rich（without getting lucky）》的推特长帖，以及后来被人汇编成的《纳瓦尔宝典》。他被冠以"硅谷哲学家王""科技佛陀"之类的名号——一个会把"如何变富、如何幸福"讲成一套清晰算法的人。（生平/出生年/身家等见《校订记录》与背景研究，引用前请核；他本人否认"亿万富翁"传闻，外界身家估算分歧极大。）

而你在这场对谈里看到的纳瓦尔，是一个已经"赢完了游戏"、转身回来劝你少玩几局的人。他不再急着证明什么——开场就用苏格拉底和第欧根尼的典故定调：不想要一样东西，和拥有它一样好。整场三个多小时，他反复在做同一件事：把那些被我们当成"人生默认设置"的东西——成功、地位、名声、忙碌、掌控——一个个拆开，告诉你哪些是正和的、值得玩，哪些是零和的、是跑步机，以及为什么"想要得更少"本身就是一种自由。

一句话定位他：一个用第一性原理把"财富与幸福"拆成普世算法、自己却越来越像第欧根尼的科技思想家——他教你怎么变富，前提是让你先看清"你到底在玩哪一场游戏、以及那场游戏值不值得赢"。

② 生平（极简主线）

- 草根移民起点：1974 年生于印度新德里，9 岁随单亲母亲移民美国，在纽约皇后区长大；考进史岱文森精英高中，后读达特茅斯学院（计算机+经济）。（籍贯/学历见背景研究，⚠️ 引用前核）

- 第一桶金与著名教训：1999 年 .com 泡沫期共同创办点评网站 Epinions（2003 年与比价站 DealTime 合并组成 Shopping.com、2004 年上市）。2005 年那场著名的"创始人起诉投资人"诉讼，让他切身懂了股权、信任与"和什么人一起玩"。

- 投资人的天使：2007 年起以 The Hit Forge 等做早期投资，投中 Twitter、Uber、Stack Overflow 等；2010 年共同创办 AngelList，把"天使投资"本身平台化。

- 思想符号成型：2018 年发《How to Get Rich》推特长帖，开篇即"追求财富，而非金钱或地位；财富是你睡觉时仍在为你赚钱的资产"，两大主轴是杠杆（leverage）与复利（compounding）。后来被 Eric Jorgenson 汇编成免费出版的《纳瓦尔宝典》（注意：作者是编者，纳瓦尔本人不从该书赚钱）。

- 此刻的他：已"过了缺钱那关"，把大量精力转向"想清楚、讲清楚"——做播客、上别人的播客，强调自己的话"不可压缩"。本期是他久未做长访谈后的一次回归。

③ 为何受访 · 为什么是这场对谈

这期没有"卖货"动机，主题就是赤裸裸的一个大问题："赢得人生这场游戏"到底意味着什么。 Chris Williamson（Modern Wisdom 主持人，英国人，从夜店推广/真人嘉宾一路做成英语世界顶级思想类播客主持）是个极擅长提问、又愿意"接话补全"的对谈者；纳瓦尔则是少数能把抽象问题讲得既精准又好懂的嘉宾。

要理解这场对谈，得抓住纳瓦尔反复点出的一条暗线——他和 Chris 都在做一件相似的事：用一档"对话"来对抗"表演"。纳瓦尔说他做播客有"自尊心在作祟"，但更重要的动机是：他的推特金句早被"成千上万人和 ChatGPT"模仿抄走了，140 字的格言已经不稀缺；真正稀缺的是深度理解——"如果你真懂一样东西，你能就它聊上一整天，从第一性原理把一切重新推导出来，根本不用背。"所以他不愿"出续集"、不愿把同一句话说两遍，只愿在具体语境里把问题真正解开。

所以这场对谈本质上是：一个已经不需要再证明自己的人，借一个同样反感"表演"的对谈者，把自己这些年想透的'人生算法'，在具体语境里再推导一遍——讲给听众，也讲给他自己。

④ 他代表哪类人（可迁移）

纳瓦尔是一类人的极致样本：赢过物质游戏、却把主要精力用来"想清楚"的科技思想家。从他身上能抽出几条对普通人也极其受用的迁移点——

1. 分清"财富游戏"和"地位游戏"——只玩正和的那个。

这是全场最锋利的框架。他把人类的游戏分两种：财富创造是正和的（你做出一个产品、规模化，大家可以一起变富，不必跟谁厮杀），地位是零和的（"它是一架排名的梯子，你升一格就有人降一格，永远是对抗性的"）。Chris 补刀："你倒是给我指指，哪儿能拿地位去银行兑现？"更狠的是后半段：地位游戏你永远"赢不完收不了手"（iTunes、YouTube、福布斯榜让你每天和同侪较量、上上下下），而财富"在某个水平上是可以满足的"。迁移到普通人：你每天焦虑的那件事，到底是在创造价值（正和），还是只是在和别人比排名（零和）？把精力从后者挪到前者，痛苦会少一大半。他甚至给了路径建议——先把钱赚到手，再去追名声，比"先红了再靠红变现"那条路容易得多。

2. 在 AI 时代，记忆一文不值，稀缺的是"理解、判断、品味"。

他对"聪明"下了一个冷酷的重新定义："ChatGPT 已经把整个互联网背下来了，你拿记忆跟它拼，祝你好运。"——所以记忆的价值在一天天归零，真正值钱的是理解、判断和品味，而这三样"只能从遇到真实问题、解决它、再找出共性"里长出来。配套那条他和 Chris 都激赏的启发式："如果你不得不去背一样东西，那是因为你没真懂它；真懂了，就不必背。" 对任何学习者这都是一记重锤：别再追求"记住更多"，要追求"理解到能从第一性原理重新推导出来"。

3. "特定知识"无法传授——你的真正护城河，是你能推导、而非背诵的东西。

他很清醒地区分了自己的"特定知识（specific knowledge）"在哪："不在幸福，不在哲学；而在于能把一门科技生意从接缝处拆开、提前判断它成不成。"这种知识的特点是教不会、抄不走，因为它高度依赖语境、来自大量真实数据的积累。迁移：别人能搜到、能背到、AI 能生成的，都不是你的护城河；只有那些"换个新问题你也能现场推导出来"的判断力，才是。

4. 注意力，才是人生唯一的货币。

他把"什么是人生最根本的资源"层层剥到底：不是钱（"问问巴菲特，钱能给他买来多少时间"），也不是时间（"时间会被浪费，因为你根本没真正在场"），而是注意力——"你选择把注意力放在什么上、并为此做了什么。"结论很硬："注意力是你唯一拥有的东西。"迁移到信息过载的今天：刷到的每一条新闻、每一次比较、每一段被过去俘获的反刍，花的都是这枚唯一的硬币。

⑤ 普通与不普通

普通的地方，普通得很坦诚：

- 他大方承认"自尊心在作祟"（"this is ego at play, so I'll admit it freely"）——做播客有炫耀的成分，他不装。

- 他承认自己大多数时候是错的："就像买彩票，只不过你事先知道一两个号码……Clubhouse 就是个反例。"一个顶级投资人，公开说自己经常看走眼。

- 他承认那条封神的帖子是标题党："'How to Get Rich'是个故意取的标题党，本该叫'How to Create Wealth'。"

- 他承认自己被抄烂了："我开创了'哲学推特'，现在我们这样的人有成千上万，ChatGPT 整天在生产这些玩意儿。"

不普通的地方，是一种罕见的"元认知"：

- 他能在'提问的层级'上回答问题。 面对"人生有没有意义""有没有自由意志"这类千古悖论，他给了一把万能钥匙："这些问题之所以像悖论，是因为你用'一个此时此地的人'的身份去问，却用'宇宙在无限时间里'的视角去答——你偷换了层级。问题应该在它被提出的那个层级上回答。"一个个体此刻问"我有自由意志吗"，答案就是"有"；只有当你假装自己是宇宙时，答案才变成"没有"。这种"先看清问题被问在哪一层"的本事，比任何具体答案都更可迁移。

- 他把"想要得更少"修炼成了能力。 从开场的第欧根尼，到"欲望是一纸契约——在如愿之前你都不会快乐"，他真正不普通的，是把"克制欲望"当成一种主动的自由训练，而不是失败者的自我安慰。

- 他愿意当众更新自己。 被问"最近改了什么看法"，他答："我现在没以前那么'自由放任'了，我开始认为文化和宗教是人类很好的'协作系统'。"一个以"个人自由"著称的人，公开承认自己在往集体协作那一端挪——这是稀缺的诚实。

一句话概括他的不普通：他不是最有钱的，也未必是最聪明的，但他可能是最舍得花力气把"人生到底在玩什么游戏"想清楚、并且敢于'想要得更少'的那一个。

⑥ 反认知洞察（这场对谈里最值得带走的几颗钉子）

1. 别玩零和的地位游戏，去玩正和的财富游戏——而且地位"赢不完"，财富能满足。

最反直觉的一点：我们本能追逐的"地位"其实是进化留下的旧程序（狩猎采集时代没有财富、只有地位），而它恰恰是有限的、对抗性的、无法在银行兑现的、且永远停不下来的跑步机。财富创造反而是正和的、可无限扩展的、能真正满足的。启示：先认清你正在玩哪一场，再决定要不要继续玩。 很多人拼命争的那点"排名",赢了也无处兑现，还永远赢不完。

2. "不想要"和"拥有"一样好——欲望是一纸让你在如愿前都不快乐的契约。

他用第欧根尼开场、用"欲望是 a contract you sign to be unhappy until you get the thing"收尾，把消费主义的底层逻辑掀了个底朝天：每多一个"我要得到 X 才幸福"的念头，你就主动给自己签了一张"在得到之前不许快乐"的合同。 真正的自由不是满足所有欲望，而是先审查"这个欲望我到底要不要签"。这不是躺平——他依然在做大事，只是"做更纯粹、更契合、只有我能做的事"。

3. 幸福不是"极乐机器"，人真正要的是惊喜——而掌控欲恰恰扼杀惊喜。

反直觉点在于：如果给你一台能持续输出快感的"极乐机器"，你并不会一直按下去——因为人真正要的是惊喜（surprise），不是恒定的快感。而"想要掌控一切"的本能，恰恰把惊喜杀死了。配合他对幸福的定义——"幸福就是对当下感到没问题（being okay with this moment）"，是一种由内而生、可习得的技能，而非成功的奖品。启示：别用'掌控'去追幸福；幸福是放下掌控、与当下和解的副产品。

4. 在 AI 时代，"会背"彻底贬值，"会推导"才升值。

"如果你不得不背它，就是你没懂它。"——当 ChatGPT 背下了整个互联网，任何能被检索、被记诵、被生成的东西都不再是你的优势；只有理解、判断、品味，以及"从第一性原理现场重推"的能力，才稀缺。这对教育、职业、个人学习全适用：别再比'知道得多'，要比'理解得深到能重新生成'。

5. 注意力是唯一的货币——你把它花在哪，就是把命花在哪。

钱买不到时间（巴菲特也不行），时间本身也会因"不在场"而浪费，所以人生真正、且唯一的货币是注意力。每一次刷新闻、刷比较、被过去反刍俘获，都是在花这枚唯一的硬币。启示：管理时间不如管理注意力；问题不是"我有没有时间",而是"我此刻真的在场吗"。

6. 把问题放回它被提出的层级——很多"无解的纠结"会自行化解。

"我有自由意志吗""人生有意义吗"——这类纠结之所以无解，是因为你用个体的身份提问、却用上帝/宇宙的视角作答，偷换了层级。把答案拉回提问的那一层，悖论就散了。这是一把能用在很多地方的元工具：当你为某事反复拧巴时，先问一句——"我是不是在用一个尺度提问、却在另一个尺度上找答案？"

7. "阿尔法男最后吃"——能力越大、照顾的部落越大，社会才用'地位'回报你。

他把被滥用的"alpha male"翻了个面："阿尔法男不是抢先吃饭的那个，而是让所有人先吃、自己最后吃的那个。"逻辑是：资源越多，你该照顾的部落越大（自己→家庭→大家族→同胞→世界），而社会回报你的方式，正是那个你用别的办法换不来的东西——地位。这反过来给"地位"找了个正当出口：地位不该去抢，而该作为"你照顾了多少人"的副产品被授予。 他也顺势点出：打击行善的富人（捐了钱还要把名字摘掉）是在帮倒忙——"想改变一个人的行为，要在他做对时强化，而不是在他做错时斥责。"

尾声 · 一句话留给读者

整场对谈的句号，最"纳瓦尔"：Chris 问他"接下来大家能从你这儿期待什么"，他答——"Expect nothing.（别期待什么。）" 顿了顿又补一句："But I do hope to deliver something.（不过我确实希望能拿出点东西来。）"

这两句几乎是他整个人生算法的缩影：先把'期待/欲望'降到零，再在无所求的状态里，认真做出点东西。 不是因为你必须赢，而是因为你本就想做。

如果你也站在"人生这场游戏"里、被各种排名和欲望推着跑，他给的答案其实就藏在开场那个住在木桶里的人身上——

先看清你在玩哪一场游戏，再决定要不要赢；很多时候，"不想要"就已经是赢了。

完整对话 · 全长 3 小时 16 分 · 中英对照

幸福与成功的两条路

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Happiness is being satisfied with what you have. Success comes from dissatisfaction.
幸福，是满足于你已拥有的；成功，则源于不满足。
Chris（克里斯）：
Is success worth it then?
那成功值得吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Oof. I'm not sure that statement is true anymore. Like, I made that statement a long time ago. And a lot of these things are just notes to myself and they're highly contextual. They come in the moment, they leave in the moment. Happiness. Okay. So, very complicated topic, but I always like the Socrates story where he goes into the marketplace and they show him all these luxuries and fineries and he says, "How many things there are in this world that I do not want." Right? And that's a form of freedom. So not wanting something is as good as having it. In the old story with Alexander and Diogenes, right? Alexander goes out and conquers the world and he meets Diogenes, who's living in a barrel, and Diogenes says, "Get out of the way, you're blocking my sun." And Alexander says, "Oh, how I wish I could be like Diogenes in the next life." And Diogenes says, "That's the difference. I don't wish to be Alexander." So, two paths to happiness and one path to success. You get what you want, you satisfy your material needs, or like Diogenes, you just don't want it in the first place. And I'm not sure which one is more valid. And it also depends what you define as success.
哎。我现在不确定那句话还成不成立了。那是我很久以前说的。我那些话很多只是写给自己的笔记，高度依赖语境——它们诞生于某个瞬间，也消失于那个瞬间。幸福。好。这是个很复杂的话题，不过我一直很喜欢苏格拉底（Socrates）的那个故事：他走进集市，人们向他展示各种奢华精美的物件，他说："这世上竟有这么多我不需要的东西。"对吧？这就是一种自由。所以，不想要某样东西，和拥有它一样好。还有亚历山大（Alexander）和第欧根尼（Diogenes）的老故事——亚历山大出征征服世界，遇见住在一个木桶里的第欧根尼，第欧根尼说："让开，你挡住我的阳光了。"亚历山大说："啊，但愿我下辈子能像第欧根尼一样。"第欧根尼说："这就是区别。我可不愿做亚历山大。"所以，通往幸福有两条路，通往成功只有一条。你或是得到你想要的、满足物质需求，或是像第欧根尼那样，压根就不想要。我不确定哪一种更说得通。而且这还取决于你把成功定义成什么。
Chris（克里斯）：
If the end goal is happiness, then why not cut to the chase and just go straight for it? Does being happy make you less successful?
如果终极目标是幸福，那为什么不直奔主题，直接去追幸福？快乐会让人变得不那么成功吗？

快乐反而能更成功

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That is a conventional wisdom. That may even be the practical earned experience of your reality. You find that when you're happy, you don't want anything, so you don't get up and do anything. On the other hand, you still got to do something. You're an animal. You're here. You're here to survive. You're here to replicate. You're driven. You're motivated. You're going to do something. You're not just going to sit there all day. Unlikely. Some people do. Maybe it's in their nature. But I think most people still want to act. They want to live in the arena. I found, for myself, as I've become happier—happier is a big word, but you know, more peaceful, more calm, more present, more satisfied with what I have—I still want to do [00:02] things. I just want to do bigger things. I want to do things that are more pure, more aligned with what I think needs to be done and what I can uniquely do. So in that sense, I think that being happier can actually make you more successful, but your definition of success will likely change along the way.
那是一种传统观念。它甚至可能就是你在现实里亲身验证的经验：你会发现，当你快乐时，你什么都不想要，于是你不会起身去做任何事。但另一方面，你还是得做点什么。你是动物，你存在于此，你要生存、要繁衍，你被驱动着、被激励着，你总会去做点什么，不会一整天就那么坐着——不太可能。有些人确实那样，也许那是他们的天性。但我想大多数人还是想行动，想活在竞技场（arena）里。就我自己而言，随着我变得更快乐——"快乐"这词太大了，不过你懂的，更平和、更宁静、更活在当下、更满足于现有的一切——我[00:02]依然想做事。我只是想做更大的事，想做更纯粹、更契合我认为该做的、以及只有我能独一无二完成的事。从这个意义上说，我觉得更快乐反而能让你更成功，只不过一路走来，你对成功的定义很可能会改变。
Chris（克里斯）：
Is that a realization you think you could have gotten to had you not had some success in the first place?
你觉得，如果当初没有先取得一些成功，你能领悟到这一点吗？

先走物质成功这条路

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
At least for me, I always wanted to take the path of material success first. I was not going to go be an ascetic and sit there and renounce everything. That just seems too unrealistic and too painful. In the story of Buddha, he starts out as a prince, and then he sees that it's all kind of meaningless because you're still going to get old and die, and then he goes into the woods looking for something more. I'll take the happy route that involves material success. Thank you. I think it's quicker in some ways.
至少对我来说，我一直想先走物质成功这条路。我不会去当苦行僧，坐在那儿把一切都舍弃掉——那太不现实，也太痛苦了。佛陀（Buddha）的故事里，他起初是个王子，后来看清这一切某种程度上都没有意义，因为你终究会变老、会死去，于是他走进森林去寻求更深的东西。我宁可选那条包含物质成功的快乐之路。谢了。我觉得那条路在某些方面更快。
Chris（克里斯）：
One of your insights is, it's far easier to achieve our material desires than it is to renounce them.
你有个洞见是：满足我们的物质欲望，远比舍弃它们要容易得多。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It depends on the person, but I think you have to try that path. If you want something, go get it. I quipped that the reason to win the game is to be free of it. So you play the games, you win the games, and then hopefully you get bored of the games—you don't want to just keep looping on the same game over and over—although a lot of these games are very enticing and have many levels and are relatively open-ended. And then you become free of the game in a sense that you're no longer trying to win it. You know you can win it, and either you move to a different game or you play the game for the sheer joy of it.
这因人而异，但我觉得你得去试那条路。如果你想要什么，就去把它拿到手。我有句俏皮话：赢得这场游戏的意义，在于从它当中解脱出来。所以你去玩这些游戏，赢下它们，然后但愿你会对它们感到厌倦——你不会想在同一个游戏里一遍遍循环——尽管很多游戏确实极具诱惑、关卡众多、相对开放。再然后，你就从游戏中解脱了，意思是你不再执着于赢它。你知道自己能赢，于是你要么换去玩别的游戏，要么纯粹为了乐趣而玩。

别成为"受苦上瘾者"

Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah. Another one of yours: most of the gains in life come from suffering in the short term so you can get paid in the long term. I think that's classic winning the marshmallow test on a daily basis. But there's an interesting challenge where I think people need to avoid becoming a suffering addict, sort of using suffering as the proxy for progress, as opposed to the outcome of the suffering, right? It's like, "I was in pain not eating the marshmallow. I was [00:04] in pain doing this work." I have attached well-being and satisfaction to pain, not to what the pain gets me on the other side of it.
对。你还有句话：人生大部分收获，都来自短期的受苦，好让你长期获得回报。我觉得那是典型的"天天通过棉花糖测试"。但这里有个有意思的难题——我觉得人得避免成为"受苦上瘾者"，也就是把受苦本身当成进步的代名词，而不是看重受苦的结果，对吧？就像："我忍着不吃棉花糖很痛苦，我[00:04]做这份工作很痛苦。"我把幸福感和满足感系在了痛苦本身上，而不是系在痛苦能在另一头换来的东西上。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
If you define pain as physical pain, then it's a real thing. It happens and you can't ignore it. But that's not what we mean by suffering. Suffering is mostly mental anguish and mental pain. And it just means you don't want to do the task at hand. If you were fine doing the task at hand, then you wouldn't be suffering. And then the question is, what's more effective: to suffer along the way, or just to interpret it in a way that it's not suffering? You hear from a lot of successful people, they look back and they say, "Oh, the journey was the fun part." Right? That was actually the entertaining part, and I should have enjoyed it more. It's a common regret.
如果你把痛苦定义为生理上的疼痛，那它是真实存在的，确实会发生，你无法忽视它。但那不是我们说的"受苦"。受苦主要是精神上的煎熬和痛苦，它无非意味着你不想做手头这件事。如果你做这件事时心安理得，你就不会受苦。那么问题就是：哪种更有效——是一路受苦，还是干脆换个角度把它理解成不是受苦？你常听很多成功人士回头说："哦，那段旅程才是有意思的部分。"对吧？那其实才是最享受的部分，我当初真该多享受一些。这是一种常见的遗憾。

回到过去的思想练习

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
There's a little thought exercise I like to do, which is, you can go back into your own life and try to put yourself in the exact position you were in five years ago, ten years ago, fifteen years ago, twenty years ago. And you try to remember: okay, who was I with? What was I doing? What was I feeling? What were my emotions? What were my objectives? And really, really try to transport yourself back and see if there's any advice you'd give yourself, anything you'd do differently. Now, you don't have new information—don't pretend you could have gone back and bought a stock or bought Bitcoin or whatever. But just knowing what you know now in terms of your temperament and a little bit of age-related experience, how would you have done things differently? And I think it's a worthwhile exercise to do. So don't let me rob you of the conclusion. But I'll tell you, for me, I would have done everything the same, except I would have done it with less anger, less emotion, less internal suffering. Because that was optional. It wasn't necessary. And I would argue that someone who can do the job at least peacefully, but maybe happily, is going to be more effective than someone who has unnecessary emotional turmoil.
我喜欢做一个小小的思想练习：你可以回到自己的人生里，努力把自己放回五年前、十年前、十五年前、二十年前你所处的那个确切位置。然后试着回忆：好，当时我和谁在一起？我在做什么？我有什么感受？我的情绪是什么？我的目标是什么？真的、真的努力把自己送回那一刻，看看你有没有什么建议想给当时的自己，有没有什么你会换种做法的事。注意，你并没有新信息——别假装你能回去买只股票、买点比特币之类的。仅仅是凭你如今对自己性情的了解，以及一点随年龄而来的阅历，你当初会怎样做得不一样？我觉得这个练习很值得做。所以我不想替你把结论说尽。但我告诉你，对我而言，我每件事都会照旧做，只不过我会带着更少的愤怒、更少的情绪、更少的内在受苦去做。因为那部分是可选的，并非必需。而且我认为，一个至少能平静地、甚至或许快乐地把活儿干完的人，会比一个陷在不必要情绪动荡里的人更有成效。
Chris（克里斯）：
Well, you end up with a series of miserable successes, right? The outcome may have been the same, but the entire experience of getting [00:06] there...
那样你最后就落得一连串"痛苦的成功"，对吧？结果也许一样，但整个争取到[00:06]它的过程……

旅程才是唯一存在的东西

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And the journey is not only the reward, the journey is the only thing there is. Even success—it's human nature to bank it very, very quickly. Because the normal loop that we run through is: you sit around, you're bored, then you want something. Then when you want something, you decide you're not going to be happy until you get that thing. Then you start your bout of suffering or anticipation while you strive to get that thing. If you get that thing, then you get used to it and then you get bored again. Then a few months later you want something else, and if you don't get it then you're unhappy for a bit and then you get over it, then you want something else. Right? That's the normal cycle. So whether you're happy or unhappy at the end, it tends not to last. Now, I don't want to be glib and say that, oh, there's no point in making money or being successful. There absolutely is. Money solves all your money problems, so it is good to have money. That said, there are those stories—I don't know if you've seen those studies, I don't know how real these are, a lot of these psych studies don't replicate, but it's a fun little study—that shows that people who break their back and people who win the lottery are back to their baseline happiness two years later. Again, I don't know if that's entirely true. I think money can buy you happiness if you earned it, because then along the way you have both pride and confidence in yourself, and you have a sense of accomplishment, and you set out to do something and you were right. So I'll bet that lingers. And then, as I said, money solves your money problems. So I don't want to be too glib about it, but I would say in general, this loop that we run through of desire, dopamine, fulfillment, unfulfillment—you have to enjoy the journey. The journey is all there is, right? Ninety-nine percent of your time is spent on the journey. So what kind of a journey is it if you're not going to enjoy it?
而且旅程不只是回报，旅程是唯一存在的东西。哪怕是成功——人的天性也会非常非常快地把它"入袋为安"。因为我们日常跑的那个循环是这样的：你闲坐着，你无聊，于是你想要某样东西。一旦你想要它，你就认定不得到它你就不会快乐。接着你开始一阵子受苦或期盼，一边奋力去争取它。如果你得到了，你很快就习以为常，然后又开始无聊。几个月后你又想要别的东西，得不到就难受一阵子，然后又释怀，接着又想要别的。对吧？这就是常规的循环。所以无论你结局是快乐还是不快乐，那种状态往往都不持久。当然，我不想轻飘飘地说，哦，赚钱或成功没有意义——绝对是有意义的。钱能解决你所有跟钱有关的问题，所以有钱是好事。话虽如此，有那么些故事——我不知道你有没有看过那些研究，我也不知道它们有多可靠，很多心理学研究都重复不出来，不过这是个有意思的小研究——它显示，摔断了脊背的人和中了彩票的人，两年后都会回到各自的幸福基线。再说一次，我不确定这是不是完全属实。我认为，如果钱是你自己挣来的，它就能买到幸福，因为一路走来你既有自豪也有自信，还有成就感——你立志去做某件事，而你做对了。所以我敢打赌那份感觉会留存。再加上，正如我说的，钱能解决你跟钱有关的问题。所以我不想把这事说得太轻巧，但总体来说，我们跑的这个"欲望—多巴胺—满足—不满足"的循环——你必须去享受旅程。旅程就是全部，对吧？你百分之九十九的时间都花在旅程上。那如果你不打算享受它，这又算是怎样一段旅程呢？

如何精简欲望

Chris（克里斯）：
How do you shortcut that desire contract?
你怎么去抄近路、缩短那段"欲望的契约期"？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
You could focus. You could decide that I don't want most things. I think we have a lot of unnecessary desires that we just pick up everywhere. We have opinions on everything, judgments on everything. So I think just knowing that those are the source of unhappiness will make you be choosy about your [00:08] desires. And frankly, if you want to be successful, you have to be choosy about your desires. You have to focus. You can't be great at everything. You can't be great at everything—you're just going to waste your energy and waste your time.
你可以专注。你可以决定：大多数东西我都不要。我觉得我们有太多不必要的欲望，是我们到处随手捡来的。我们对什么都有看法、对什么都有评判。所以我想，仅仅意识到这些就是不快乐的根源，就会让你对自己的[00:08]欲望挑剔起来。坦白说，如果你想成功，你就必须对欲望挑剔，你必须专注。你不可能样样都出色。你不可能样样都出色——那只会白白浪费你的精力和时间。

名声值不值得追求

Chris（克里斯）：
Is fame a worthwhile goal?
名声是一个值得追求的目标吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It gets you invited to better parties. It gets you to better restaurants. Fame... so fame is this funny thing where a lot of people know you, but you don't know them. And it does get you put on the pedestal. It can get you what you want at a distance. So I wouldn't say it's worthless—obviously people want it for a reason. It's high status, so it attracts the opposite sex, especially for men it attracts women. That said, it is high cost. It means you have no privacy. You do have weirdos and lunatics. You do get hit up a lot for weird things. And you're on a stage, so you're forced to perform, so you're forced to be consistent with your past proclamations and actions. And you're going to have haters and all that nonsense. But the fact that we do it, the fact that we all seem to want it, means that it would be disingenuous to say, "Oh no, I'm famous but you don't want to be famous." That said, I think fame, like anything else, is best produced—or pursued—as a byproduct of something potentially more worthwhile.
它能让你被邀请去更好的派对，能让你进更好的餐厅。名声……名声是这么个有趣的东西——很多人认识你，但你不认识他们。它确实会把你捧上神坛，能让你远距离就得到你想要的东西。所以我不会说它一文不值——人们想要它显然是有原因的。它是高地位，所以会吸引异性，尤其对男人来说会吸引女人。话虽如此，它代价高昂。它意味着你毫无隐私，你会招来怪人和疯子，你会因为各种古怪的事被人缠上。而且你站在台上，于是你被迫表演，被迫与自己过去的宣言和行为保持一致。你还会有黑粉之类的破事。但我们既然在追求它、我们似乎都想要它，那么说"哦不，我虽然出名，但你可别想出名"就太虚伪了。不过，我认为名声跟任何东西一样，最好是作为某种可能更有价值之事的副产品而产生——或被追求。
Chris（克里斯）：
Wanting to be famous, and craving to be famous, and being famous for being famous—these are sort of traps. Fame for fame's sake.
想出名、渴望出名、为出名而出名——这些都算是陷阱。为名声而追名声。

赢得的名声 vs 空洞的名声

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, exactly. So it's better that it's earned fame. So for example, earned respect in the tribe is, you do things that are good for the tribe. Who are the most famous people in human history? There are the people who sort of transcended the self—the Buddhas and the Jesuses and the Mohammeds of the world. Who else is famous? The artists are famous; art lasts for a long time. The scientists are famous; they discover things. The conquerors are famous, presumably because they conquered for their tribe—there was someone that they were fighting for. So generally, the higher up you rise by doing things for greater and greater groups of people— [00:10] even though it may be considered tyrannical or negative, like, you know, Genghis Khan is famous, but to the Mongols he was doing good, to the rest of them not so much—the higher level you're operating at, the more people you're taking care of, the more you sort of earn respect and fame. And I think those are good reasons to be famous. If fame is empty, if you're famous just because your name showed up in a lot of places, or your face showed up in a lot of places, then that's a hollow fame. And I think deep down you will know that. And so it'll be fragile, and you'll always be afraid of losing it, and then you'll be forced to perform. So the kind of fame that pure actors and celebrities have, I wouldn't want. But the kind of fame that's earned because you did something useful—why dodge that? No, you can't.
对，没错。所以名声最好是赢得来的。比如说，在部落里赢得尊重，靠的是你做对部落有益的事。人类历史上最出名的人是谁？有那些某种程度上超越了自我的人——世上的佛陀、耶稣、穆罕默德们。还有谁出名？艺术家出名，因为艺术能流传很久。科学家出名，因为他们有所发现。征服者出名，大概因为他们是为自己的部落而征服——他们是有所守护、有所效力的对象的。所以总体来说，你越是通过为越来越庞大的群体做事而上升——[00:10]哪怕这可能被视为暴虐或负面，比如，你知道，成吉思汗（Genghis Khan）很有名，对蒙古人来说他在行善，对其他人就未必了——你所在的层级越高、你照顾的人越多，你就越能赢得尊重和名声。我觉得这些才是值得出名的好理由。如果名声是空的，如果你出名只是因为你的名字到处出现、你的脸到处出现，那就是空洞的名声。我想你内心深处会清楚这一点。所以它会很脆弱，你会一直害怕失去它，然后你就被迫去表演。所以纯粹的演员和明星那种名声，我不想要。但因为你做了有用的事而赢得的那种名声——何必去躲呢？不，你躲不掉。

更新观点像极了伪善

Chris（克里斯）：
There's a challenge, I think, especially if people make very loud public proclamations about things. You mentioned there about you're almost a hostage to the things that you used to say—that being able to update your opinions and change your mind looks very similar, to the internet, as hypocrisy does. You know, the difference between me saying something in the past and saying something different now is, perhaps I've learned, perhaps I've updated my beliefs. But so few people do it in a legitimate way. I think the grifter, the shill—"you see, this is the smoking gun that shows that he didn't really believe that thing all along." And, yeah, I went to a retreat in LA a couple of years ago, and there was a guy that I used to follow—a big business and productivity advice content creator, really, really successful—and he just totally stepped back from everything, went, like, monk mode and focused on his business. And I asked him why. And he said, "I started feeling like I had to live up to in private the things that I was saying in public." Right? Yeah. It's a—what was it, who said it, was it Mencken, that "foolish consistency is the hobgoblin of little minds"? Right?
我觉得这里有个难题，尤其是当人们对某些事做出非常高调的公开宣言时。你刚才提到，你几乎成了自己过去言论的人质——在互联网看来，能够更新看法、改变主意，跟伪善长得极其相似。你知道，我过去说一套、现在说另一套，区别可能在于我学到了新东西、可能在于我更新了信念。可是真正以正当方式做到这点的人太少了。我觉得那种骗子、托儿——"你瞧，这就是铁证，说明他一直就没真信过那套。"对，几年前我去洛杉矶（LA）参加一个静修营，那里有个我以前关注的人——一个做商业和效率建议的大内容创作者，非常非常成功——结果他彻底退出了一切，进入了类似"僧侣模式"，专心做自己的生意。我问他为什么。他说："我开始觉得，我得在私下里活出我在公开场合说的那些话。"对吧？嗯。这是——那句话是谁说的来着，是门肯（Mencken）说的吗，"愚蠢的一致性是小心灵的妖怪"？对吧？

一切学习都是纠错

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
But essentially, look, all life is, all learning is, error correction. Right? [00:12] Every knowledge creation system works through correcting errors—making guesses and correcting errors. So by definition, if you're learning, you're going to be wrong most of the time, and you'll be updating your priors. And so, for example, I did this Joe Rogan podcast—I don't know, it's like eight or nine years ago—and people will call out, like, the one thing that didn't turn out to be correct, right? And it's just like, and they just beat on it, because it helps them, in their mind, raise their status a little bit. "Aha, I caught him in an error." Well, I think if you catch someone in a blatant lie, where they believed one thing and they say another, that's legit—that's a character flaw, they shouldn't be lying. But on the other hand, if they just made a guess at something and they got it wrong—and by the way, mostly it's about the AI, AGI thing, and I think I'm still right about that, but it's a different story. People who think we have achieved AGI just failed a Turing test from their side. It's funny how people latch onto single proclamations, but the reality is, all of us are dynamical systems. We're always changing, we're always learning, we're always growing. And hopefully we're correcting errors. What you don't want to be doing is lying in public because you're trying to look good. And I think people can smell that. What this world really lacks right now is authenticity, because everybody wants something. They want to be seen as something, they want to be something that they're not. And so you do catch a lot of people saying things that they don't really believe. And I think people are very sensitive to that.
但本质上，你看，整个生命、所有学习，都是纠错。对吧？[00:12]每一个知识创造系统的运作方式都是纠错——做出猜测，再纠正错误。所以从定义上讲，只要你在学习，你大部分时间都会是错的，你会不断更新你的先验判断（priors）。比如说，我上过乔·罗根（Joe Rogan）的播客——大概是八九年前吧——人们总会揪出那么一件后来被证明不对的事，对吧？然后他们就死咬着不放，因为在他们心里这能稍微抬高自己的地位。"啊哈，我抓到他的错误了。"我觉得，如果你抓到某人公然撒谎——他明明信一套却说另一套——那是站得住脚的，那是人品缺陷，他们不该撒谎。但另一方面，如果他们只是对某事做了个猜测、猜错了——顺便说一句，那大多是关于人工智能、通用人工智能（AGI）的事，我觉得我在那点上仍然是对的，不过那是另一回事了。那些认为我们已经实现AGI的人，恰恰是他们自己没通过图灵测试。人们死抓着只言片语不放，这挺好笑的，但现实是，我们所有人都是动态系统。我们一直在变化，一直在学习，一直在成长，但愿我们也在纠错。你不该做的，是为了让自己显得好看而在公开场合撒谎。我觉得人们能嗅出这一点。这个世界眼下真正缺的是真诚（authenticity），因为人人都想要点什么。他们想被看成某种样子，想成为他们并非的那种人。于是你确实会逮到很多人在说他们并不真信的话。我觉得人们对此非常敏感。
Chris（克里斯）：
Bullshit radars have become hypersensitized to try and work out whether or not this person means the thing that they're saying.
人们的"扯淡雷达"已经变得高度敏感，时刻在判断这个人是不是真心相信他所说的话。

"说错"与"心口不一"的区别

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. I mean, a lot of people are wrong. Most of us are wrong most of the time, especially in any new endeavor.
对。我是说，很多人都会错。我们大多数人大部分时候都是错的，尤其是在任何新的尝试里。
Chris（克里斯）：
Difference between being wrong and disingenuous, though.
不过，"说错"和"心口不一"是有区别的。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Correct.
没错。
Chris（克里斯）：
Purposefully wrong.
故意地错。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Correct. Exactly. So I think that's the big difference. If someone is wrong, no big deal, as long as they have a genuine reason for saying what they're saying or believing what they're believing. But if they are lying to elevate their status, or their appearance, or to live up to some expectation, that's the mistake. And that's a mistake not just for the listener—it's a mistake for themselves, because then you're going to get trapped in a hall of mirrors. [00:14] You yourself are going to be consistent with your past proclamation. So if you're lying to others, you're going to be lying to yourself. You're puppeted by a person that you are not even.
没错，正是如此。所以我觉得这就是最大的区别。如果一个人错了，没什么大不了，只要他说那番话、信那番话是出于真诚的理由。但如果他撒谎是为了抬高自己的地位、形象，或是为了活成某种期待，那才是错误。而这个错误不只伤了听众——它伤的是他自己，因为接下来你会被困在一座"镜厅"里。[00:14]你会被迫与你过去的宣言保持一致。所以如果你对别人撒谎，你最终也会对自己撒谎。你被一个连你自己都不是的"那个人"操纵着。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's right. Yeah. It's like—what was that line? And you're basically trying to impress people who don't care about you. They don't like the real you. And if they saw the real you, they wouldn't care. And the people who would like the real you don't get to see the real you, so they pass you by.
没错。对。这就像——那句话怎么说来着？你基本上是在努力取悦那些根本不在乎你的人。他们不喜欢真实的你。而如果他们看到真实的你，他们也不会在意。至于那些本会喜欢真实的你的人，却没机会看到真实的你，于是他们与你擦肩而过。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Right. You only want the respect of the very, very few people that you respect. Trying to demand respect from the masses is the fool's errand.
对。你只该在意你所尊敬的极少数人对你的尊重。试图向大众索取尊重，是一桩徒劳之举。

地位游戏 vs 财富创造

Chris（克里斯）：
Status games—the allure of accruing, whether it's fame, actual fame, or just the competition, comparison trap—it's always there. There's a real draw of being swayed by social approval. How should people learn to get less distracted by status games in that way?
地位游戏——那种不断累积的诱惑，无论是名声、真正的名声，还是单纯的攀比、比较陷阱——它无处不在。被社会认可所左右，是一种实实在在的拉力。人们该怎样学会少被这类地位游戏分心？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I think it just helps to see that status games don't matter as much as they used to. In old society—let's go back hunter-gatherer times—there was no such thing as wealth. You just had what you could carry. There was no stored wealth, so wealth games didn't really exist. There were no wealth creation games. All that existed was status games. If you were high status, then you got what little was available first. But even back then, you had to earn your status by taking care of the tribe. Now we have wealth creation, where you can actually create a product or a service, you can scale that product or service, and can provide abundance for a lot of people. And that's not zero-sum, that's a positive-sum game. I can be wealthy, you can be wealthy, we can create things together. And clearly, since we are all collectively far, far wealthier than we were in hunter-gatherer times, wealth creation is positive. But status is limited. There's limited status to go around. It's a ranking ladder, it's a hierarchy. And so to rise in status, somebody else has a lower status. Now, you can have multiple kinds of status, so you can expand some kinds of status, but it's not like wealth creation where it can go infinitely, where we can all be living in the stars and moon bases [00:16] or Mars colonies or what have you. So just realize that status games are inherently limited. They're always combative. They always require direct combat, whereas wealth creation games can be just, you're creating products. You don't have to fight anybody else. Yes, in the marketplace your product has to succeed, but that's not quite the same as invective against other people, or being angry with other people, or feeling pushed down or pushed up, or having a beef with somebody. So I would argue that wealth creation games are both more pleasant—they're positive-sum—and they actually have concrete material returns. If you have more money, you can buy more things.
我觉得，认清"地位游戏不再像过去那么要紧"会很有帮助。在旧社会——咱们回到狩猎采集时代——根本没有财富这回事。你拥有的只是你能随身携带的东西。没有储存下来的财富，所以财富游戏其实并不存在，也没有财富创造游戏。当时存在的只有地位游戏。如果你地位高，那么仅有的那一点资源你能优先拿到。但即便那时，你也得靠照顾部落来赢得地位。如今我们有了财富创造：你真的可以创造一个产品或服务，把它规模化，为很多人提供丰裕。而那不是零和的，那是正和（positive-sum）游戏。我可以富有，你可以富有，我们可以一起创造东西。而且很明显，既然我们整体上比狩猎采集时代富裕得太多太多，财富创造就是正向的。但地位是有限的。能分的地位就那么多。它是一架排名的梯子，是一种等级制。所以要在地位上升，就得有别人地位下降。当然，地位可以有很多种，所以某些种类的地位是可以扩张的，但它不像财富创造那样能无限延伸——财富创造能让我们都住到星辰之间、月球基地[00:16]、火星殖民地之类的地方。所以你只要意识到，地位游戏本质上是受限的，它永远是对抗性的，永远需要正面厮杀；而财富创造游戏可以仅仅是你在创造产品，你不必去和任何人打架。是的，在市场上你的产品得成功，但那跟谩骂他人、对他人愤怒、感到被压下去或被顶上来、跟谁结下梁子，是很不一样的。所以我认为，财富创造游戏既更令人愉快——它是正和的——又确实带来实打实的物质回报。你钱多了，就能买更多东西。
Chris（克里斯）：
Show me where you can exchange your status at the bank.
你倒是给我指指看，哪儿能拿你的地位去银行兑现。

有钱之后为何还追地位

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Exactly. Yeah, it's vague and it's fuzzy. Now, you see people get rich—they have money. What do they want? They want status. So they go to Hollywood, start starring in movies. They donate to nonprofits. They go to Cannes or Davos or what have you. And they start trying to trade the money for status. So people always want what they don't have. And we are evolutionarily hardwired for status because, as I said, wealth creation didn't really exist until the agricultural revolution, when you could store grain. And then the industrial revolution took it to another level. And now the information age is taking it to yet another level. There's never been an easier time to make money. Yes, it's still hard. But there's never been an easier time to create wealth, because there's so much leverage out there. There's so much opportunity. You still have to go find it—it's not easy, it's not going to fall in your lap, and you have to learn something and know something and do something interesting. But nevertheless, it's possible for many more people. A few hundred years ago, you were born a serf, you were going to die a serf. There was almost no way out of that. That's changed. And so I would argue that you're better off focusing on wealth games than status games. If you're trying to build up, for example, your following on a social network and get famous and then get rich off of being famous, that's a much harder path than getting rich first [00:18] and then going for your fame afterwards, would be my advice.
正是。对，它是模糊又含混的。如今你看人们发了财、有了钱，他们想要什么？他们想要地位。于是他们跑去好莱坞（Hollywood）开始演电影，他们给非营利机构捐款，他们去戛纳（Cannes）、去达沃斯（Davos）之类的地方。他们开始试图拿钱去换地位。所以人们总是想要自己没有的东西。而我们在进化上被硬编码成追逐地位，因为正如我说的，财富创造在农业革命之前并不真正存在——农业革命让你能储存谷物。随后工业革命把它推上了一个新台阶。而如今信息时代又把它推上了更高一层。赚钱从未像现在这样容易。是的，它仍然很难。但创造财富从未像现在这样容易，因为外头有太多的杠杆（leverage），有太多的机会。你仍然得去把它找出来——这不容易，它不会自己掉进你怀里，你得学点东西、懂点东西、做点有意思的事。但尽管如此，对多得多的人来说，这已经成为可能。几百年前，你生为农奴（serf），就会死为农奴，几乎没有出路。如今这变了。所以我认为，与其专注地位游戏，你不如专注财富游戏。我的建议是：如果你想着，比如，先在社交网络上把粉丝攒起来、出了名，再靠出名去赚钱，那条路要难得多，远不如先把钱赚到手，[00:18]之后再去追求你的名声。

地位的游戏更难"赢完收手"

Chris（克里斯）：
Well, a lot of people do that, as you said. It's funny how people who have achieved such a level of wealth—you don't think, why do you need the status? Given that most people use status to then try and cash in to achieve wealth, if you've achieved fucking money already, if you're post-money or asset-heavy, as it's known, why are you trying to go in the other direction? As you said, because we've got an illustrious history biologically of wanting status. And wealth is kind of novel, it's new. Wealth is something that you have to understand more intellectually.
嗯，正如你说的，很多人就是那么干的。有意思的是，那些已经达到这种财富水平的人——你会想，你还要地位干什么？既然大多数人是用地位来变现、去换取财富，那如果你他妈的已经有钱了，如果你已经"过了缺钱那关"、或者说"资产丰厚"，你又为什么要往反方向去？正如你说的，因为我们在生物学上有一段追逐地位的辉煌历史。而财富算是新鲜玩意儿，是新东西。财富是你得更靠理智去理解的东西。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, there's a physical component—more food, more survival. But to truly understand the effects and the powers and the abilities and limitations and the advantages and disadvantages of wealth, you have to use your neocortex a lot more.
对，财富有一个生理层面的成分——更多食物、更高的存活率。但要真正理解财富的种种作用、力量、能力、局限以及利弊，你得大量动用你的新皮层（neocortex）。
Chris（克里斯）：
Does that mean the reason to play the game is to win the game and be done with it—is harder to win and be done with for status than it is for wealth?
这是不是意味着，"玩这场游戏的意义在于赢下它然后收手"——对地位来说，比对财富来说，更难做到赢完就收手？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That's a good observation. I hadn't thought that through, but you're right. Yeah, I think that's right. I think people always want more status, but I think you can be satisfied at a certain level of wealth. Whereas, well, you always have this sort of sense—and this is what leaderboards are, right? This is the iTunes billboard chart.
这观察得好。我之前没把这点想透，但你说得对。对，我觉得是这样。我觉得人总想要更多地位，但我认为人在某个财富水平上是可以满足的。而地位呢，你总会有那种感觉——这正是排行榜的本质，对吧？就像iTunes的公告牌榜单。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's right. And it is zero-sum. And it is, I guess, the Forbes richest people on the planet.
没错。而且它是零和的。它大概就是《福布斯》（Forbes）全球富豪榜那种东西。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, that one's harder to climb the ladder on. But the fact that, for example, iTunes and YouTube can put you in competition against your contemporaries every single day, and make you go up and down, and show you likes and comments and ratings—this is how much you fell, this is how much you—
对，那架梯子更难往上爬。但比如说，iTunes和YouTube能让你每一天都和你的同侪较量，让你时上时下，给你看点赞、评论和评分——这是你跌了多少，这是你——
Chris（克里斯）：
Exactly. They keep you running on that treadmill forever. Jimmy Carr has this cool idea where he says trajectory is more important than position. So if you are number 101 in the world, but last year you were number 200, versus you're number two in the world but last year you were number one—there is this sense of, the deceleration is very, very tangible. And it's like, again, it goes back to evolution. You know, something that is bleeding eventually dies unless you stop the bleeding. So you're hardwired not to lose what you have. And because we evolved in conditions where...
没错。它们让你永远在那台跑步机上跑个不停。吉米·卡尔（Jimmy Carr）有个很妙的观点，他说轨迹比位置更重要。所以如果你是世界第101名，但去年你是第200名，对比之下你是世界第二名、但去年你是第一名——那种感觉里，"在减速"是非常非常真切可感的。而这又一次回到进化上。你知道，一个正在流血的东西，除非你止住血，否则终将死去。所以你被硬编码成不愿失去你已拥有的东西。而因为我们是在那样的环境里进化出来的……

自尊 · 世上最糟的事，是不喜欢自己

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
We're so close to just not surviving. You don't want to give anything up; it's hardwired into us to not give anything up. So you grip tightly.
我们离"活不下去"只差一线。你不愿放弃任何东西——不肯放手是刻进我们基因里的，所以你才会死死抓住不放。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's right.
没错。

（广告插播：8sleep Pod 4 Ultra 睡眠床垫，约 00:20，与访谈内容无关，此处略去逐句翻译。原话大意：把 8sleep 床垫附件像床笠一样加到你的床垫上，可自动给床的两侧降温或升温最高 20 度，内置传感器以 99% 准确度追踪睡眠时长、睡眠阶段、HRV、打鼾和心率，临床证明每晚最多增加一小时总睡眠，有 30 天试睡，可全球配送，用优惠码 modernwisdom 在 8sleep.com/modernwisdom 下单立减 350 美元。）

Chris（克里斯）：
The worst outcome in the world is not having self-esteem. Why?
世上最糟的结果就是没有自尊（self-esteem）。为什么？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, it's a tough one. I look at the people—and I don't want to offend anybody—but I look at the people who don't like themselves, and that's the toughest slot, because they're always wrestling with themselves. And it's hard enough to face the outside world. No one's going to like you more than you like yourself. So if you're struggling with yourself, then the outside world becomes an insurmountable challenge. It's hard to say why people have low self-esteem—it might be genetic, it might just be circumstantial. A lot of times I think it's because they just weren't unconditionally loved as a child, and that sort of seeps in at a deep core level. But self-esteem issues can be the most limiting one. An interesting thought is that, to some extent, self-esteem is a reputation you have with yourself. You're watching yourself at all times. You know what you're doing, and you have your own moral code—everyone has a different moral code.
嗯，这个问题不好答。我观察那些人——我不想冒犯任何人——但我看那些不喜欢自己的人，那是最难的处境，因为他们时刻都在跟自己角力。光是面对外部世界就已经够难了。没有人会比你自己更喜欢你。所以如果你连自己这一关都过不去，外部世界就成了无法逾越的难关。很难说清人为什么会低自尊，可能是遗传的，也可能只是境遇造成的。很多时候我觉得是因为他们小时候没有被无条件地爱过，这种缺失会渗透到内心最深的底层。但自尊问题可能是最大的桎梏。一个有意思的看法是：某种程度上，自尊就是你在自己心里积累的"声誉"。你时刻都在注视着自己，你清楚自己在做什么，你有自己的道德准则——每个人的道德准则都不一样。

自尊从何而来 · 活出自己的准则，以及为他人牺牲

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
But if you don't live up to your own moral code—the same code that you hold others to—it will damage your self-esteem. So perhaps one way to build up your self-esteem is to live up to your own code: very rigorously have one, and then live up to it. Another way to raise your self-esteem might be to do things for others. If I look back on my life and ask, what are the moments that I'm actually proud of? They're very far and few between. It's not that often. And it's not the things you would expect. It's not the material success. It's not having learned this thing or that. It's when I made a sacrifice for somebody or something that I loved. And that's when I'm actually, ironically, most proud. Now, that's through an explicit mental exercise, but I'll bet you at some level I'm recording that implicitly. So that tells me that even if I am not being loved, then the way to create love is to give love—to express love through sacrifice and through duty. And so I think doing things like that can build up your self-esteem really fast.
但如果你没能活出自己的道德准则——就是你拿来要求别人的那同一套准则——它就会损害你的自尊。所以也许提升自尊的一个办法，就是活出你自己的准则：严格地立一套准则，然后真正去践行它。另一个提升自尊的办法，也许是为他人做点事。回顾我这一生，要问哪些时刻是我真正引以为傲的？少之又少，并不常有。而且不是你以为的那些事——不是物质上的成功，不是学会了这个那个，而是当我为我所爱的人或事做出牺牲的时候。讽刺的是，那才是我真正最自豪的时刻。这虽然是通过一种有意识的思考得出的，但我敢说，在某个层面上我是在无意识地记录这些的。这告诉我：即使我没有被爱，创造爱的方式也是去给予爱——通过牺牲、通过尽责来表达爱。所以我认为，做这样的事能很快地建立起你的自尊。
Chris（克里斯）：
It's interesting when you talk about sacrifice, because a lot of the time people say, "I sacrifice so much for my job." It's like, yeah, but that was you sacrificing something that you wanted less for something that you wanted more, as opposed to genuinely taking some sort of cost. And yeah, I wonder whether self-esteem is you adhering to your internal—your actions and your values aligning—even when it's difficult, or perhaps even more so when it's difficult. I wonder whether there is a price that people who are more introspective, high-integrity, pay, because you've got this heavier set of overheads that you need to pay in some way.
你谈到牺牲很有意思，因为很多时候人们会说"我为工作牺牲了那么多"。但其实那是你拿你不太想要的东西，去换你更想要的东西，而不是真正付出了某种代价。我在想，自尊会不会就是你忠于内心——让你的行为和价值观保持一致——即便在艰难时刻也是如此，甚至越是艰难越是如此。我也在想，那些更内省、更正直的人是不是要付出某种代价，因为你有一套更沉重的"开销"要以某种方式去偿付。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Well, if being ethical were profitable, everybody would do it, right? So at some level, it does involve a sacrifice. But that sacrifice can also be thought of as you're thinking for the long term rather than the short term. For example, the virtues are a set of beliefs that, if everybody in society followed them as individuals, it would lead to win-win outcomes for everybody. So if I am honest...
嗯，如果有道德是有利可图的，那人人都会去做了，对吧？所以在某个层面上，它确实涉及牺牲。但那种牺牲也可以理解为：你是在为长期着想，而不是为短期着想。比如说，那些美德其实是一套信念——如果社会里每个人作为个体都遵循它们，就会带来对所有人都有利的双赢结果。所以如果我诚实……

博弈论与美德 · 高信任社会、猎鹿博弈与长期的自利

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...and you are honest, then we can do business more easily, we can interact more easily, because we can trust each other. So even though there might be a few liars in the system, as long as there aren't too many liars and too many cheaters, a high-trust society where everybody's honest is better off. And I think a lot of the virtues work this way. If I don't go around sleeping with your wife and you don't sleep with mine, and if I don't take all the food that's at the table first, and so on, then we all get along better and we can play win-win games. In game theory, the most famous game is the Prisoner's Dilemma, but that's all about everybody cheating—the Nash equilibrium, the stable equilibrium there, is everybody cheats. And the only way you can play a win-win game is if you have long-term iterated moves. But that's not actually the most common game played in society. The most common game played is one called a stag hunt, where if we cooperate, we can bring down a big stag and both have big dinners. But if we don't cooperate, then we have to go hunt like rabbits and we each have small dinners. So that game has two stable equilibriums: one could be where we're both hunting the rabbit, and one could be where we're hunting the stag. So the high-trust society is a more virtuous society, where I can trust you to come hunt the stag with me and show up on time and do the work and divide it up properly. So you want to live in a system where everybody has their own set of virtues and follows them, and then we all win. But I would argue you don't need to do that for sacrifice. You don't need to do that for other people. You can do it just purely for yourself. You will have higher self-esteem. You will attract other high-virtue people.
……而你也诚实，那我们做生意就更容易，互动也更容易，因为我们能彼此信任。所以即使系统里有几个骗子，只要骗子和作弊者不太多，一个人人诚实的高信任社会就会过得更好。我认为很多美德都是这样运作的。如果我不到处去睡你老婆，你也不睡我老婆；如果我不抢先把桌上的食物全拿走，诸如此类——那我们大家就相处得更好，就能玩双赢的博弈。在博弈论里，最有名的博弈是囚徒困境（Prisoner's Dilemma），但那讲的全是人人作弊——那里的纳什均衡（Nash equilibrium）、那个稳定均衡，就是人人都作弊。而你唯一能玩成双赢博弈的办法，是有长期、反复进行的博弈。但其实那并不是社会里最常见的博弈。最常见的博弈叫"猎鹿博弈"（stag hunt）：如果我们合作，就能猎到一头大鹿，两人都能饱餐一顿；但如果我们不合作，就只能各自去猎兔子，每人只得一顿小餐。所以这个博弈有两个稳定均衡：一个是我们都在猎兔子，另一个是我们在猎鹿。高信任社会就是更有美德的社会——我能信任你和我一起去猎鹿、准时出现、干好活、并公平分配。所以你想生活在这样一个系统里：人人都有自己的一套美德并去遵循，于是大家都赢。但我得说，你不必为了牺牲才这么做，也不必为了别人才这么做。你完全可以纯粹为了自己这么做。你会拥有更高的自尊，你会吸引来其他高美德的人。
Chris（克里斯）：
Would I go on a stag hunt with me?
（也就是问自己）我会愿意和"我自己"这样的人一起去猎鹿吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Correct. Yeah, that's right. If you're the kind of person who long-term signals ethics and virtues, then you will attract other people who are ethical and virtuous. Whereas if you are a shark, you will eventually find yourself swimming entirely amongst sharks. And that's an unpleasant existence. But again, this goes back to the equivalent of the marshmallow test. And by the way, the marshmallow test does not replicate—I saw it got hit by the replication crisis hard recently. But it is about trading off the short term for the long term.
对，正是如此。如果你是那种长期释放出道德与美德信号的人，你就会吸引来其他有道德、有美德的人。反之，如果你是一条鲨鱼，你最终会发现自己完全游在一群鲨鱼当中，而那是一种很不愉快的生存状态。但这又回到了类似"棉花糖实验"（marshmallow test）的道理。顺便说一句，棉花糖实验是无法被复现的——我最近看到它在复现危机（replication crisis）中被狠狠打脸了。但它讲的就是用短期去换取长期。

自我怀疑与无条件的爱 · 你不知道自己知道的，远多于你知道的

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And so I think for a lot of these so-called virtues, there are long-term selfish reasons to be virtuous.
所以我认为，对于很多这些所谓的美德，行善其实有着长期的自利理由。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah. Did you deal with self-doubt in the past? Is that something that was a hurdle for you to overcome?
是啊。你过去经历过自我怀疑吗？那对你来说是需要克服的一道坎吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yes and no. I think I dealt with self-doubt in the sense that, "Oh, I don't know what I'm doing and I need to figure it out." But I didn't doubt myself in the way of "somebody else knows better than me for me," or "I'm an idiot," or "I'm not worthwhile," or anything. I guess I had the benefit that I grew up with a lot of love—the people around me loved me unconditionally. And so that just gave me a lot of confidence. Not the kind of confidence that would say "I have the answer," but the kind of confidence that "I will figure it out, and I know what I want," or "only I am a good arbiter of what I want."
既有也没有。我经历过的那种自我怀疑是"哦，我不知道自己在做什么，我得把它搞清楚"。但我从没用"别人比我更懂什么对我好"、"我是个白痴"、"我不值得"这种方式去怀疑自己。我想我占了个便宜：我是在很多爱中长大的——身边的人无条件地爱我。这就给了我很多自信。不是那种"我手握答案"的自信，而是那种"我会把它搞清楚，而且我知道我想要什么"、"只有我才是判断我想要什么的好裁判"的自信。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, that level of self-belief, I suppose, allows you to determine what is it that matters to me, my self-esteem—should I chase this thing or not? I can make a fair judgment on that, as opposed to being so swayed. But it's such a good point: even if you think you're not consciously logging the stuff that you're doing, there is some that's in the back of your mind. Was it the daemon—is that what the ancient Greeks used to talk about?
是啊，我想，那种程度的自我信念让你能判定什么对我重要、什么关乎我的自尊——这件事我该不该去追？我可以对此做出公允的判断，而不是被轻易左右。不过你说的这点很好：即便你以为自己没有在有意识地记录所做的事，其实有些东西就存在你脑海深处。那是不是"daemon（精灵/守护灵）"？古希腊人以前说的是不是这个？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. Also, in computer science, there's a concept of a daemon, which is a program that's always running in the background—you can't see it. But yeah, it probably comes from the ancient Greek daemon. But what you know that you don't even know you know is far greater than what you know you know. You can't even articulate most of the things you know. There are feelings you have that have no words for them. There are thoughts you have that are felt within the body or subconsciously that you never articulate to yourself. You can't articulate the rules of grammar, yet you exercise them effortlessly when you speak. So I would argue that your implicit knowledge, and your knowledge that is unknown to yourself, is far greater than the knowledge you can articulate and...
对。另外在计算机科学里也有 daemon（守护进程）这个概念，就是一个一直在后台运行、你看不见的程序。不过它大概确实源自古希腊的 daemon。但你"知道却不知道自己知道"的东西，远远多于你"知道自己知道"的东西。你所知的大多数东西你甚至无法用言语表达。你有些感受是没有词语去形容的；你有些想法是在身体里或潜意识里被感知到的，你从未对自己言明过。你说不出语法规则，可你说话时却毫不费力地在运用它们。所以我得说，你的隐性知识、你那些自己都不知道的知识，远多于你能用言语表达出来的、能……

意识与自尊 · 内在的黄金法则：像别人本应对待你那样对待自己

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...communicate. And so at some level, you're always watching yourself. That's what your consciousness is, right? It's the thing that's watching everything, including your mind, including your body. So if you want to have high self-esteem, then earn your own self-respect. I had this idea—the internal golden rule. The golden rule says: treat others the way you want to be treated. The internal golden rule says: treat yourself like others should have treated you. And it was a riposte to maybe people that didn't grow up with unconditional love in that way. On the love thing, one of the interesting things about love is you can try to remember the feeling of being loved. So go back to when someone was in love with you, or someone did love you, and really remember that feeling. Really sit with it and try to recreate it within yourself. And then go to the feeling of you loving someone, when you were in love—and I'm not even talking about romantic love necessarily, so be a little careful there. I'm talking more about love for—it sometimes gets complex if you're talking about past romantic love—a sibling or a child or something like that, or a parent. And think about when you felt love towards someone or something. And now, which is better? I would argue that the feeling of being in love is actually more exhilarating than the feeling of being loved. Being loved is a little clawing. It's a little too sweet. You kind of want to push the person away. It's a little embarrassing. And you know that if that person is too much into it, you feel constrained. On the other hand, the feeling of being in love is very expansive. It's very open. It actually makes you a better version of yourself. It makes you want to be a better person. And so you can create love anytime you want. It's just that craving to receive it—that's the problem.
……能传达出来的知识。所以在某个层面上，你总是在注视着自己。这就是你的意识（consciousness），对吧？它就是那个注视着一切的东西，包括你的心智，包括你的身体。所以如果你想拥有高自尊，那就去赢得你对自己的尊重。我有过这么一个想法——"内在的黄金法则"。黄金法则（golden rule）说：以你希望被对待的方式去对待他人。而内在的黄金法则说：像别人本应对待你那样去对待你自己。这是对那些没在那种无条件的爱中长大的人的一种回应。说到爱，关于爱有一件有意思的事：你可以试着去回忆"被爱"的感觉。回到曾有人爱上你、或有人确实爱过你的时候，真切地去回忆那种感觉，静静地与它共处，并试着在自己内心重新唤起它。然后再去体会"你爱着别人"、你陷入爱时的感觉——我说的甚至不一定是浪漫之爱，所以这里要稍微留意一下。我更多指的是那种爱——如果说的是过去的浪漫之爱有时会变得很复杂——比如对手足、对孩子之类，或对父母的爱。想想你对某人或某物心怀爱意的时候。那么现在，哪一种更好？我得说，"爱着别人"的感觉其实比"被人爱"的感觉更令人振奋。被爱有点黏人，有点太腻了，你会有点想把对方推开，有点难为情。而且你知道，如果那人陷得太深，你会感到被束缚。另一方面，"爱着别人"的感觉则非常开阔、非常敞开，它实际上让你成为更好的自己，让你想做一个更好的人。所以你随时都可以创造爱。问题只在于那种渴望"被给予"爱的执念。

骄傲的代价 · 骄傲是学习之敌，让你困在局部最优

Chris（克里斯）：
The most expensive trait is pride. How come?
代价最高的特质是骄傲（pride）。为什么这么说？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Oh, that was a recent one. I tweeted that just because I think pride is the enemy of learning. So when I look at my friends and colleagues, the ones who are still stuck in the past...
哦，那是我最近说的。我之所以发那条推，是因为我认为骄傲是学习之敌。当我看我的朋友和同事，那些还困在过去的……
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...and have grown the least are the ones who were the proudest, because they sort of feel like they already had the answers, and so they don't want to correct themselves publicly. And so this goes back to the fame conversation: you get locked into something you said, it made you famous, you're known for that, and now you want to pivot or change. So pride prevents you from saying "I'm wrong."
……成长最少的，恰恰是那些最骄傲的人，因为他们觉得自己已经握有答案了，所以不愿意当众纠正自己。这又回到了之前关于名声的话题：你被你说过的某句话锁死了，那句话让你出了名，你因此而被人熟知，可现在你想转向、想改变。所以骄傲让你说不出"我错了"。
Chris（克里斯）：
What's pride in this context here?
在这里这个语境下，骄傲指的是什么？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It could be as simple as you're trading stocks and then you don't admit you were wrong, so you hang on to a lousy trade. It could be that you made a decision to marry someone, or move somewhere, or enter a profession, and it doesn't work out, and then you don't admit that you were wrong, so you get stuck in it. It's mostly about getting trapped in local maxima, as opposed to going back down and climbing up the mountain again. And that's why it's an expensive trait—because you continue to need to repay it in one form or another.
它可能简单到：你在炒股，然后你不肯承认自己错了，于是死抱着一笔烂交易不放。也可能是你做了决定要娶某人、搬去某地、进入某个行业，结果不顺，然后你不承认自己错了，于是被困在里面。它主要是关于被困在"局部最优"（local maxima）里，而不是先下山、再重新爬另一座山。这就是为什么它是代价高昂的特质——因为你得以这样那样的形式不断为它还债。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, you're just stuck at a suboptimal point. It's going to cost you money. It's going to cost you success.
是啊，你就卡在一个次优的点上。它会让你赔钱，会让你失去成功。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And time.
还有时间。
Chris（克里斯）：
And time.
还有时间。

愿意从零开始 · 伟大的人总能推倒重来：以马斯克为例

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
The great artists always have this ability to start over—whether it's Paul Simon or Madonna or U2, and I'm dating myself a little bit. But even the great entrepreneurs, they're just always willing to start over. I'm always struck by the Elon Musk story, where he did PayPal as X.com originally—actually it was his financial institution that got merged into PayPal.
伟大的艺术家总有这种能力——能够推倒重来，无论是 Paul Simon、Madonna 还是 U2，我这是有点暴露年龄了。但即便是伟大的企业家也一样，他们就是总愿意从头再来。马斯克（Elon Musk）的故事总让我印象深刻：他最早做 PayPal 时叫 X.com——其实是他那家金融机构后来被并进了 PayPal。
Chris（克里斯）：
It's good that you've got the domain, you know what I mean?
好在他还留着那个域名，你懂我的意思吧？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, exactly. "I'll just park that, I'll hold on." He's consistent—he's been using it for quite a while. And he said something along the lines of, "I made $200 million from the sale of PayPal. I put $100 million into SpaceX, $80 million into Tesla, $20 million into SolarCity, and I had to borrow money for rent." This guy is a perennial risk-taker. He's always willing to start over. He doesn't have any pride about being seen as successful or being seen as a failure. He's willing to put it all on the line.
对，没错。"我就先把它停在那儿、留着。"他很一以贯之——他用这个名字已经用了相当久了。他说过大致这样的话："我卖掉 PayPal 赚了两亿美元，我把一亿投进 SpaceX、八千万投进特斯拉（Tesla）、两千万投进 SolarCity，结果我还得借钱付房租。"这家伙是个永不停歇的冒险者，他总是愿意从头再来。无论被看作成功还是被看作失败，他都毫无包袱。他愿意把一切都押上去。
Chris（克里斯）：
He'll back himself again each time.
他每一次都会再押注自己。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
He'll back himself again each time. But the key thing is he's always willing to start over. Even now, when he's sort of made his new startup as the USA—he's basically trying to fix it like he would fix one of his startups. And I think that is a willingness to look like a fool. And...
他每一次都会再押注自己。但关键在于，他总是愿意从头再来。即便现在，他差不多把"美国"当成了他的新创业项目——他基本上是在像修理自己旗下某家创业公司那样去修理它。我认为那是一种甘愿显得像个傻子的意愿。而且……
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...that is a willingness to start over. And a lot of people just don't have that. They become successful, or they become rich, or they become famous, and that's it—they're stuck. They don't want to go back to zero. And creating anything great requires zero to one. And that means you go back to zero. And that's really painful and hard to do.
……那是一种愿意从头再来的意愿。而很多人就是没有这种意愿。他们一旦成功了、或发财了、或出名了，就到此为止了——他们卡住了。他们不愿回到零点。可创造任何伟大的东西都需要"从零到一"（zero to one），而那意味着你要回到零。那真的很痛苦，也很难做到。

快乐是一种选择 · 别为"该得到它的那个东西"牺牲掉你真正想要的

Chris（克里斯）：
Talking about risk, something I've been thinking about a lot to do with you: any moment when you're not having a good time, when you're not really happy, you're not doing anyone any favors. I think lots of people have become unusually familiar with suffering silently in that sort of way—of not having a high bar for your expectation for quality of life.
说到风险，有件跟你有关的事我想了很久：任何一个你过得不开心、并不真正快乐的时刻，你其实没在帮任何人的忙。我觉得很多人已经异常习惯于那样默默地受苦——对自己的生活质量没有设一个高标准。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, a lot of it is just you're memeing yourself into a bad outcome, because you think that somehow suffering is glorious, or that it makes you a better person. My old quip was: "If you're so smart, why aren't you happy? Why can't you figure that one out?" The reality is you can be smart and happy. There are plenty of people in human history who were smart and happy. And I think it just starts with saying, "Yeah, you know what, I'm going to be happy." There was a guy I met in Thailand a long time ago who used to work for Tony Robbins. He had a great attitude. We were sitting around, and he said, "You know, I realized one day that someone out there had to be the happiest person in the world—that person just has to exist. So why not me? I'll take on that burden. I'll be that guy." And I heard that and was like, "Wow, that's pretty good. That's a good frame." He knew how to reframe things. And so I think a lot of happiness is just a choice, in the sense that first you just identify yourself as: "Actually, I'm going to be a person that's going to be happy. I'm going to figure it out." And you just figure it out along the way. You're not going to lose your other predilections. You're not going to lose your ambition or desire for success. I think a lot of people have this fear that, "Oh, if I'm happy, then I won't want to be successful." No—you'll just want to do things that are more aligned with the happy version of you, and you'll be successful...
是啊，很大程度上这只是你在用"梗"把自己洗脑进一个糟糕的结局，因为你觉得受苦在某种意义上是光荣的、或者它能让你成为更好的人。我以前有句俏皮话："你那么聪明，怎么不快乐呢？这道题你怎么就解不出来？"现实是你可以既聪明又快乐。人类历史上有大把既聪明又快乐的人。我觉得这一切的起点，就是说一句："好吧，你知道吗，我就是要快乐。"很久以前我在泰国遇到一个人，他以前为 Tony Robbins 工作，心态特别好。我们坐在一起聊，他说："你知道吗，我有一天意识到，世界上总得有一个最快乐的人——那个人就是必然存在的。那为什么不能是我呢？我来扛下这个担子，我来当那个人。"我听到这话就想："哇，这真不错，这个框架很好。"他懂得如何重构（reframe）事情。所以我认为快乐很大程度上就是一个选择，意思是：首先你只要把自己定义为"其实，我就要做一个快乐的人，我会把它想明白"。然后你就一路把它想明白。你不会因此失去你别的偏好，不会失去你的野心或对成功的渴望。我觉得很多人有这种恐惧："哦，如果我快乐了，那我就不想成功了。"不会的——你只会更想去做那些和"快乐版的你"更契合的事，而你会成功……
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...at those things. And believe me, the happy version of you is not going to look back at the unhappy version and say, "Oh man, that guy was going to be more successful, I wish I was him." You're actually trying to be successful so you'll be happy. That's the whole point. You've gotten it backwards.
……在那些事情上成功。而且相信我，"快乐版的你"绝不会回头看"不快乐版的你"然后说："唉，那家伙本来会更成功的，真希望我是他。"你之所以努力追求成功，本来就是为了能快乐啊。这才是关键所在。你把因果搞反了。
Chris（克里斯）：
You unlocked one of my trap cards. One of my favorite insights is that we sacrifice the thing we want for the thing that's supposed to get it. So we sacrifice happiness in order to be successful, so that when we're finally sufficiently successful, we can actually be happy. And if you have some sort of simultaneous equation, you just sort of strip "success" off from both sides.
你触发了我的一张陷阱卡。我最喜欢的洞见之一是：我们为了"本该用来得到它的那个东西"，反而牺牲掉了我们真正想要的东西。也就是说，我们牺牲快乐去换取成功，好让自己终于足够成功之后，能真正快乐起来。可如果你把它看成某种联立方程，你大可以把"成功"这一项从等式两边都消掉。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
At least in my own life, I have not found there to be a trade-off. If anything, I have found that the happier I get, the more I am going to do the things that I'm good at and aligned with, and that will make me even happier. And so I actually end up more successful, not less.
至少在我自己的人生里，我没发现这中间存在取舍。如果说有什么，那就是我发现：我越快乐，我就越会去做那些我擅长、与我契合的事，而那又会让我更快乐。于是我反而变得更成功，而不是更不成功。

毫不愧疚地优先自己 · 整体性的"自私"与人之本性

Chris（克里斯）：
The "aligned with" thing is interesting. I'm going to try and put this across as delicately as I can: I would say, from the bit of time that we've spent together, you have a really interesting trait of holistic selfishness. You're sort of prepared to put yourself first. You seem largely unfazed by saying or doing things that might result in other people feeling a little bit awkward, if it's truthful for you. It's like unapologetically self-prioritizing, I guess.
"与你契合"这点很有意思。我尽量说得委婉一些：从我们相处的这点时间来看，我会说你有一个非常有意思的特质——一种"整体性的自私"（holistic selfishness）。你某种程度上是准备好把自己放在第一位的。只要是对你而言真实的，你似乎基本不会因为说或做某些可能让别人有点尴尬的事而动摇。我想，那是一种毫不愧疚地把自己放在优先位置的态度。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, I think everybody is. Maybe "unapologetic" is the part that's relatively rare, but I think everybody puts themselves first. That's just human nature. You're here because you survive. You're a separate organism.
是啊，我觉得每个人都是这样。也许"毫不愧疚"这部分相对少见，但我认为每个人都把自己放在第一位。这就是人之本性。你之所以在这里，是因为你活了下来。你是一个独立的有机体。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's interesting. Maybe, but—
这很有意思。也许吧，但是——
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I know we like to virtue-signal and pretend we're doing it for each other. How many times does somebody say, "Yeah, of course, I'd love to come to the wedding," when they're thinking, "I don't want to be at the fucking wedding"? How many times does someone say, "How are you doing today?" and they don't actually want you to tell them? I don't go to weddings.
我知道我们都喜欢做"美德表演"（virtue-signal），假装我们是在为彼此而做。有多少次有人嘴上说"当然啦，我很乐意去参加婚礼"，心里却想着"我他妈才不想去那场婚礼"？又有多少次有人问"你今天过得怎么样？"，其实根本不想听你真说？我是不去参加婚礼的。
Chris（克里斯）：
But this is my point. So I don't think you're necessarily right with that. I don't think they put themselves first. I sometimes think that they compromise what it is that they want in order to appease socially what's in front of them.
但这正是我要说的。所以我并不认为你这点说得一定对。我不认为他们是把自己放在第一位。我有时候觉得，他们是在妥协掉自己想要的东西，去迎合眼前的社交场面。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, I just view it as everyone's wasting their time on it.
嗯，我只是把它看成——大家都在这上面浪费时间。

人生太短 · 删掉日历、不做不想做的事

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Don't do something you don't want to do. Why are you wasting your time? There's so little time on this earth. Life goes fast. What is it—4,000 weeks? That's your lifespan. And yes, we hear that, but we don't remember it. But I guess I'm keenly aware of how little time I have, so I'm just not going to waste it.
别去做你不想做的事。你干嘛浪费时间？人在这世上的时间太少了。人生过得飞快。是多少来着——四千个星期？那就是你的寿命。是的，这话我们都听过，但我们记不住。可我想，我是敏锐地意识到自己时间有多少的，所以我就是不打算浪费它。
Chris（克里斯）：
How have you got more comfortable at being the unapologetic self-prioritizer?
你是怎么变得越来越坦然地去做那个毫不愧疚地优先自己的人的？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, I've gotten utterly more and more ruthless on it. Mainly, it's that I see or hear of other people's freedom, and then that liberates me further. So I read a blog post by pmarca, a.k.a. Marc Andreessen, where he said, "Don't keep a schedule." And I took that to heart. So I deleted my calendar, and I don't keep a schedule. I try to remember it all in my head. If I can't remember it, I'm not going to add it to my schedule.
是啊，我在这件事上变得越来越彻底地不留情面。主要是因为我看到或听到别人的自由，那又进一步解放了我。比如我读过一篇 pmarca（也就是 Marc Andreessen，马克·安德森）写的博文，他说："别记日程表。"我把这话记在了心上。于是我删掉了我的日历，我不记日程表，我尽量把所有事都记在脑子里。如果我记不住，那我就不会把它加进我的日程里。
Chris（克里斯）：
I'm glad you got here on time.
那我很高兴你准时到了这儿。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, exactly. I had to look things up at the last minute. But ironically, I don't even know if Marc himself follows that—but he made the correct point. I read a little story about Jack Dorsey doing all his business off his iPhone and iPad, and not even going into a Mac. And I said, "Okay, I want to do that. So I'm going to operate through text messaging," and I put up my nasty email.
对，没错。我是临到最后才去查了些信息。但讽刺的是，我都不知道 Marc 本人是不是真照着做——可他说的那个点是对的。我还读过一个小故事，说 Jack Dorsey（杰克·多西）所有业务都靠 iPhone 和 iPad 处理，甚至都不碰 Mac。我就说："好，我也要这么干。所以我要通过短信来运作一切。"然后我挂出了我那封"恶劣"的邮件回复。
Chris（克里斯）：
Does that feel like more freedom?
那感觉更自由吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It does, yeah, because you're on the go. So I have a nasty email autoresponder that says, "I don't check email—and don't text me either. If you need to find me, you'll find me." Obviously, some of this is a luxury of success, but some of these habits I adopted long before, actually. The hostile email autoresponder started a long time ago. I used to own the domain "don't-do-coffee.com"—I let it go. I used to reply from that email just so people would get the point. But I stopped being rude about it. Now I just ghost. I just disappear. My wife knows not to ever book or schedule me for anything. I'm not expected to go to couples' dinners, not expected to go to birthdays, and not expected to go to weddings. If somebody tries to rope her into having me show up, she says, "He makes his own decisions. You've got to ask him directly."
确实更自由，因为你处于"随走随做"的状态。我设了一封很恶劣的邮件自动回复，写着："我不查邮件——也别给我发短信。你要是真需要找我，你会找到我的。"显然这其中有一部分是成功带来的奢侈，但其实有些习惯我很早以前就养成了。那封充满敌意的邮件自动回复很早就开始了。我以前还拥有过一个域名叫"don't-do-coffee.com"（别约咖啡）——我后来放掉了。我以前就用那个邮箱回信，纯粹是为了让人领会我的意思。不过我后来不再用粗鲁的方式了，现在我就直接"已读不回"（ghost），直接消失。我太太知道永远别替我预订或安排任何事。别指望我去参加情侣聚餐，别指望我去参加生日会，也别指望我去参加婚礼。如果有人想拉拢她、让她安排我出现，她就会说："他自己做决定，你得直接去问他。"

扼杀偶遇？ · 清空日历换来的是全然的偶遇

Chris（克里斯）：
What about vice versa—are you not killing serendipity in a way?
那反过来呢——你这样是不是在某种程度上扼杀了偶遇（serendipity）？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
No, no. I'm freeing up all my time, so my entire life is serendipity. I get to interact with whoever I want, whenever I want, wherever. You hear the invite, but make the decision—because if there are fewer things incoming, you're assuming that you know what's best. I don't commit to anything in the future. So I'll say, "Okay, if that thing is interesting, I'll see if I can get in that day, when I'm in the mood." But there's nothing worse than something coming up that your past self committed you to that your present self doesn't want to do. "God damn it, past Naval." And then it destroys your entire calendar. It destroys your day, because there's like this one-hour slot sitting like a turd on my calendar that I have to schedule my whole day around. I can't do anything 20 minutes before, 20 minutes afterwards. Even for phone calls—if someone wants to do a phone call, I say, "Okay, just text me when you're free, I'll text you when I'm free, and we'll just do it on the fly." It's a much better way of living than this overly scheduled cal.com or iCal, whatever. The overscheduled life is not worth living. I think it's a terrible way to live life. That's not how we evolved, it's not how we grew up, it's not how we were as children—hopefully, unless you have a helicopter parent or a tiger mom. Your natural order is freedom. I had a friend who said to me once, "You know, I never want to have to be at a specific place at a specific time," and I was like, "Oh my God, that's freedom." When I heard that, it changed my life.
不，不会。我是在把我所有的时间都腾空，所以我的整个人生就是偶遇。我可以在任何我想要的时候、任何地点，和任何我想交流的人交流。你听到邀约，但你来做决定——因为如果进来的事更少，你就等于假定自己知道什么最好。我对未来不做任何承诺。所以我会说："好，如果那件事有意思，等那天我有心情了，我看看能不能去。"但最糟糕的事莫过于：你"过去的自己"替你承诺下来的事冒了出来，而你"现在的自己"却根本不想做。"该死，过去的纳瓦尔。"然后它就毁掉你整张日历，毁掉你这一整天，因为日历上有这么一个一小时的格子，像一坨屎一样杵在那儿，我整天都得围着它来安排。它之前的 20 分钟、之后的 20 分钟我什么都干不了。哪怕是打电话也一样——如果有人想通电话，我就说："好，你有空时给我发短信，我有空时给你发短信，咱们临时随机就办了。"这比那种被 cal.com 或 iCal 之类排得满满当当的方式，是好得多的活法。过度排程的人生不值得过。我觉得那是一种糟糕透顶的活法。我们演化出来不是这样的，我们长大的方式不是这样的，我们小时候也不是这样的——但愿如此，除非你摊上了直升机父母或虎妈。你的自然状态就是自由。我曾有个朋友有一次对我说："你知道吗，我永远不想被迫在某个特定的时间出现在某个特定的地点。"我当时就想："我的天，那就是自由。"我听到那句话的时候，它改变了我的人生。
Chris（克里斯）：
You still—an alarm clock? Alarm-clockless?
你还用——闹钟吗？还是没有闹钟？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yes, I'm alarm-clockless. Today I did set my alarm just so I wouldn't miss—this is very important. If you—just so you know—I set the alarm clock for 11 a.m., in case I was stricken with the flu. I wasn't going to set my alarm clock for 8 a.m. or 9 a.m. And sure enough, I got up many hours before that. But it was sort of a backup emergency alarm. In fact, sometimes when I have something that I need to do, I don't want to look at a calendar, so I'll just set an alarm for it.
是的，我不用闹钟。今天我倒是设了闹钟，纯粹是为了不错过——这很重要。我跟你说一声，我把闹钟设在了上午 11 点，以防我万一染上流感。我才不会把闹钟设在早上 8 点或 9 点。结果不出所料，我提前好几个小时就起来了。但那只是个备用的应急闹钟。其实有时候我有件必须做的事，又不想去看日历，我就干脆为它设个闹钟。
Chris（克里斯）：
Just sink a little bit more into that—that kind of "fuck you" energy, that self-prioritizing energy—because I think people rationally love the idea of this: "I'm going to do what only I want to do," even if they've got...
再往里多挖一点那种感觉——那种"去你的"（fuck you）的劲头、那种优先自己的劲头——因为我觉得人们在理性上都很向往这种状态："我只做我想做的事"，哪怕他们有……

自由与生产力 · 把自由灌进生活，反而更高产

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...the level of freedom. It's not "fuck you" energy in the sense that I think everyone should live their life that way to the greatest extent possible. Obviously we have our requirements around work and obligations that are genuinely important to us. But don't fritter away your life on randomly scheduled things and things that aren't important, don't matter — events and weddings and, you know, tedious dinners with tedious people that you don't want to go to. To the extent you can bring freedom into your life, optimize for that. You'll actually be more productive. You won't just be happier, more free — you will be more productive. Because then you can focus on what is in front of you, whatever the biggest problem of that day. When I wake up in the morning, the first four hours are when I have the most energy, and that's when I want to solve all the hard problems. And the next four hours are when I kind of want to do some more outdoorsy activities, or I want to work out, or maybe I can have some meetings, but I'll try to do those last second based on whatever the day's priorities demand. The last four hours, I kind of want to wind down, I want to hang out with the kids and play games or read a book or something like that. So having that flexibility and freedom is really important, so you can just put whatever is most needed into the slot at that moment. Instead, if I have like a meeting at 2 p.m. and then I have to get a thing and some emails done, I put that off till 6 p.m. and I'm rushing. I'm not going to be productive.
……那种自由的层次。这并不是说要对所有人摆出"去你的"（fuck you）姿态——我是说每个人都应该尽可能地照这种方式去过自己的人生。当然，我们都有工作上的要求，有那些对我们真正重要的义务。但别把人生浪费在那些随手排进日程、并不重要、根本无所谓的事情上——各种活动、婚礼，还有跟你压根不想见的无趣的人吃那些无趣的饭。在你力所能及的范围里，把自由引进生活，并为此做优化。这样你反而会更高产。你不只是更快乐、更自由——你会更有生产力。因为那样你才能专注于眼前的事，专注于那一天最大的问题。我早上醒来，头四个小时是精力最旺的时候，那时我想解决所有难题。接下来四个小时我想做些户外活动，或者锻炼，又或者可以开点会，但我会尽量临到最后一刻再定，看那天的优先级需要什么。最后四个小时我想放松下来，想陪孩子玩游戏、读本书之类的。所以拥有这种灵活和自由非常重要，这样你就能把当下最需要做的事放进那个时段。反过来，如果我下午两点有个会，然后还得办件事、回几封邮件，我就拖到晚上六点，结果手忙脚乱，根本高产不起来。
Chris（克里斯）：
You're certainly not free.
你肯定也谈不上自由。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I'm definitely not free. But also, another thing that I really believe is that inspiration is perishable — act on it immediately. So when you're inspired to do something, do that thing. If I'm inspired to write a blog post, I want to do it at that moment. If I'm inspired to send a tweet, I want to do it that moment. If I'm inspired to solve a problem, I do it that moment. If I'm inspired to read a book, I want to read it right then. If I want to learn something, I do it at the moment of curiosity. The moment the curiosity arrives, I go learn that thing immediately. I download the book, I get on Google, I get on ChatGPT, whatever. I will figure that thing out on the spot. And that's when the learning happens.
我确实不自由。不过，我还有一个很坚定的信念：灵感是会腐坏的（perishable）——要立刻行动。所以当你受到启发想做某件事时，就去做那件事。我一有灵感想写篇博客，就想当下写；一有灵感想发条推特，就想当下发；一有灵感想解决某个问题，就当下解决；一有灵感想读本书，就想立刻读。我想学点什么，就在好奇心冒出来的那一刻去学。好奇心一来，我马上就去学那个东西——下载那本书，打开谷歌，打开 ChatGPT，随便什么。我会当场把它弄明白。学习就是在那时发生的。

自发与好玩 · 在好玩的时候学得最好，反对效率与幸福对立

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It doesn't happen because I've scheduled time, because I've set an hour aside — because when that time arrives, I might be in a different mood. I might just want to do something different. So I think that spontaneity is really important. You're going to learn best when you're having fun, when you generally are enjoying the process, not when you're forced to sit there and do it. How much do you remember from school? You were forced to learn geography, history, mathematics on this schedule, at this time, according to this person. Didn't happen. All the stuff that sticks with you is what you learned when you wanted to, when you genuinely had the desire. And that freedom, that ability to act on something the moment you want to, is so liberating that most of us go through our lives with very, very little taste of that. If you can live your entire life that way, that is a recipe for happiness.
学习不会因为我排好了时间、留出了一小时就发生——因为等那个时间到了，我可能心情已经变了，可能只想干点别的。所以我觉得自发性（spontaneity）非常重要。你在玩得开心、整体享受过程的时候学得最好，而不是被逼着坐在那里学的时候。你还记得多少学校里的东西？你被逼着按这张课表、在这个时间、按这个人的要求去学地理、历史、数学。没记住。真正留在你脑子里的，都是你想学的时候、真有那份渴望的时候学的。而那种自由、那种想做就立刻能做的能力，是如此令人解放，以至于我们大多数人一辈子都只尝到过极少极少的那种滋味。如果你能用那种方式过完整个人生，那就是通往幸福的配方。
Chris（克里斯）：
It feels like efficiency.
那感觉就像是一种效率。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It's efficient also. You have the inspiration — that is going to be the most frictionless time to ever do that particular task.
它确实也很有效率。当你有了灵感，那就是做那件特定任务时阻力最小（frictionless）的时刻。
Chris（克里斯）：
So, "Oh, I've had the inspiration to do that. I'll put that off until a time when I no longer really want to do it quite so much. And while I do want to do that thing, I'll do something else that I needed to do because it's on the schedule." It does not work.
所以那种"哦，我有了做那件事的灵感，但我要把它拖到一个我已经没那么想做的时候。而趁着我现在还想做那件事，我却去做了另一件因为排进日程而需要做的事"——这是行不通的。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Procrastination is because you don't want to do that thing right now — you want to do something else. Go do that something else. I reject this frame that efficiency and productivity and success are counter to happiness and freedom. They actually go together.
拖延，是因为你此刻不想做那件事——你想做别的。那就去做那件别的事。我拒绝接受这个框架：认为效率、生产力和成功是跟幸福、自由对立的。它们其实是相辅相成的。
Chris（克里斯）：
How so?
怎么讲？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
The happier you are, the more you can sustain doing something; the more likely you're going to do something that will in turn make you even happier, and you'll continue to do it, and you'll outwork everybody else. The more free you are, the better you can allocate your time. And the less you're caught up in a web of obligations and commitments, the more you can focus on the task at hand.
你越快乐，就越能持续地做一件事；你就越有可能去做那件反过来让你更快乐的事，然后你会一直做下去，并且工作量盖过所有人。你越自由，就越能分配好自己的时间。你越不被一张义务和承诺织成的网缠住，就越能专注于手头的任务。

广告插播 · Function 健康检测
