像玩一样工作 · 把自己产品化，靠真实逃离竞争

Chris（克里斯）：
This is related to another insight of yours: the less you want something, the less you're thinking about it, the less you're obsessing over it — the more you're going to do it in a natural way, the more you're going to do it for yourself, you're going to do it in a way that you're good at, and you're going to stick with it. The people around you will see the quality of your work is higher. This seems like a difficult tension to navigate, because an obsessive attention to detail is a competitive advantage of your work as well. So you have these two things sort of conflicting with each other.
这跟你的另一个洞见有关：你越不渴求某样东西、越不去想它、越不执念于它——你就越会以一种自然的方式去做它，越会为自己去做它，会用你擅长的方式去做，而且会坚持下去。你身边的人会看到你的作品质量更高。可这似乎是个很难拿捏的张力，因为对细节的执着同样是你工作中的一项竞争优势。所以这两样东西像是在彼此冲突。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
No one is going to beat you at being you. So one of the things I like to say is: find what feels like play to you but looks like work to others. So it looks like work to them, but to you it feels like play, it's not work. So you're going to outcompete them, because you're doing it effortlessly, you're doing it for fun. They're doing it for work, they're doing it for some byproduct. To you it's art, it's beauty, it's joy, it's flow, it's fulfilling. You must enjoy podcasting. If you didn't, you wouldn't be good at it — you would have lost it, you wouldn't have done 900 episodes either. Right? If you decided that the right way to get ahead in life was to go write books, nobody would have heard of you. Chris Williamson's book would be a complete flop. That's not who you are. You're a podcaster. You enjoy talking to people, you enjoy interviewing them. The more you do things that are natural to you, the less competition you have. You escape competition through authenticity, by being your own self. If I had to summarize how to be successful in life in two words, I would just say: productize yourself. That's it. Just figure out what it is that you naturally do that the world might want, that you can scale up and turn into a product.
没有人能在"当你自己"这件事上打败你。所以我喜欢讲一句话：找到那种对你来说像玩、对别人来说像工作的事。在他们眼里那是工作，但对你来说像在玩，不是工作。所以你会胜过他们，因为你毫不费力地做着它、是为乐趣去做的。他们是为工作、为某种附带产物去做的。而对你来说那是艺术、是美、是喜悦、是心流（flow）、是有成就感的事。你一定很享受做播客。如果你不享受，你就不会做得这么好——你早就放弃了，也不会做满 900 集了，对吧？假如你认定人生出人头地的正确方式是去写书，那就没人会听说过你了。克里斯·威廉森的书会彻底扑街。那不是你这个人。你是个播客主持人，你享受跟人聊天、享受采访他们。你越去做那些对你来说自然而然的事，你面对的竞争就越少。你靠真实、靠做你自己来逃离竞争。如果让我用两个词总结人生如何成功，我就说：把你自己产品化（productize yourself）。就这样。弄清楚那件你天生就会做、世界又可能想要、并且你能放大、变成一个产品的事。

无限机会与早熟承诺 · 用搜索的方式找人找事，默认对一切说不

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And it'll eventually be effortless for you. Yes, there's always work required, but it won't even feel like work to you — it'll feel like play to you. And modern society gives us that opportunity. You know, if you were 2,000 years ago you're born on a farm, your choices are very limited — you're going to do stuff on that farm. Now you can literally wake up and you can move to a different city, you can switch careers, you can switch jobs, you can change the people that you're with. You can change so many things about who you are and who you're with and what you're doing, that there is infinite opportunity out there for you — literally infinite. And so it's much better to treat this like a search function: to find the people who need you the most, to find the work that needs you the most, to find the place you're best suited to be at. And it's worthwhile to spend time in that exploration before diving into exploitation. The biggest mistake in a world with so many choices is premature commitment. If you prematurely commit to being a lawyer or a doctor, and now you've got like five years invested into that, you might have just completely missed — you might just end up in the wrong profession, the wrong place, the wrong people, for 30 years of your life grinding away. And yes, the best time to figure that out was before, but the second best time is now. So just change it.
而且它最终对你来说会毫不费力。是的，总归需要付出努力，但你甚至不会觉得那是工作——你会觉得像在玩。而现代社会给了我们这种机会。你想想，要是两千年前你生在一座农场，你的选择非常有限——你就只能在那座农场上干活。现在你真的可以一觉醒来就搬到另一座城市，可以换行业、换工作、换身边的人。关于你是谁、和谁在一起、在做什么，你能改变的东西多到——外面有无限的机会等着你，字面意义上的无限。所以最好把这件事当成一个搜索功能（search function）：去找那些最需要你的人，去找那些最需要你的工作，去找那个最适合你待的地方。在一头扎进"开采"（exploitation）之前，花时间去"探索"（exploration）是值得的。在一个选择如此之多的世界里，最大的错误就是过早承诺（premature commitment）。如果你过早地承诺去当律师或医生，现在已经在上面投入了五年，那你可能完全错过了——你可能就此落进错的职业、错的地方、错的人群，在错的地方苦熬人生中的 30 年。是的，搞清楚这件事最好的时机本来是从前，但第二好的时机就是现在。所以直接改就行。
Chris（克里斯）：
And also presumably kill things that aren't working very quickly.
而且大概也要很快地砍掉那些行不通的东西。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
By default, you should kill everything. You know, if you can't decide, the answer is no. And most things you should just be saying no to. Part of keeping my calendar free is just, by default, saying no to everything. Do I want to create a calendar just to add your event, right? Or to add your need or your desire? One of the other things — you know, early on in life you're looking for opportunities, so you're saying yes to everything, and that is a phase that you go through, that is the exploration phase. Later, when you've found the thing you want to work on, you're in the exploitation phase, and you have to say no to everything by default. And if you don't say no to everything by default, you have to even explicitly go out of your way to say no to something that will take up time. For example, there are a lot of people out there who are into hustle culture...
默认状态下，你应该砍掉一切。要知道，如果你拿不定主意，那答案就是不。大多数事情你都应该直接说不。我保持日程空闲的方式之一，就是默认对一切说不。难道我要专门弄一个日历，就为了塞进你的活动？或者塞进你的需求、你的渴望？还有一点——你知道，人生早期你在寻找机会，所以你对一切说是，这是你必经的一个阶段，是探索阶段。后来，当你找到了想投入的那件事，你就进入了开采阶段，这时你必须默认对一切说不。如果你不默认对一切说不，你甚至还得专门费劲去对某件会占用时间的事说不。比如说，外面有很多人迷信奋斗文化（hustle culture）……

学会说不与情绪脱敏 · 删邮件不眨眼，把情绪反应剥离

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...and a big piece of hustle culture is like, "Well, you're not going to get something if you don't ask for it." So they'll hustle people, they'll always be sending you requests, messages. Yeah, this is a famous-person problem, but I have it. And people are always asking me for things, and I kind of squirm when I get these messages. And I'm sure you get these too — text messages, emails saying, "Hey, Chris, my friend so-and-so should really be on your podcast," or "You should come to my event," "You should write a foreword for my book." And you kind of squirm when you get this, right? You have to figure out how to say no. And one of the things I learned along the way is that if you wouldn't ask somebody else to do it, and then you get that request yourself, you can just dismiss it. You don't have to respond, you don't even let it enter your brain. You have to be able to delete emails and text messages without flinching if you want to scale. And scaling is very important — scaling your time is really important. Every interruption will take you out of flow. So the only way you can remain in flow is if you get either very good at ignoring these things by default, or closing yourself off like a hermit — like our mutual friend Tim Ferriss does — or you just become emotionally capable of not registering these as something that causes turbulence inside of you.
……奋斗文化里很大一块就是"如果你不开口要，你就什么都得不到"。于是他们到处去缠人、磨人（hustle），不停地给你发请求、发消息。是的，这是名人才有的烦恼，但我有这烦恼。人们总在向我要东西，我一收到这些消息就有点浑身不自在。我相信你也会收到——短信、邮件，写着"嘿克里斯，我朋友某某真该上你的播客"，或者"你该来我的活动"，"你该给我的书写个序"。你一收到就有点别扭，对吧？你得想办法说不。我一路走来学到的一点是：如果这件事你自己都不会开口去求别人做，那当你自己收到这种请求时，你完全可以直接打发掉。你不必回复，甚至别让它进到你脑子里。如果你想规模化（scale），你必须能够眼睛都不眨地删掉邮件和短信。而规模化非常重要——把你的时间规模化非常重要。每一次打断都会把你从心流里拽出来。所以你能保持心流的唯一办法，要么是变得非常擅长默认忽略这些东西，要么是像隐士一样把自己封闭起来——就像我们共同的朋友蒂姆·费里斯（Tim Ferriss）那样——要么就是你在情绪上变得有能力对这些不起反应，不让它们在你内心掀起波澜。
Chris（克里斯）：
That "not registering it emotionally" thing — is that a...?
那个"在情绪上不去登记它"的本事——那是一种……？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It's fundamental. That's so fundamental to so many things in life.
那是根本性的。它对人生中那么多事情都太根本了。
Chris（克里斯）：
Okay, can we dig into that a little bit? Because, again, I've only seen you as you, right? I didn't know you 20 years ago, I didn't know you as a child. So I've only seen you with this holistic selfishness, the integrated self-prioritization — whatever, I don't know what we called it.
好，我们能就此往里挖一点吗？因为，再说一遍，我只见过现在这个你，对吧？二十年前的你我不认识，小时候的你我也不认识。所以我看到的只是带着这种整体性自私（holistic selfishness）、这种一体化的自我优先（integrated self-prioritization）的你——随便吧，我也不知道我们管它叫什么。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Selfish is fine. I'll take selfish. I'm selfish. I'm a very selfish person. Don't contact me.
自私这个词没问题，我接受"自私"。我就是自私。我是个非常自私的人。别联系我。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, that emotional reaction. I also get the sense, too, that maybe people have lived an obligation life for so long that they actually kind of struggle to tap into what it is that they want. They've hidden their wants and their desires and their needs, and they've deprioritized themselves so much for so long, they go, "What do I want, actually? What is it? Do I want to go to this thing or not? Because all I've done is be fucking puppeted, right? I've been marionetted by other people's desires for so, so, so long. I can't even tap into that anymore." And saying no feels like a war crime.
对，就是那种情绪反应。我还有一种感觉：也许人们过义务式的人生过得太久了，以至于他们其实很难触及自己究竟想要什么。他们把自己的想要、渴望和需求都藏了起来，把自己降到末位降了太久太久，以至于他们会想"我到底想要什么？究竟是什么？我到底想不想去这件事？因为我做的全是被人当傀儡操纵，对吧？我被别人的欲望像提线木偶一样牵着，牵了那么久那么久，我再也触及不到那个东西了。"而说"不"感觉像是犯了战争罪。

观察自己的念头 · 用旁观者视角看心智，问题先在脑中成立

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So I think it's really good to be able to view your own mind and your own thoughts objectively. And that is the big benefit of meditation: it creates a small gap between your conscious observation-self and your mind. And that lets you then look at your thoughts and evaluate them a little bit like you would a third party's statements. If you just take your mind to be you, and they're integrated and one and the same at all times, and you're reacting from the mind, then you're not even going to question things that come into your mind. Anything that comes in that creates your reaction will immediately create a reaction. But if you can observe your thoughts a little bit — and not in some... but you can even just do it through therapy, you can do it through journaling, you can do it any way you would like, you can just take long walks. You don't have to meditate and do lotus position, all that is unnecessary. But if you can observe your own thoughts and view them a little objectively, then you can start being a little more choosy, a little more critical. And you can realize that there are no problems in the real world other than maybe things that inflict pain on your body. Everything else has to become a problem in your mind first. You have to view it and interpret it and create a narrative that it is a problem before it becomes a problem. And then you realize that a lot of your emotional energy is spent on reacting to things that your mind is automatically saying are problems — and you don't need all those problems. Do you really need that many problems in your life? Again, I would say try to focus on just one overarching problem, and then go solve that problem. It's like, if you want to be successful, define success very concretely, focus on that — and everything else, when it enters your mind, it becomes a problem. Whether it's a judgment about the girl walking down the street, or the car that just cut in front of you, or whether it's, you know, your accountant did this stupid thing — like, yes, it's going to trigger you...
所以我觉得，能够客观地审视自己的心智、自己的念头，是非常有益的。这正是冥想（meditation）的一大好处：它在你那个有意识的观察之我和你的心智之间，撑开一道小小的缝隙。这样你就能审视自己的念头，多少像评价一个第三方的言论那样去评估它们。如果你把自己的心智直接当成你，二者时时刻刻浑然一体，而你又是从心智里做出反应，那你甚至不会去质疑进入脑中的东西。任何进来、引发你反应的东西，都会立刻引发反应。但如果你能稍微观察一下自己的念头——也不必非得用某种……其实你哪怕通过心理治疗也行，通过写日记也行，用你喜欢的任何方式都行，你甚至可以就去散散长长的步。你不必去冥想、去摆莲花坐，那些都没必要。但只要你能观察自己的念头、稍微客观地看待它们，你就能开始变得更挑剔一点、更带批判一点。你会意识到，现实世界里其实没有什么问题——除了或许那些让你身体疼痛的事。其余一切，都得先在你脑中变成一个问题。你得先去看它、解读它、编出一套"它是个问题"的叙事，它才成为一个问题。然后你会意识到，你大量的情绪能量都花在对那些被你的心智自动判定为问题的事情做反应上——而你根本不需要那么多问题。你的人生真的需要那么多问题吗？我还是那句话，试着只聚焦于一个总揽性的问题，然后去把那个问题解决掉。就好比，如果你想成功，就把成功定义得非常具体，专注于此——而其他一切，一旦进入你的脑子，就都变成了问题。无论是对街上走过的姑娘的一个评判，还是刚加塞抢到你前面的那辆车，又或者是你的会计干了件蠢事——是的，它会触发你（trigger）……
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...but observe for a moment that, like, "It's triggering me. I've created a problem. Do I really want to have this problem right now? Do I want to spend the energy on this problem, or do I want that going somewhere else?" And it doesn't have to be that overt. You don't have to — the mind mud-wrestling with itself is also a problem.
……但你不妨观察那么一下，比如"它在触发我。我制造出了一个问题。我此刻真的想要这个问题吗？我想把能量花在这个问题上，还是想让它流向别处？"而且这不必那么外显。你不必——心智跟自己玩泥地摔跤，本身也是个问题。
Chris（克里斯）：
Because I'd love to do that. I have my problems, my problems have got problems, and I have a real problem about fixing my problems.
因为我特爱这么干。我有我的问题，我的问题还自带问题，而且我在"修理我的问题"这件事上，本身就有个大问题。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, exactly. So you're just going to be much happier and much more focused. Again, I think happiness and focus and success can kind of complement each other. You're going to have much more energy — just think about it as mental energy — you're going to have much more mental energy to focus on the actual problems you want to solve, if you don't start unconsciously, subconsciously, reactively picking up problems everywhere. So before anything can be a problem that takes up your emotional energy, you have to accept it as a problem. You can be choosy about your problems. And I'm not saying I'm perfect in that regard, but I think I'm better than I used to be.
对，正是。所以你会快乐得多、专注得多。我还是认为，快乐、专注和成功多少是能互补的。你会有多得多的能量——就把它当作心理能量来想——你会有多得多的心理能量去专注于你真正想解决的问题，只要你不开始无意识地、潜意识地、条件反射地到处捡起问题。所以在任何东西能成为一个占用你情绪能量的问题之前，你都得先接受它是个问题。你可以挑剔地选择自己的问题。我并不是说我在这方面做得完美，但我想我比从前强了。

对掌控之外的事冷漠 · 模因病毒与"先把自家整顿好"

Chris（克里斯）：
Well, lots of people are addicted to solving problems — so much so that sometimes people create problems when we don't have any, simply so that we can solve them. We have that going on, and then even worse is we take on problems that we can't affect.
是啊，很多人对解决问题上了瘾——瘾大到我们有时候明明没问题，也要造出问题来，纯粹是为了能解决它们。我们身上就有这毛病，而更糟的是，我们还揽下那些我们根本影响不了的问题。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So, you know, another one of my little quips was: a rational person should cultivate indifference to things that are out of their control. Or — a rational person can find peace by cultivating indifference to things that are out of their control. And I'm as guilty as anybody of doom-surfing on X or social media and getting worked up about things that I can't do anything about, right? Like, do I want to be fighting those battles in my mind when I literally cannot do anything about it? So if you find yourself looping on a problem — like you're watching the news too much and you're getting caught up in a problem you can't do anything about — you have to step away from that. And modern media is a delivery mechanism for mimetic viruses. And now what's happened is — you know, 100 years ago, 500...
所以，我还有一句小格言是这么说的：一个理性的人应该培养对那些不在自己掌控之内的事的冷漠。或者说——一个理性的人，可以通过培养对掌控之外之事的冷漠，来获得内心的平静。而我跟任何人一样有罪，照样会在 X 或社交媒体上刷末日新闻（doom-surfing），为那些我根本无能为力的事情上火，对吧？比如说，我真的想在脑子里去打那些仗吗——明明我对它字面意义上无能为力？所以如果你发现自己在某个问题上反复打转——比如你新闻看得太多，陷进了一个你毫无办法的问题里——你就必须从中抽身。而现代媒体，是一套传播模因病毒（mimetic viruses）的投递机制。如今发生的情况是——你知道，一百年前、五百……
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...years ago, if something wasn't happening in your immediate vicinity, you wouldn't hear about it, it wouldn't be a problem for you. But now every single one of the world's problems has turned into a mimetic virus, which is going into the battlefield of the news and is trying to infect your mind in real time.
……年前，如果一件事不是发生在你身边近处，你根本不会听说，它对你来说也就不成其为问题。但现在，世界上的每一个问题都变成了一种模因病毒，冲进新闻这片战场，试图实时地感染你的心智。
Chris（克里斯）：
Hyperspeak.
超速传播（hyperspeak）。〔存疑：原词"Hyperspeak"听感不确定，疑为口误或新造词，暂保留原文〕
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. So that you become obsessed with the war in Ukraine, which is really far away, or you get obsessed with climate change, or you get obsessed with AI doom, or you get obsessed with whatever. And there's nothing as riveting as the old religion: the world is ending, the world is ending, pay attention, the world is ending.
对。于是你变得执迷于乌克兰战争——那其实离你很远——或者执迷于气候变化，或者执迷于 AI 末日（AI doom），或者执迷于随便什么。而没有什么比那套古老的宗教更扣人心弦了：世界要完了，世界要完了，快注意，世界要完了。
Chris（克里斯）：
It's like Cassandra complex at global scale.
这就像是全球尺度的卡珊德拉情结（Cassandra complex）。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Cassandra complex at global scale. And I would argue that large percentages of the population are essentially just infected with these mimetic viruses that have taken over their brain and are causing them to do incredible gyration about things that probably aren't even true, or are greatly exaggerated. But even to the extent they are true, they're things that that person can do nothing about. And they should put their own house in order first. So, you know, another little line I have for myself is: "Your family is broken, but you're going to fix the world," right? People are running out there to try and fix the world when their own lives are a mess, right? And I think it defies credibility if you can't fix your own life first. I'm not going to take you seriously if you can't fix your own life. Like all these philosophers who seem like people you emulate and so smart, or like these brilliant celebrities, and they go off and commit suicide — well, you just kind of invalidated your whole way of life. It's like that line in No Country for Old Men, where the killer is waiting for the protagonist, and the protagonist shows up, and the killer says, "Well, if your set of rules brought you here, then what good are your rules?" Didn't work.
全球尺度的卡珊德拉情结。我会说，相当大比例的人口，本质上就是被这些模因病毒感染了，病毒接管了他们的大脑，让他们为那些很可能根本不是真的、或被极度夸大的事情，做出难以置信的扭动挣扎。可即便那些事在某种程度上是真的，它们也是那个人毫无办法去改变的事。他们应该先把自己家里整顿好。所以，我还有一句给自己的小话是："你的家庭一团糟，你却要去拯救世界"，对吧？人们冲出去想拯救世界，可自己的生活却乱成一锅粥，对吧？我觉得，如果你连自己的人生都修不好，那就毫无可信度可言。如果你修不好自己的人生，我不会拿你当回事。就像那些看似值得你效仿、聪明绝顶的哲学家，或者那些才华横溢的名人，结果他们跑去自杀了——那你基本上就把自己的整套活法给推翻了。这就像《老无所依》（No Country for Old Men）里的那句台词，杀手等着主角，主角出现了，杀手说："要是你那套规矩把你带到了这步田地，那你的规矩还有什么用？"行不通嘛。

智力的真正考验 · 得到你想要的，并想要对的东西

Chris（克里斯）：
I am holistically selfish in that I want to be objectively successful in everything I set out to want.
我是整体性自私的，意思是我想在我立志去要的每一件事上，都取得客观意义上的成功。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, you have one life — don't settle for mediocrity.
对，你只有一条命——别将就于平庸。
Chris（克里斯）：
Don't settle for mediocrity.
别将就于平庸。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And I think — people debate intelligence, for example. We talk about IQ tests and all that. But I think the only true test of intelligence is if you get what you want out of life.
而且我觉得——比如人们老争论智力。我们谈 IQ 测试之类的。但我认为，智力唯一真正的考验，是你能否从人生里得到你想要的东西。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And there are two parts to that. One is getting what you want — so you know how to get it. And the second is wanting the right things — knowing what to want in the first place. I could want to be a six-foot-eight basketball player, and I'm not going to get that. So it's wanting the wrong thing.
而这里头有两部分。一是得到你想要的——也就是你懂得如何去得到它。二是想要对的东西——一开始就知道该想要什么。我可以想当一个六英尺八（约 2.03 米）的篮球运动员，但我得不到。那就是想要了错的东西。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's wanting something that you can't get.
那是想要某个你得不到的东西。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That's wanting something you can't get. It's also wanting something that you don't want.
那是想要某个你得不到的东西。它还包括想要某个你其实并不想要的东西。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, wanting something that's a booby prize.
对，想要某个"安慰奖式的废物"（booby prize）。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
There are plenty of booby prizes out there too, right?
外面也有的是这种安慰奖式的废物，对吧？
Chris（克里斯）：
I haven't heard that word in about 20 years.
这个词我大概有二十年没听过了。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, prizes that are just not worth having, or that create their own problems. But if you're not careful, you can end up in a place in life — not only that you don't want to be, but one that you didn't even mean to get to. That's if you're kind of proceeding unconsciously. But how many people are? And usually I think people end up there because they're going on autopilot with sort of societal expectations, or other people's expectations. So, you know, or out of guilt, or out of like mimetic desire. You know, Peter Thiel has this whole thing from René Girard about how mimetic desires are picked up from other people. And some of those are automatically baked into society — like, you know, go to law school, go to med school, go to whatever, go to business school. Or they might be from watching what your friends are doing, you know, what the other monkeys are doing. Or it might just be what your parents' expectations are. It might be guilt — you know, guilt is just society's voice speaking in your head, socially programmed so you'll be a good little monkey and do things that are good for the tribe. But I think the best outcomes come when you think it through for yourself and decide for yourself. And I don't think people spend enough time deciding. For example, we run on these four-year cycles. In Silicon Valley, you go join a startup, you vest your stock over four years — that's the standard. In college, you go for four years. High school, you go for four years. Some things take longer: you have children, they hit puberty nine years later — that's like a nine-year cycle until that relationship changes. But we're used to these fairly long cycles, multi-year cycles, in which we are committed to things. You go to law school, you know, four- or five-year cycle. You go be a lawyer, 40-year cycle. These are very long...
对，那些根本不值得拥有、或者自带一堆问题的奖品。但如果你不小心，你可能在人生里落到一个境地——不只是你不想待的境地，而是一个你压根没打算抵达的境地。那是在你某种程度上无意识地往前走的情况下。可有多少人不是这样呢？而我觉得，人们之所以落到那种境地，通常是因为他们开着自动驾驶，跟着某种社会期待、或别人的期待在走。还有，你知道，出于内疚，或者出于那种模因式欲望（mimetic desire）。你知道，彼得·蒂尔（Peter Thiel）有一整套取自勒内·吉拉尔（René Girard）的理论，讲模因式欲望是怎样从别人那里习得的。其中有些是自动烘焙进社会里的——比如说，去上法学院、去上医学院、去上随便什么、去上商学院。又或者它们来自你看着你朋友们在做什么，你知道，其他猴子在做什么。又或者它纯粹是你父母的期待。它可能是内疚——你知道，内疚不过是社会的声音在你脑中说话，被社会编程好，好让你做一只乖巧的小猴子，去做对部落有好处的事。但我认为，最好的结果，出自你为自己把事情想透、为自己做决定的时候。而我觉得人们花在做决定上的时间根本不够。比如说，我们都按这种四年周期在运转。在硅谷，你去加入一家创业公司，你的股票分四年归属（vest）——这是标准。上大学，读四年。读高中，读四年。有些事更久：你生了孩子，九年后他们进入青春期——那相当于一个九年周期，那段关系才会改变。但我们已经习惯了这些相当长的周期、多年的周期，在其中我们对各种事情做出承诺。你去上法学院，四到五年的周期。你去当律师，四十年的周期。这些都是非常长的……

决策时间与秘书定理 · 用比例去想，靠次数而非时间

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...cycles. The amount of time we spend deciding what to do and who to do it with — very short, very, very short, right? We spend, you know, three months deciding, one month deciding, on a job where we're going to be for 10 years or 5 years. And because a lot of discovery is path-dependent — where the next thing you find on the path is dependent on where you were on the previous path — you sort of start going down this vector that is a very long distance. People decide frivolously which city to live in, and that's going to decide who their friends are, what their jobs are, their opportunity, their weather, their food supply, their air supply, quality of life. It's such an important decision, but people spend so little time thinking it through. I would argue that if you're making a four-year decision, spend a year thinking it through — like, really thinking it through.
……周期。而我们花在决定做什么、跟谁一起做上的时间——非常短，非常非常短，对吧？我们就花，你知道，三个月去决定、一个月去决定一份我们将干上十年或五年的工作。又因为很多发现是路径依赖的（path-dependent）——你在路上发现的下一样东西，取决于你在前一段路上所处的位置——你就这么开始顺着一个跨度极长的向量走下去。人们轻率地决定住在哪座城市，而这将决定他们的朋友是谁、工作是什么、有什么机会、什么样的天气、食物供给、空气供给、生活质量。这是个如此重要的决定，人们却花那么少的时间去把它想透。我会说，如果你在做一个四年期的决定，那就花一年去把它想透——我是说，真正地想透。
Chris（克里斯）：
25% of the time.
花上总时长的 25%。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, exactly.
对，正是。
Chris（克里斯）：
There's the secretary theorem — I don't know if you know that one. Is that, after you've done this many people, pick the best one of the next however many?
有个"秘书定理"（secretary theorem）——不知道你知不知道这个。是不是说，在你面试过这么多人之后，从接下来那么多人里挑最好的那个？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That's right. Yeah. The secretary theorem is: this computer science professor is trying to figure out how much time he should spend interviewing secretaries, and then how long to keep the secretary. So let's say he's going to have a secretary for 10 years. Does he keep searching for, you know, one year, two years, three years? One month, two months? What is the optimal time? And it turns out that the optimal time is somewhere around a third — about a third of the way through, you take the best person you've worked with and try to find someone that good or better. So by the time you've gone about a third of the way through, you have — excuse me — seen enough that you now have a sense of what the bar is. And then anybody who meets or exceeds that bar is good enough. And this applies to dating, this applies to jobs and careers, this applies generally. But the interesting thing about the secretary theorem is that it's actually not time-based. It's not based on one-third of the time. It's iteration-based.
没错。是的。秘书定理是这样：有位计算机科学教授想搞清楚，他该花多少时间去面试秘书，然后该把这位秘书留用多久。比方说他打算用一位秘书十年。那他要不停地物色一年、两年、三年？还是一个月、两个月？最优的时间是多少？结果发现，最优时间大约在三分之一处——大约走到三分之一的地方，你就把你共事过的最好的那个人定下来作为标杆，然后去找一个跟他一样好或更好的人。所以等你走过大约三分之一，你就已经——不好意思——见得够多，对"门槛在哪"有了感觉。此后任何达到或超过那道门槛的人，就够好了。这适用于约会，适用于工作和职业，普遍适用。但秘书定理有意思的一点在于，它其实并不是基于时间的。它不是基于三分之一的时间，而是基于迭代次数（iteration-based）。
Chris（克里斯）：
The number of candidates. The number of shots you took on goal.
候选人的数量。你朝球门射门的次数。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That's right. So you want to have lots and lots of iterations. So in that sense, you need to bail out quickly and you need to be decisive quickly.
没错。所以你要有非常非常多次的迭代。从这个意义上说，你需要快速抽身，也需要快速果断。
Chris（克里斯）：
You need to take opportunities quickly and bail out quickly.
你需要快速抓住机会，也快速抽身。

学习 · 一万次迭代，而非一万小时

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Correct. Like if you go back and you look through failed relationships, probably the biggest regret will be staying in the relationship after you knew it was over. Exactly. You should have left sooner. The moment you knew it wasn't going to work out, you should have moved on. So in that sense, I think Malcolm Gladwell popularized this idea of 10,000 hours to mastery. I would say it's actually 10,000 iterations to mastery. It's not actually 10,000. It's some unknown number, but it's about the number of iterations that drives a learning curve. And iteration is not repetition. Repetition is a different thing. Repeating is doing the same thing over and over. Iteration is modifying it with a learning and then doing another version of it. So that's error correction. So if you get 10,000 error corrections in anything, you will be an expert at it.
没错。如果你回头看那些失败的恋情，最大的遗憾多半是：明明知道已经结束了，你却还赖在那段关系里。一点没错。你本该早点抽身。当你意识到它不会有结果的那一刻，你就该往前走了。所以从这个意义上说，我觉得马尔科姆·格拉德威尔（Malcolm Gladwell）让"一万小时成大师"这个说法流行了起来。可我会说，真相其实是"一万次迭代成大师"。也不真的就是一万——是某个未知的数字，但关键在于推动学习曲线的迭代次数。而迭代不等于重复。重复是另一回事，重复是把同一件事一遍遍地做。迭代是带着一点领悟去修改它，再做出另一个版本。所以那就是纠错。所以只要你在任何事上累积了一万次纠错，你就会成为这件事的专家。

心态 · 别和愤世嫉俗者合伙：悲观是进化的产物

Chris（克里斯）：
Don't partner with cynics and pessimists. You mentioned there about the people who've got a nightmare going on at home and are trying to fix the world. But a lot of the time that cynicism and pessimism, we find in ourselves. We see the world, whether we want to, whether it's because we've imbibed what the news or the negative people around us have said, or it's a bit more kind of endogenous than that. It's just sort of in us. It's the way that we see the world. How can people avoid cynicism and pessimism within themselves?
"别和愤世嫉俗者、悲观主义者合伙。"你刚才提到那些自家一团糟、却想着去拯救世界的人。但很多时候，那种愤世嫉俗和悲观，是在我们自己身上找到的。我们看待世界的方式——不管我们愿不愿意，不管是因为我们吸收了新闻或身边那些负能量的人所说的话，还是它更像是内源性的、就长在我们心里——总之就是我们看世界的方式。人怎么才能避免自己内心的愤世嫉俗和悲观？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. Cynicism and pessimism is a tough one. We're naturally hardwired for it. Again, and I go back to evolution, I'm sorry to keep harping on evolution, but within biology, there's very few good explanatory theories and, you know, theory of evolution by natural selection is probably the best one. So if you can't explain something about life or psychology or human nature through evolution, then you probably don't have a good theory for it. And I would say that pessimism is another one that comes out of this, which is in the natural environment, you're hardwired to be pessimistic because let's say that I see something rustling in the woods. And if I move towards it and it turns out to be food and prey, then good, I get to eat one meal. But if it turns out to be a predator, I get eaten. And that's the end of that. So we are hardwired to avoid ruin and just dying. So we are naturally hardwired to be pessimists. But modern society is very different. Despite whatever problems you may have with modern society, it is far, far safer than living in the jungle and just trying to survive. And the opportunities and the upside are nonlinear.
是啊。愤世嫉俗和悲观是个难题。我们天生就被写死了会这样。我又得搬出进化论了——抱歉我老是揪着进化论不放，但在生物学里，真正有解释力的理论极少，你懂的，"自然选择下的进化论"大概是最好的那一个。所以如果你没法用进化论去解释关于生命、心理或人性的某件事，那你多半就还没有一个好的理论。我会说，悲观也是从这里冒出来的：在自然环境里，你天生被设定成悲观的，因为——比方说我看到树林里有东西在窸窸窣窣地动。如果我朝它走过去，结果它是食物、是猎物，那好，我能吃上一顿。可如果它原来是个捕食者，那我就被吃了，故事到此为止。所以我们天生被设定成要规避万劫不复、规避送命。所以我们天生就被写死了是悲观主义者。但现代社会很不一样。不管你对现代社会有多少不满，它都远比在丛林里求生要安全太多太多了。而且机会与上行空间是非线性的。

投资与决策 · 上行无限，对具体悲观、对整体乐观

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
For example, when you're investing, if you short a stock, the most money you can make is 2x. You just lose, you know, if the stock goes to zero, you double your money. But if the stock is the next NVIDIA and it goes 100x or 1,000x, you make a lot of money. So upside because of leverage is nearly unlimited. Also in modern society, because there's so many different people you can interact with, if you go on a date and it fails, there are infinite more people to go on a date with. In a tribal system, there might have been 20 people and you can't even get through all of them. So modern society is far more forgiving of failure and you just have to sort of neocortically realize and override that. You have to realize that you're much more running a search function to find the thing that'll work, and then that one thing will pay off in massive compounding. Once you find your mate for the rest of your life, you find your wife or your husband, then you can compound in that relationship. It's okay if you had 50 failed dates in between. The same way once you find the one business you're meant to plow into and it'll compound returns, it's okay if you had 50 small failed ventures or 50 small failed job interviews. The number of failures doesn't matter. And so there's no point in being a pessimist. You want to be an optimist, but I would say you want to be skeptical about specific things. Every specific opportunity is probably a fail, but you want to be optimistic in the general. In the general, you want to be like something in here is going to work out.
举个例子，你做投资时，如果你做空一只股票，你最多能赚两倍——你只是赚，懂吧，如果股票跌到零，你的钱翻一番。但如果这只股票是下一个英伟达（NVIDIA），涨了100倍、1000倍，你就赚得盆满钵满。所以因为有杠杆（leverage），上行空间几乎是无限的。同样在现代社会，因为你能打交道的人形形色色多得是，你去约会一次失败了，还有无穷多的人可以去约。在部落体系里，可能拢共就20个人，你连都约不完。所以现代社会对失败要宽容得多，你只需要在新皮层（neocortex）层面上意识到这一点、并去覆盖掉那套本能。你得意识到，你更多是在运行一个搜索函数，去找到那个行得通的东西，然后这一个东西就会以巨大的复利（compounding）回报你。一旦你找到了相伴余生的伴侣，找到了你的妻子或丈夫，你就能在那段关系里复利累积。哪怕这中间你有50次失败的约会，也没关系。同样，一旦你找到了那个你注定要倾力投入、能带来复利回报的事业，哪怕你之前有50个夭折的小创业、50场失败的求职面试，也没关系。失败的次数无所谓。所以当悲观主义者毫无意义。你要当个乐观主义者，但我会说，你要对具体的事保持怀疑。每一个具体的机会大概率都会失败，但你要在整体上乐观。在整体上，你要相信这一摊子里总有什么会成。
Chris（克里斯）：
How do you navigate that tension?
你怎么去拿捏这中间的张力？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I mean, exactly as I said, I'm optimistic in the general that if something fails right now, then — this is a little woo-woo — but it wasn't meant to be. It was a learning experience. It was an iteration. As long as I learned something from it, then it's a win. If I didn't learn from it, then it's a loss. But as long as you're learning and you keep iterating fast and cutting your losses quickly, then when you find the right thing, you have to be optimistic and compound into it. So you don't want to jump into the first thing. You don't want to marry the first person you date necessarily, unless you got very lucky.
我是说，就像我刚说的，我在整体上是乐观的：如果某件事此刻失败了，那么——这话有点玄乎——它本来就不该成。它是一次学习经历，是一次迭代。只要我从中学到了点什么，那就是赢。如果我什么都没学到，那才算输。但只要你在学习、在快速迭代、在迅速止损，那么当你找到对的那个东西时，你就必须乐观，并把复利押进去。所以你不该一头扎进碰到的第一个东西。你不必非得嫁/娶你约会的第一个人——除非你运气特别好。

策略 · 杠铃策略：探索要快，下注要狠

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
But you want to investigate and explore very, very quickly until you find the match. And then you have to be willing to go all in. You have to be willing to move your chips to the center of the table. So both those approaches are required. So it's a barbell strategy. It's sort of black or it's white. And most people are sort of stuck in this gray bit. And I'm like half in, but I kind of don't really know if I am. I also think labels like pessimists, optimists, cynics, introvert, extrovert, these are very self-limiting. Humans are very dynamic. There are times when you feel like being introverted. There are times when you feel like being extroverted. There are contexts in which you'll be pessimistic. There are contexts in which you'll be optimistic. Leave all those labels alone. It's better just to look at the problem at hand, look at reality the way it is. Try to take yourself out of the equation in a sense. Like obviously you're involved, but motivated reasoning is the worst kind of reasoning. You're not going to find truth through highly motivated reasoning. You have to be objective. And objective means trying to take yourself out of it as much as possible, or at least your personality out of it as much as possible. And so to the extent you run with this thick identity and personality, it's going to cloud your judgment. It's going to try and lock you into the past. If you say, I'm a depressed, unhappy person, yeah, I'm going to be unhappy. That's a way of locking yourself into your past. Even saying, I have trauma, I have PTSD. Yeah, you feel something. There are memories, there are flashes, there are occasional bad feelings, but don't define yourself by it because then you'll lock it into your identity and just going to loop on it. It's better to stay flexible because reality is always changing and you have to be able to adapt to it. Adaptation is also intelligence. Adaptation is survival. Adaptation is kind of how you're here. You're here because you're an adapter and your ancestors were adapters. So to adapt, you will be able to see things clearly, and if you're seeing them through your own identity, it's going to cloud your judgment.
但你要非常非常快地去考察、去探索，直到找到那个匹配的。然后你必须愿意全押。你必须愿意把筹码全推到桌子中央。所以这两种打法都得有。这是一种杠铃策略（barbell strategy），要么黑、要么白。而大多数人卡在中间那块灰色地带，像是"我算半进去了吧，可我自己也不太确定算没算"。我还认为，像悲观者、乐观者、愤世嫉俗者、内向者、外向者这类标签，是非常自我设限的。人是非常动态的。有时候你想内向一点，有时候你想外向一点。有些情境下你会悲观，有些情境下你会乐观。把那些标签统统扔一边。不如就看眼前的问题，看现实本来的样子。某种意义上，试着把你自己从这道方程里抽出来——你显然身在其中，但动机性推理（motivated reasoning）是最糟糕的一种推理。带着强烈动机去推理，你是找不到真相的。你必须客观。而客观意味着尽量把你自己抽离出来，至少把你的个性尽量抽离出来。所以你越是顶着一个厚重的身份和个性去行事，它就越会蒙蔽你的判断，越会把你锁死在过去。如果你说"我是个抑郁、不快乐的人"，那好，我就会一直不快乐。那是一种把自己锁进过去的方式。哪怕说"我有创伤，我有创伤后应激障碍（PTSD）"——是，你确实感受到了点什么，有记忆，有闪回，偶尔有糟糕的情绪，但别拿它来定义自己，因为那样你就会把它锁进你的身份里，然后就一直在它上面打转。还是保持灵活更好，因为现实永远在变，你必须能去适应它。适应也是智力，适应就是生存，适应大体上就是你今天能在这儿的原因。你在这儿，是因为你是个适应者，你的祖先也都是适应者。所以为了适应，你要能把事情看清楚；而如果你是透过自己的身份去看它们，那就会蒙蔽你的判断。

幸福 · 幸福是"对当下感到没问题"

Chris（克里斯）：
Moving on to sort of thinking about happiness — obviously a topic of yours. That's, it's honestly the one that I feel least qualified to talk about. Is it like a guy that's got long arms teaching you how to bench press, or a dude that's really tall teaching you how to deadlift? Someone that feels like they came from behind the eight ball?
接下来聊聊关于幸福的思考——这显然是你常谈的题目。说实话，这是我最觉得自己没资格谈的一个。这是不是有点像一个手臂很长的人教你怎么卧推，或者一个个子很高的人教你怎么硬拉？一个感觉自己一开始处境就不利、是从坑里爬出来的人？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, you're asking a crazy person about their thoughts. So just thought it through. Is happiness still more about peace than it is about joy? It's just one of those overloaded words that means different things to different people. So I'm not even sure we're communicating the same language, but what is happiness?
是啊，你这是在问一个疯子他的想法。所以，姑且把它想透吧。幸福是不是仍然更多关乎平静、而非快乐？它就是那种被塞了太多含义的词，对不同的人意味着不同的东西。所以我都不确定我们说的是不是同一种语言——但，幸福到底是什么？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I think it's just basically being okay with where you are. Not wanting? Not wanting things to be different than the way they are. Not having the sense that anything is missing in this moment. Needing something to change — your current positive situation being contingent on an adjustment, I'm getting something from the outside world. Ironically, I think most people, if you were to ask them when they were happiest for a sustained period of time — not for a brief moment, because pleasure can override happiness and create kind of this illusion of happiness — but if you ask people when they were happy for a sustained period of time, they were probably doing some variation of nothing.
我觉得它基本上就是：对你所在的处境感到没问题。不渴求？不渴求事情变成另一个样子。不觉得此刻有什么是缺失的。需要某样东西来改变——你当下的好状态得依赖于某种调整，依赖于我从外部世界获取点什么。讽刺的是，我觉得大多数人，如果你去问他们持续一段时间里最幸福是什么时候——不是某个短暂的瞬间，因为愉悦（pleasure）能盖过幸福、制造出一种幸福的错觉——但如果你去问人们持续一段时间里幸福是什么时候，那时他们多半是在做某种形式的"无所事事"。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's interesting. Because in the chase is this sort of lack, this contingency.
这有意思。因为在追逐之中，藏着一种匮乏感、一种"取决于外物"的依赖性。

幸福 · 极乐机器：人真正要的是惊喜

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That's right. But then you get bored. If you just sit around all the time, you get bored. So you want adventure, you want surprise. Like there's the funny thought experiment of the bliss machine, right? Which is, suppose I could drill a hole in your head and put an electrode in — and they did this with monkeys — and I can put a wire in there and I can stimulate just the right part of your brain, and I can put you in bliss, and you'll just be in bliss. Would you want that? "Might be nice. For how long?" Do it, and I'll tell you, right? So most people will say, well, I don't want that, I want meaning, I don't want just bliss, I want meaning. And you're like, okay, well I'll put an electrode in there and I'll give you meaning, how about that? And if you kind of run this thought experiment long enough, I think most people realize, actually, what I want is I want surprise. I want the world to surprise me, and I want to wrestle with it in ways that are somewhat predictable, but somewhat not. And you kind of end up back where you started. So I don't know if necessarily, for some people, pure happiness is the ultimate goal. They want to just be blissfully happy wherever they are, whenever they are. But I think other people, most people would
没错。但接着你就会无聊。要是你整天就那么干坐着，你会无聊。所以你想要冒险，想要惊喜。比如有那个有趣的"极乐机器"思想实验，对吧？就是说，假设我能在你脑袋上钻个孔、插进一根电极——人们真在猴子身上做过这个——我往里接根线，刺激你大脑里恰到好处的那个部位，就能把你送进极乐，你就一直处在极乐里。你想要那个吗？"听起来不错。能持续多久？"你接受啊，然后我再告诉你，对吧？所以大多数人会说，不，我不想要那个，我要意义，我不要单纯的极乐，我要意义。然后你说，好啊，那我往里插根电极、给你"意义"，这样如何？而如果你把这个思想实验推演得足够久，我觉得大多数人会意识到：其实我想要的是惊喜。我想要世界给我惊喜，我想以一种半可预测、半不可预测的方式去和它角力。于是你绕了一圈又回到了起点。所以我不确定对某些人来说，纯粹的幸福是不是终极目标。他们想要的就是无论身在何时何地都极乐地幸福着。但我觉得另一些人、大多数人会——
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
— say, well, I'm here in this world, I'm here in this life, I don't understand it or why, but I want to be engaged. I want to be surprised. I want to do things. I want to accomplish things. I want to want things and then get them, right? That's kind of the whole game that we're all playing here.
——会说，好吧，我来到了这个世界，我活在这一生里，我不懂它、也不懂为什么，但我想投入其中。我想被惊喜到。我想做事，想达成些什么。我想去渴望某些东西、然后把它们得到手，对吧？这差不多就是我们所有人在这里玩的整盘游戏。

多巴胺与控制 · 掌控欲反而扼杀了惊喜

Chris（克里斯）：
Surprises are really interesting, the sort of unpredictability. I think — total bro science here — but I'm pretty sure that that's kind of how dopamine works, that things are a bit better than you expected. That within that, it means that if you, for the perennial insecure overachievers that cloy for control, that really want to be able to — the schedule is perfectly done and we know the itinerary, we know where we're going to be at this time — you're in some ways, I guess, reducing down the capacity for surprise because everything has become very contrived, prescribed, done in advance, laid out. Your ability to be surprised actually diminishes.
惊喜真的很有意思，那种不可预测性。我觉得——这里纯属民科——但我挺确定多巴胺大概就是这么运作的：事情比你预期的好那么一点点。这就意味着，对那些长期没有安全感、又渴求掌控的过度成就者来说，他们特别想要——日程排得天衣无缝，行程我们都清楚，几点钟该在哪儿我们都知道——某种程度上，我猜，你其实是在压缩惊喜的容量，因为一切都变得非常刻意、被规定好、提前办妥、安排到位。你被惊喜到的能力反而萎缩了。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, if nothing worked out the way you expected, if it was all serendipity and you didn't want that, you would just be a ball of anxiety. On the other hand, if everything worked out as you expected and wanted, you'd be so bored you might as well be dead. So there's some — you know, the river of life kind of flows between these two banks, and enjoy it.
是啊，如果没有一件事按你预期的来，一切全凭机缘巧合、而你又不想要这种局面，那你就会变成一团焦虑。另一方面，如果一切都恰如你所预期、所想要的那样发生，你又会无聊到生不如死。所以——你懂的，生命之河大体就在这两岸之间流淌，享受它就好。

自我 · 反思与反刍的分界线

Chris（克里斯）：
You say thinking about yourself is the source of all unhappiness, but presumably you need to work on yourself and your weaknesses as well, so some degree of reflection is important. And if thinking about yourself is a source of unhappiness, is this a price that you need to pay? I need to sort of reflect inward, I'm going to have to diminish this level of happiness for a little while, and then I can use this new level — I've got my brown belt on and I can go out into the world as a brown belt.
你说"想着自己"是一切不快乐的根源，但你大概也得在自己身上、在自己的弱点上下功夫，所以某种程度的反思是重要的。如果"想着自己"是不快乐的一个根源，那这是不是一笔你必须付的代价？我得向内反思一阵，我得暂时把幸福水平往下压一压，然后我才能用上这个新的层级——我系上了我的棕带，可以以一个棕带的身份走进这个世界。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
What I'm specifically referring to is that if you're thinking about your personality and your ego and the character of you, and you're obsessing over that, that's where a lot of depression and unhappiness sort of lingers and gets cultivated. So thinking about "woe is me, this happened to me, that happened to me, I have this personality,
我特指的是这一种：如果你在想着你的个性、你的自我（ego）、那个"你"的人设，并且对此念念不忘，那就是大量抑郁和不快乐滞留、并被培育出来的地方。所以那种"我好惨啊，这事落在了我头上，那事也落在我头上，我有这样的个性，
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I have this issue, I deserve this, I didn't get that" — that's, you're just strengthening a little beast in there that is insatiable. And that's where I think a lot of unhappiness comes from. "What's the beast?" It's the ego. But that word is so overused that I kind of hate to use the word. But it's like a recurrent collection of thoughts that are very self-obsessed and will never be satisfied, and very concretized as well, so they're not malleable, not particularly flexible. You're just adding to them by thinking about them all the time. You're creating narratives and stories and identities. But that's different from solving personal problems. So if you encounter something, you learn from something, you're reflecting upon the learning, then you can reflect upon it, absorb it, and then just move on. But sitting there saying, "I'm Chris, I'm Naval, I deserve this, this happened to me, that person wronged me, this is who I am, this shouldn't have happened, I need to go get revenge on this, or I need to fix that or change this" — I mean, that I think is where a lot of mental illness comes from. So it depends if you are thinking about something to solve a problem and get it off your chest and get it off your mind. If it leaves your mind clearer at the end of it, then I think it was worthwhile. If it leaves your mind busier at the end of it, then you're probably going in the wrong direction.
我有这个毛病，我活该得到这个，我没得到那个"——这一切，你只是在喂养里头那只小野兽，一只永远喂不饱的野兽。我觉得大量不快乐就是从这儿来的。"那野兽是什么？"是自我（ego）。但这个词被用滥了，我都有点不想用它了。可它就像是一团反复出现的念头，极度自我沉迷、永远不会满足，而且还被固化得很死，所以它们不可塑、不太灵活。你成天想着它们，只是在给它们添砖加瓦。你在制造叙事、故事和身份。但这和解决个人问题是两码事。所以如果你遇到一件事、从一件事里学到了东西、你在反思这份领悟，那你可以去反思它、吸收它，然后就往前走。可要是坐在那儿念叨"我是克里斯，我是纳瓦尔，我活该得到这个，这事落在我头上，那个人亏待了我，这就是我这个人，这事本不该发生，我得去找它报仇，或者我得去修好那个、改掉这个"——我是说，我觉得大量精神疾病就是这么来的。所以要看你想一件事是不是为了解决问题、把它从胸口卸下来、把它从脑子里清出去。如果想完之后你的脑子更清明了，那我觉得就值得；如果想完之后你的脑子更繁忙了，那你多半是在往错的方向走。

自我 · 超脱是副产品；去想比自己更大的事

Chris（克里斯）：
Is this a justification for detachment, cultivated ignorance, distraction?
这是不是在为"超脱"、为刻意的无知、为分散注意力开脱？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Detachment is not a goal. Detachment is a byproduct. It's just a byproduct of just realizing what matters and what doesn't. And just for one moment on the self thing — I think everybody craves thinking about something more than themselves. If you want to be happy, to some extent you have to forget about your personal problems. And one way to do that is take on other problems, bigger problems. And that could be a mission, that could be spirituality, that could be kids, it could be
超脱不是目标，超脱是副产品。它只是你认清了什么重要、什么不重要之后的副产品。趁着还在说"自我"这件事——我觉得每个人都渴望去想一些比自己更大的东西。如果你想幸福，某种程度上你必须忘掉自己的个人问题。而做到这一点的一个办法，就是去承担别的问题、更大的问题。那可以是一项使命，可以是灵性，可以是孩子，也可以是——
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
— caring about the planet, although I think people take that a little far, you know, and then they get kind of oppressive and tyrannical and support abstract concepts. But so these can be taken too far, just like religion, for example, just like anything in excess. "Anything in excess, right." But generally, the less you think about yourself, the more you can think about a mission or about God or about a child or something like that. So I remember Vinay Hiremath, the founder of Loom, said, "I am rich and I have no idea what to do with my life," and you replied, "God, kids, or mission, pick at least one." That's right. Preferably all three. It's very liberating. Yeah, thinking — I think overthinking about yourself is probably — it may not be the cause of depression, but it certainly doesn't help. "Rumination." Yeah. I kind of had a self-induced Stockholm syndrome from this sort of a thing, because I like to think about stuff, and it provides you with an endless number of things to think about. So you're kind of — yeah, you have this — you're the prisoner and the prison guard at the same time.
——关心地球，不过我觉得有些人在这上头走得有点过了，懂吧，然后他们就变得有点压迫、有点专横，去拥护一些抽象概念。所以这些东西也可能被推得太过，就跟宗教一样，比方说，就跟任何过了头的东西一样。"任何东西一过头，对吧。"但总的来说，你越少想自己，就越能去想一项使命、想上帝、想一个孩子之类的。我记得 Loom 的创始人维奈·希雷马特（Vinay Hiremath）说过："我有钱了，可我完全不知道该拿我这辈子怎么办。"而你回他："上帝、孩子、使命，至少挑一个。"没错，最好三个都要。它很让人解脱。是啊，想——我觉得过度地想自己，大概——它也许不是抑郁的成因，但它肯定帮不上忙。"反刍（rumination）。"对。我从这类事里得了一种自我诱发的斯德哥尔摩综合征，因为我喜欢琢磨东西，而它会给你提供无穷无尽可琢磨的东西。所以你就有点——对，你同时是囚犯，又是看守这个牢的狱卒。

治疗文化 · 想自己越多，越痛苦

Chris（克里斯）：
And I had Abigail Schreier on the show. She wrote this book called Bad Therapy, sort of pushing back against therapy culture specifically for kids. But there was a blast radius that covered pretty much everything, including kind of CBT. And we're getting perilously close to some really evidence-based stuff here. But the more that I've thought about it and the more that I've looked at the evidence, there is basically a direct correlation between how much you think about yourself and how miserable you are.
我请过阿比盖尔·施赖尔（Abigail Schreier）上节目。她写了本书叫《糟糕的治疗》（Bad Therapy），算是在反击治疗文化，尤其是针对儿童的那一套。但它的"爆炸半径"几乎覆盖了所有东西，连认知行为疗法（CBT）那一类都被波及了。我们这是危险地逼近一些真正有实证依据的东西了。但我越是琢磨、越是去看那些证据，就越发现：你想自己想得有多少，和你有多痛苦，基本上是直接相关的。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Therapy is great if it lets you vent and it solves the thing, and then X sessions later you're done, you're clear. But if you're just looping on the same thing forever, then it's actually the opposite. You're bathing in it. You're indulging in it. Yeah.
如果治疗能让你把情绪发泄出来、把那件事解决掉，然后过了X次之后你就结束了、清明了，那它很棒。但如果你只是永远在同一件事上打转，那它实际上就起了相反的作用。你是在里头泡澡，你是在沉溺其中。是啊。

技巧 · "变快乐"的技巧本身就是一种挣扎

Chris（克里斯）：
How have your "become happy" techniques developed over time?
你那些"让自己变快乐"的技巧，这些年是怎么演变的？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, I used to have a lot of them. Now I kind of try not to have any,
是啊，我以前有一大堆。现在我反倒尽量一个都不要，
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
because I think the techniques themselves are kind of a struggle. It's sort of like bidding for status implies you're low status. It reveals that you're low status. So someone who's basically trying to show off comes across as low status. The same way someone who's trying to be happy is sort of saying I'm unhappy and creating that frame. So it's better just to not even think in terms of — "You position yourself as being in lack in order to attain." Yeah, I don't even think in terms of happiness, unhappiness anymore. I just kind of just do my thing.
因为我觉得这些技巧本身就是一种挣扎。这有点像——争抢地位本身就暗示了你地位低，它暴露了你地位低。所以一个基本上在卖力炫耀的人，给人的观感反而是地位低。同理，一个努力想让自己快乐的人，其实是在说"我不快乐"，并制造出那个框架。所以最好连用这种方式去想都不要——"你为了去获得，先把自己摆在了'匮乏'的位置上。"是的，我现在连用"快乐、不快乐"这种框架去想都不想了。我就只是做我自己的事。

成功 · 没有公式，是压倒性的渴望

Chris（克里斯）：
Again, another question that's similar to a bunch of them. Do you think you could have got there had you not done the procedural, systematic, step-by-step-by-step, "this is what it is," and then come out the other side?
又是一个，和前面好几个都类似的问题。你觉得，如果你没走那套程序化的、系统化的、一步接一步的、"事情就是这样这样"的路子、然后从另一头走出来，你还能到达现在这个地方吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I don't think there are any formulas. I think it's unique to each person. It's like asking a successful person, "How did you become successful?" Each one of them will give you a different story. You can't follow anyone else's path. And most of them are even probably telling you some narratized version of it that isn't quite true.
我认为根本没有什么公式。我觉得它对每个人都是独一无二的。这就像去问一个成功的人："你是怎么成功的？"他们每一个人都会给你一个不同的故事。你没法照搬任何别人的路。而且他们当中大多数人，给你讲的甚至多半还是某个被叙事加工过、并不太真实的版本。
Chris（克里斯）：
I mean, that's something that I continually realize, especially as I get to spend more time around people that are successful. And you hear, um, "it's very important to prioritize work-life balance," right? That's one of the most common things that people who have attained success say. That's not my experience.
我是说，这正是我不断意识到的一点，尤其是当我有机会跟成功的人待得更久之后。你会听到，嗯，"把工作与生活的平衡放在首位非常重要"，对吧？这是已经获得成功的人最常说的话之一。可这不是我的亲身经历。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
But if you look at — you shouldn't be asking somebody who is successful, what they do to continue their success now. You should be asking them, what did they do to attain their success when they were where you are? And the people who are really extraordinarily successful didn't sit around watching success porn. They just went and did it. They just had such an overwhelming desire to be successful at the thing that they were doing that they just went and did that thing. They didn't have time to study and learn and listen, and they just did it. It's the overwhelming desire that's the most important, and the focus that comes from that.
但如果你看——你不该去问一个成功者，他现在为了延续成功在做什么；你该问他的是，当他还处在你这个位置时，他为了获得成功做了什么。而那些真正极其成功的人，并不是坐在那儿看"成功色情片"（success porn，指空看别人成功故事却不行动）。他们就是去做了。他们对自己手头那件事就是有一股压倒性的、想要做成的渴望，所以他们就直接去做那件事了。他们没时间去研究、去学习、去听别人讲，他们就是去做了。最重要的就是那股压倒性的渴望，以及由它带来的专注。
Chris（克里斯）：
It's that tweet of yours that was, "people who are good at making wealth," or "people are good at attaining wealth don't need to teach anybody else how to do it."
就是你那条推特说的，"擅长创造财富的人"，或者说"擅长获取财富的人，并不需要去教别人该怎么做。"
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, you don't need mentors, you need action. That was one of them. Another one is, you know,
对，你不需要导师，你需要行动。那是其中一条。还有一条是，你懂的，

睡眠 · 渴望与理解，胜过强迫自己

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
the people who actually know how to make money, you don't need to sell your course on it. Um, yeah, there's lots of variations on it. But — another one — if you don't lie awake at night thinking about it, you don't want it badly enough.
真正懂怎么赚钱的人，你不需要靠卖关于它的课程为生。嗯，对，这条有很多变体。但还有一条——如果你不会半夜睡不着、躺着想着它，那就说明你想要它想得还不够狠。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah. I've heard you talk before about how sort of unclosed loops, problems that you're working on, can cause you to be sleepless and this.
是啊。我以前听你讲过，那些没闭合的"环"、你正在攻的难题，会让你睡不着觉之类的。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I'm not a good sleeper. "Tell me about that." Oh, I mean, my Eight Sleep hates me. It's always funny — I failed at sleeping again. Brian Johnson thinks I'm going to die early. He's probably right. But I —
我睡眠不好。"跟我说说。"哦，我是说，我那台 Eight Sleep（智能床垫）讨厌死我了。每次都挺好笑的——我又一次"睡觉失败"了。布莱恩·约翰逊（Brian Johnson）觉得我会早死。他大概是对的。但我——
Chris（克里斯）：
How much do you reckon you sleep at night? You got any idea?
你估摸自己一晚上睡多久？心里有数吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Oh, it's so random. Some nights I sleep eight hours, some nights I sleep four hours, but it's literally just random.
哦，太随机了。有些晚上我睡八小时，有些晚上睡四小时，但真的就是纯随机。
Chris（克里斯）：
Are you bothered about that? You're trying to optimize your sleep, coach teaching you how to —
你为这个烦恼吗？你在试着优化睡眠、有教练教你怎么——
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I don't flog myself over things. If I want to sleep, I'll sleep. If I don't want to sleep, I don't sleep. It's not a — I don't think I'm doing anything right or wrong. Don't label it good night, bad night. No. I work out every day because I think it gives me more energy and I've gotten into a good habit with it. Maybe I'll do the same thing with sleep, maybe I'll develop a good habit, but I'm not going to beat myself up over it. There'll come a point where it's important to me — when it's important to me, I'll just do it. You know, most of — like, for example, you look at people with addictions, right? Overeating or smoking or whatever, they can kind of go through all the different methods, but it's half-hearted. And then one day they're like, oh shit, I've got lung cancer, my dad has lung cancer, and they drop it immediately. So I think a lot of change is more about desire and understanding than it is about forcing yourself or trying to domesticate yourself.
我不会为这些事鞭打自己。我想睡，我就睡；我不想睡，我就不睡。这不是一个——我不觉得我做对了或做错了什么。别给它贴上"好觉、坏觉"的标签。不必。我每天健身，因为我觉得它给我更多精力，而且我已经把它养成了一个好习惯。也许我会对睡眠如法炮制，也许我会养成一个好习惯，但我不会为它把自己往死里折磨。总会有那么一刻它对我变得重要——等它对我重要了，我自然就去做了。你懂的，大多数——比如说，你看那些有瘾的人，对吧？暴食啊、抽烟啊什么的，他们可以把各种方法都试个遍，但都是半心半意的。然后某一天他们突然："我靠，我得了肺癌，我爸得了肺癌"，于是他们立刻就把烟戒了。所以我觉得，很多改变更多是关乎渴望和理解，而不是关乎强迫自己、或者试图把自己驯化。
Chris（克里斯）：
So it's efficiency again, I guess, you know, aligning the thing that you want to do with the way that you feel about what it is that you want to do.

Naval（纳瓦尔）：

对，是不要被一种"半吊子的渴望"或者模仿性的渴望（mimetic desire）缠住。说到底，就是清楚地觉察到，此时此刻你真正想要的到底是什么。

欲望与压力 · 半心半意做不成事，压力源于两个互相冲突的欲望

你之前提到过焦虑。"想象一下，如果你不再总是焦虑，你会变得多高效"——这是你的一句话。焦虑是 21 世纪当红的情绪。很多有干劲的人都非常焦虑、非常多疑，而这恰恰是他们成功的原因：高度关注、注重细节、不肯放手、夜里辗转难眠地想——这就是多疑在起作用。关于焦虑以及如何应对，你都领悟到了些什么？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So anxiety and stress are interesting—they're very related. Stress is like, if you look at an iron beam, when an iron beam is under stress, it's because it's being bent in two different directions at the same time. So when your mind is under stress, it's because it
焦虑和压力很有意思——它们关系密切。所谓压力（stress），就好比一根铁梁：铁梁承受应力，是因为它同时被往两个不同方向弯折。所以当你的心承受压力时，是因为它……
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
…has two conflicting desires at once. So for example, you want to be liked, but you want to do something selfish, and you can't reconcile the two, and so you're under stress. You want to do something for somebody else, you want to do something for yourself—these are examples. You don't want to go to work, but you want to make money, so you're under stress. So you have two conflicting desires, and I think one of the ways to get through stress is to acknowledge that: "Oh, I actually have two conflicting desires, and either I need to resolve it—I need to pick one and then be okay losing the other—or I will decide later." But at least just being aware of why you're stressed can help alleviate a lot of stress.
……同时有两个互相冲突的欲望。比如，你想被人喜欢，但你又想做件自私的事，两者无法调和，于是你就有了压力。你想为别人做点什么，又想为自己做点什么——这些都是例子。你不想去上班，但你又想赚钱，于是你就有了压力。所以你有两个互相冲突的欲望。我觉得渡过压力的方法之一，就是承认："哦，我其实有两个互相冲突的欲望，要么我得解决它——挑一个，然后接受失去另一个；要么我以后再决定。"但哪怕只是意识到自己为何而压力，也能大大缓解压力。

焦虑的本质 · 焦虑是堆积如山未解决问题露出的冰山一角

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And then anxiety, I think, is sort of this pervasive, unidentifiable stress, where you're just kind of stressed out all the time and you're not even sure why, and you can't even identify the underlying problem. And the reason for that is because you have so many unresolved problems, unresolved stress points that are piled up in your life, that you can no longer identify what the problems are. And there's this mountain of garbage in your mind, and a little bit of it is poking out the top like an iceberg—and that's anxiety. But underneath, there's a lot of unresolved things. And so you just need to kind of go through, very carefully, every time you're anxious: "Okay, why am I anxious this time? I don't know why. Oh well, let me sit here and just think about it. Let me write down what the possible causes could be. Let me meditate on it, let me journal, let me talk to a therapist, let me talk to my friends. Let me just kind of see when does that stress go away." If you can identify and unravel and resolve these issues, then I think that helps get rid of anxiety. A lot of the anxiety is piled up because we move through life too quickly, not observing our own reactions to things; we don't resolve them.
而焦虑（anxiety），我认为是一种弥漫性的、说不清的压力——你就是总感觉压力很大，却连自己为什么也搞不清，甚至找不出背后的问题。原因在于，你生活里堆积了太多未解决的问题、未化解的压力点，多到你已经无法辨认问题到底是什么了。你脑子里有一座垃圾山，只有一小角像冰山一样露出水面——那就是焦虑。但水面之下，藏着大量没有解决的东西。所以你需要每次焦虑时，都非常仔细地去梳理："好，我这次为什么焦虑？我不知道。那好，让我坐下来想一想，把可能的原因写下来，冥想一下，写写日记，找心理咨询师聊聊，找朋友聊聊，看看这股压力什么时候会消退。"如果你能识别、抽丝剥茧并解决这些问题，我觉得就有助于摆脱焦虑。很多焦虑之所以堆积，是因为我们活得太匆忙，没去观察自己对事物的反应，也就没去化解它们。
Chris（克里斯）：
So this goes counter to what I was saying earlier about not reflecting too much on things.
那这跟我之前说的"别太过反刍、反思"不就矛盾了吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
But you reflect on the problems to observe them and solve them; you don't reflect on them to feel better about them, to indulge them. If you're doing it to just feel better about yourself, that could be strengthening your personality and your ego, and could be creating a more fragile
但你反思问题，是为了观察它、解决它；不是为了让自己感觉好受些、去放纵那种情绪。如果你反思只是为了让自己舒服一点，那可能是在加固你的人格和自我（ego），反而会塑造出一个更脆弱的……

死亡与当下 · 一切归零，浪费时间就是不临在于当下

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
…personality. You know, one big anxiety resolver for me is just ruminating on death. I think that's a good one. You're going to die, it's all going to zero, you cannot take anything with you. And I know this is trite, and I know we don't spend enough time thinking about the big questions—we kind of give up on them when we're very, very young. A little child might ask the big questions, like "Why are we here? What's the meaning of life? What is this all about? Is there Santa Claus? Is there God?" But then, as adults, we're taught not to think about these things; we've given up on them. But I think the big questions are the big questions for good reasons. And if you can keep the idea in front of you at all times—that you're going to die, and that everything goes literally to zero—what's there to stress about?
……人格。对我来说，一个很大的"焦虑化解器"就是去反复思量死亡。我觉得这招很好。你终将一死，一切都会归零，你什么都带不走。我知道这话听起来老套，也知道我们花在思考"大问题"上的时间太少了——我们很小很小的时候就放弃了那些问题。小孩子也许会问那些大问题，比如"我们为什么在这里？生命的意义是什么？这一切到底是怎么回事？有圣诞老人吗？有上帝吗？"可一旦长大成人，我们就被教导不要去想这些事，我们放弃了它们。但我认为，大问题之所以是大问题，是有充分理由的。如果你能时时刻刻把这个念头摆在眼前——你终将一死，一切真的会归零——那还有什么好焦虑的呢？
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah. For better or worse, life is very short. How should people deal with its briefness?
是啊。不管是好是坏，人生都很短暂。人们该如何面对它的短促？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Enjoy it. Make the best of it. It's even briefer than that—each moment just disappears. It's gone. There's only a present moment, and it's gone instantly. So if you're not there for it—if you're stressed out, or you're anxious, or you're thinking about something else—you missed it. So any moment when you're not in that moment, you are dead to that moment. You might as well be dead, because your mind is off doing something else, or living in some imagined reality that is just a very poor substitute for the actual reality. So one of my recent realizations was: what is wasted time? What is a waste of time? I don't like to waste time, but what is wasted time? And everything is wasted time in a sense, because nothing matters in the ultimate. But in each moment, the thing matters; in each moment, it's the only thing that matters. Actually, what's happening in front of you literally has all the meaning in the world. And so what matters is just being present for the thing. So if you're doing something that you want to do, and you're fully there for it,
享受它，尽可能过好它。其实它比那还要短促——每一个瞬间都在消失，转眼即逝。只有一个当下，而它瞬间就没了。所以如果你不在场——你压力缠身、焦虑不安、或者心思在别处——你就错过了它。任何一个你没有身处其中的瞬间，你对那个瞬间而言就是死的。你倒不如真死了算了，因为你的心已经跑去干别的，或者活在某种想象出来的现实里，而那只是真实现实的拙劣替代品。所以我最近的一个领悟是：什么是被浪费的时间？什么叫浪费时间？我不喜欢浪费时间，可到底什么才是被浪费的时间？从某种意义上说，一切时间都是被浪费的，因为终极来看一切都无足轻重。但在每一个当下，那件事是重要的；在每一个当下，它是唯一重要的东西。事实上，正在你眼前发生的事，确确实实承载着世上的全部意义。所以真正重要的，就是临在于那件事。所以，如果你在做一件你想做的事，并且全身心地投入其中，

临在与心智 · 浪费的时间是你不临在的时间；要的不是心安，而是心的安宁

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
…then it's not wasted time. If you don't want to do it, and your mind is running away from it, and you're reacting against it, and you're wishing you were somewhere else, and you're thinking about some other thing, or you're anticipating some future thing, or regretting some past thing, or being fearful of something—then that's wasted time. That's time that's being wasted, when you're not actually present for the reality in front of you. So my definition of wasted time… yes, I do want some material things in life, and there are things that have more value than others within this life, but this life is very short and bounded. So the true wasted time is the time that you're not present for—when you're not there for it, when you're not doing the thing you want to do to the best of your capability such that you're immersed in it. If you're not immersed in this moment, then you're wasting your time.
……那它就不是被浪费的时间。如果你并不想做这件事，你的心在逃避它、抗拒它，你希望自己在别处，你想着别的事，或者在期待某件将来的事、懊悔某件过去的事、惧怕某样东西——那就是被浪费的时间。当你并未真正临在于眼前的现实时，时间就被浪费了。所以我对"被浪费的时间"的定义是……没错，我在生活里也确实想要一些物质的东西，而且这一生中确有些东西比另一些更有价值，但这一生非常短暂、有限。所以真正被浪费的时间，就是你没有临在的那些时间——你不在场、你没有竭尽全力去做你想做的事、没有沉浸其中的时间。如果你没有沉浸在当下这一刻里，那你就是在浪费时间。
Chris（克里斯）：
People get worried about dying and no longer being here, but they don't realize that so much of their life is spent not being here in any case.
人们担心死亡、担心不再存在于此，却没意识到，他们一生中有那么多时间，本来就并未"在场"。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That's right. But I think people crave being here for it. And when you're here for it, you're actually not thinking about yourself—you are more immersed in the thing, in the moment, in the task at hand. We don't want peace of mind, we want peace for our mind.
没错。但我觉得人们其实渴望真正在场。而当你在场时，你其实不会想着自己——你更深地沉浸在那件事、那个当下、手头的任务里。我们要的不是"心安"（peace of mind），而是给我们的心一份安宁（peace for our mind）。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's right, yeah.
没错，是的。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
The mind is what will eat you alive if you let it. And there's more to you than the mind.
你若放任它，心智（the mind）会把你活活吞噬。而你远不止是这颗心智。

怎么说？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：

嗯，我不太想去"拆解身体"（disassemble the body），打个比方说。

意识与无常 · 一切皆生灭于意识之中，唯有觉知是真实的

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. At the end of the day, everything arises within your consciousness, right? You've got nowhere else to experience it. And that consciousness is relatively static, in the sense that it's been exactly the same from the moment you were born to the moment you die. And everything that you experience—from your body, to your mind, to the world, to everything—is within that consciousness, that base layer of being. And this is what the Buddhists will tell you is the real thing. Everything that comes and goes in the middle, including your mind, including your body, is unreal. And trying to find
是这样。归根结底，一切都生起于你的意识（consciousness）之中，对吧？你没有别的地方去体验它。而这意识相对来说是恒定的——意思是从你出生那一刻到你死亡那一刻，它一直一模一样。你所体验的一切——从你的身体，到你的心智，到这个世界，到所有的一切——都在那个意识之中，那个存在的底层。佛家会告诉你，这才是真实的东西。而中间那些来来去去的一切，包括你的心智、包括你的身体，都是虚幻的。试图去寻找……
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
…stability in those transient things is [like] a castle that you're building on sand—that's going to crumble. Life is going to play out the way it's going to play out. There will be some good and some bad. Most of it is actually just up to your interpretation. You're born, you have a set of sensory experiences, and then you die. How you choose to interpret those experiences is up to you, and different people interpret them in different ways.
……在那些转瞬即逝的东西里求稳定，就像把城堡建在沙子上——终会崩塌。人生会按它自己的方式上演，有好有坏。其中大部分其实取决于你的诠释。你出生，经历一连串感官体验，然后死去。你如何选择诠释这些体验，全在于你，不同的人会有不同的诠释方式。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, it's the old line about two people walking down the street: they're having the exact same experience, one is happy, one is sad, right? It's a narrative in their heads. It's how they choose to interpret.
是啊，就是那句老话——两个人走在同一条街上，经历完全相同，一个开心，一个难过，对吧？区别只在他们脑子里的叙事，在于他们各自选择如何诠释。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So when I said that, it was a long time ago, I was talking more about having positive interpretations [rather] than negative interpretations. But these days, I think it's better just not to have any interpretations, and to just allow things to be. You're still going to have interpretations—you can't stop it, and nor should you try—but even having an interpretation is just a thing you can leave alone.
我当年说那番话，已经是很久以前了，那时我更多是在讲：要有正面的诠释，而不是负面的诠释。但如今我觉得，最好是干脆不带任何诠释，让一切如其所是。你还是会有诠释——你阻止不了，也不该去阻止——但即便是"有了一个诠释"这件事本身，你也可以随它去、不必理会。

提升生活质量 · 观察自己的心，放手不是一次性事件

Chris（克里斯）：
