头脑与直觉 · 直觉是聚合后的判断力，做难的决定要相信直觉

Chris（克里斯）：
I had this insight, or a question, I guess: how much do you think we should trust the voice in our heads? Because half of wisdom suggests relying on your sort of bottom-up intuition, and then half of it has to be as sort of top-down rational as possible. How do you navigate the tension between head and gut in this way?
我有个领悟，或者说一个问题：你觉得我们该在多大程度上信任脑子里的那个声音？因为有一半的智慧告诉你要靠那种自下而上的直觉，另一半却要尽可能地自上而下、保持理性。在头脑（head）和直觉（gut）之间的这种张力，你是怎么权衡的？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I think the gut is what decides; the head is kind of what rationalizes it afterwards. The gut is the ultimate decision maker.
我认为是直觉在做决定，头脑则是事后给它找理由的那个。直觉才是最终的决策者。
Chris（克里斯）：
And what is the gut?
那直觉到底是什么？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
The gut is refined judgment. It's taste aggregated. And it could be aggregated through evolution—it's in your genes, in your DNA—or it could be aggregated through your experiences and what you've thought through. The mind is good at solving new problems, new problems in the external world that have definite, defined edges—beginnings and ends and objectives. What the mind is actually really bad at is making hard decisions. So when you have a hard decision to make, I find it's better to—yes, you ruminate on it, you think through all the pros and cons, but then you sleep on it. You wait a couple of days. You wait until the gut answer appears with conviction and it feels right. And when you're younger, it takes longer, because you just don't have as much experience. And when you're older, it can happen much faster.
直觉就是经过提炼的判断力，是被聚合起来的品味（taste aggregated）。它可能是通过进化聚合而来的——藏在你的基因里、DNA 里；也可能是通过你的经历、通过你想透的那些事聚合而来。头脑擅长解决新问题，那些外部世界里有明确、清晰边界的新问题——有开头、有结尾、有目标。而头脑真正不擅长的，是做艰难的决定。所以当你要做一个艰难的决定时，我发现更好的办法是——是的，你先去反复掂量，把所有利弊想一遍，但接着把它"睡一觉再说"，等上几天。你等到那个直觉的答案带着笃定浮现、感觉对了为止。年轻时这会花更久，因为你阅历不够；上了年纪后，这个过程会快得多。
Chris（克里斯）：
And you have less time to [decide].
而且你能拖的时间也更少了。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. And old people are more set in their ways as a consequence, right? They know what they want, they know what they don't want. So it takes time to develop your gut instinct and judgment. But
对。结果就是老年人更固执、更"定型"了，对吧？他们知道自己想要什么，也知道自己不想要什么。所以培养你的直觉本能和判断力是需要时间的。但是……

相信直觉 · 直觉一旦养成就别违背它，欲望会盖过判断把你困住

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
…once you've developed them, don't trust anything else, because you can't go against your gut—it'll bite you in the end. Usually, in relationships that failed, you can look back and say, "Oh, actually, I knew it was going to fail because of this reason, but I kind of went ahead anyway, because I wanted it to be this way. I wanted this person to be a different way than they are. I wanted to get a different thing out of it than I thought—than I knew—I was going to get. But I just wanted it." So sometimes desire will override your judgment, and then trap you.
……一旦你把它们培养出来了，就别再信任别的任何东西，因为你违背直觉是不行的——它最终会反咬你一口。通常，回看那些失败的关系，你都能说："哦，其实我早就知道因为这个原因它会失败，但我还是一意孤行了，因为我希望它是那个样子。我希望这个人不是他现在这个样子。我希望从中得到一个不同于我所想、所明知会得到的结果。可我就是想要。"所以有时候，欲望会盖过你的判断，然后把你困住。
Chris（克里斯）：
Wishful thinking.
一厢情愿（wishful thinking）。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, wishful thinking. It traps you into a pathway that just chews up time.
对，一厢情愿。它把你困在一条只会白白吞噬时间的路上。

改变 · 你改不了别人，只能改变自己和对别人的反应

Chris（克里斯）：
What's that insight of yours? "We think we can't change ourselves, but we can; we think we can change other people, but we can't."
你那句领悟怎么说来着？"我们以为自己改变不了自己，其实能；我们以为能改变别人，其实不能。"
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Exactly. I think, to add to that: you can't change other people, [but] you can change your reaction to them. You can change yourself. But other people only change through trauma, or their own insight, on their own schedule, and never in a way that you like.
正是。我想再补一句：你改变不了别人，但你能改变自己对他们的反应。你能改变自己。可别人只会因为创伤、或自己的顿悟而改变，按他们自己的时间表，而且从来不会朝着你喜欢的方向变。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, Alain de Botton says that people do sometimes change, but rarely in relationships, and never when they're told to.
是啊，阿兰·德波顿（Alain de Botton）说过，人有时确实会改变，但很少在亲密关系里改变，而且绝不会因为被命令而改变。

赞美而非批评 · 想改变别人行为，只能在他做对时由衷地赞美

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Absolutely. Yeah, the fastest way to alienate somebody is to tell them to change. In fact, the Dale Carnegie School of Public Speaking—the way that operates—they get you up there, and they realize that the number one problem with public speaking is that people are very self-conscious. And so people who are practicing in the Dale Carnegie School of Public Speaking… I don't know, I never went through it, I heard this second-hand so I could be wrong, but I like the story: they get up and they start speaking, and the people in the audience are only allowed to compliment them—genuine compliments, not fake compliments, on things that they did well. But you're not allowed to criticize them on things that they did poorly. And eventually they kind of get through it and they develop self-confidence. The same way, there's like the Michel Thomas School of language learning, and on that one, what they do is you listen to a teacher talking to a student. They're not teaching you; you're not expected to remember or memorize anything. You just listen to a student stumbling over the language. It's a better way to learn, because you yourself don't feel flustered, [like] you're being tested, graded.
完全同意。是的，让一个人对你心生疏远的最快办法，就是叫他改变。其实，戴尔·卡耐基演讲学校（Dale Carnegie School of Public Speaking）的运作方式就是这样：他们让你上台，他们发现公开演讲的头号问题是人太在意自己（self-conscious）。所以在卡耐基演讲学校练习的人……我不太清楚，我没亲自上过，这是听别人说的，可能有误，但我喜欢这个说法：他们站起来开始讲，而台下的观众只被允许称赞他们——是真诚的赞美，不是虚假的恭维，针对他们做得好的地方。但你不被允许批评他们做得差的地方。最后他们慢慢闯过去了，建立起了自信。同样地，还有米歇尔·托马斯语言学习法（Michel Thomas School of language learning），在那套方法里，你做的就是听一位老师在和一个学生对话。他们不是在教你；你不被要求去记住或背诵任何东西。你只是听着一个学生磕磕巴巴地学语言。这是一种更好的学习方式，因为你自己不会感到慌乱，不会觉得自己正在被测试、被打分。
Chris（克里斯）：
Oh, so you're not in your own head as much.
哦，所以你不会那么深陷在自己脑子里。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Correct. You're not in your own head, and you might even be laughing at the student, or you might be agreeing with the teacher, or vice versa, or sympathizing with the student. But because you are a passive observer, you can be more objective about it, and you aren't threatened or fearful, and you can learn better. And coming back to the original point of "you can't change people": if you do want to change someone's behavior, I think the only effective way to do it is to compliment them when they do something you want—not to insult them or be negative or critical when they do something you don't want. And we can't help it; it's obviously in our nature to criticize, and I do it as well. But it just reminds me that, like, when somebody does something praiseworthy, don't forget to praise them. Definitely go out of your way—and it'll be genuine, it has to be genuine, it can't be a fake thing. This is not one of those "just dropping compliments" type things; eventually people will see through that—they want authenticity. But just don't forget to praise people when they do something praiseworthy, and you'll get more of that behavior.
没错。你不在自己脑子里打转，你甚至可能在笑那个学生，或者认同老师、反过来认同学生，或者同情那个学生。但因为你是个被动的旁观者，你能更客观地看待它，你不会感到威胁或恐惧，于是你能学得更好。回到最初那个"你改变不了别人"的论点：如果你确实想改变某人的行为，我认为唯一有效的办法，就是在他做了你想要的事时去赞美他——而不是在他做了你不想要的事时去羞辱、否定或批评他。我们也忍不住——批评显然是我们的天性，我自己也会这么做。但它提醒我，当有人做了值得称赞的事时，别忘了去称赞他。一定要特意去做——而且要发自真心，必须是真心的，不能是假的。这可不是那种"逢人就撒赞美"的把戏；人们终究会看穿，他们要的是真诚。但只要记得在别人做了值得称赞的事时去赞美他，你就会得到更多那样的行为。

关系 · 别爱上对方的"简历"，真爱是难以言说的合一

Chris（克里斯）：
There was a really famous thread on Reddit about five questions to ask yourself if you're uncertain about your relationship. One of the questions was: "Are you truly in love with your partner, or just their potential, or the idea of them?" And that's, you know, "they show such great promise, look at their ability for change and growth, they're on the right path."
Reddit 上有个很有名的帖子，列了五个当你对自己的感情没把握时该问自己的问题。其中一个问题是："你是真的爱你的伴侣，还是只爱他们的潜力，或者你心中关于他们的那个想象？"也就是那种"他们前途无量，看看他们改变和成长的能力，他们走在正确的道路上"。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
The partner-matching thing is so hard. When people come and ask me, like, "Oh, should I be with this person?"—well, if you're asking me, the answer is clearly no, right? Because you wouldn't have to ask if you were with the right person. Or when you ask someone why they're in a relationship with somebody, and they start reading out his or her résumé—right, that's also a bad sign.
找对象这件事太难了。当有人跑来问我，"哦，我该不该和这个人在一起？"——好吧，如果你来问我，那答案显然是"不该"，对吧？因为如果你跟对的人在一起，你根本不需要问。又或者，当你问某人为什么和某人在一起，而对方开始念那个人的"简历"——对，那也是个坏兆头。
Chris（克里斯）：
So what do you mean?
你这话什么意思？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Oh, it's like, "We have so much in common, we like to golf together"—it's not a basis for a relationship. Or, "She's a ballerina," or "He went to Harvard," or what have you—this is a résumé item, so that's not who the person actually is.
哦，就像"我们有好多共同点，我们喜欢一起打高尔夫"——这不是一段关系的基础。或者"她是芭蕾舞演员""他上过哈佛"之类的——这都是"简历条目"，并不是这个人真正是谁。
Chris（克里斯）：
What's a better answer?
那什么才是更好的答案？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
"I just love being with this person. I just trust them. I enjoy being around
"我就是喜欢和这个人在一起。我就是信任他们。我享受待在……

合一之爱 · 真爱是意识与意识相遇，源于人对"合一"的渴望

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
…them. I love how capable he is. I love how kind she is. I love her spirit. I love his energy." The more materially and concretely definable the reasons are that you're together, the worse they are. The ineffable is actually where the sort of true love lies. Because real love is a form of unity. It's a form of connection. It's connecting spirits—"Oh, you too?" My consciousness meets your consciousness. The underlying drive in love, in art, in science, in mysticism, is the desire for unity. It's the desire for connection. As Borges famously wrote, in every human there's a sense that something infinite has been lost. There's a God-shaped hole in you that you're trying to fill. And so we're always trying to find that connection. Love is trying to find it in one other person and saying, "Okay, you're male, I'm female," or whatever—whatever your predilections are—"and now we connect, now we form a whole, a connected whole." Or in mysticism, it's all about, "Okay, sit down, meditate, and you'll feel the whole." In science, it's like, "Oh, atoms bouncing is mechanics, but that generates heat, so thermodynamics," and motion, or kinetics, are one combined theory—that's a whole. Electricity and magnetism are one thing—that's the whole, [and it] creates that sense of awe. In art, it's like, "I feel an emotion, I create a piece of art around it," and then you see that painting, or you see the Sistine Chapel, or you read the poem, and you feel that emotion. So again, it's creating unity, it's creating connection. And I think everybody craves that. And so when you really love somebody, it's because you feel a sense of wholeness by being around them. And that sense of wholeness probably doesn't have anything to do with what school they went to, or what career they're in.
……他们身边。我爱他有多能干。我爱她有多善良。我爱她的精神气。我爱他的能量。"你们在一起的理由越是物质化、越是具体可定义，它们就越糟糕。真正的爱，其实恰恰存在于那不可言说之处（the ineffable）。因为真爱是一种合一（unity），是一种连结（connection）。它是精神与精神的相连——"哦，你也是？"我的意识遇见你的意识。爱、艺术、科学、神秘主义背后那共同的驱动力，就是对合一的渴望，对连结的渴望。正如博尔赫斯（Borges）有句名言：每个人心里都有一种感觉，仿佛某种无限的东西已经失落了。你心里有一个上帝形状的空洞，你一直想把它填满。所以我们总在寻找那种连结。爱，是想在另一个人身上找到它，说："好，你是男的，我是女的"，或者随便什么——不管你的偏好如何——"现在我们连结起来，现在我们合成一个整体，一个相连的整体。"而在神秘主义里，核心就是："好，坐下来，冥想，你就会感受到那个整体。"在科学里，则像是"哦，原子碰撞是力学，但它会产生热，于是有了热力学"，而运动、动力学，是一套合并起来的理论——这就是一个整体。电和磁是同一回事——这就是那个整体，它带来那种敬畏感。在艺术里，就像"我感到一种情绪，我围绕它创作一件作品"，然后你看到那幅画、看到西斯廷礼拜堂（the Sistine Chapel）、读到那首诗，你也感受到那种情绪。所以归根结底，这又是在创造合一、创造连结。我想每个人都渴望那个。所以当你真正爱一个人时，是因为你在他身边感到一种圆满感（wholeness）。而那种圆满感，多半跟他们上过哪所学校、从事什么职业毫无关系。
Chris（克里斯）：
Just sort of tying that into "life is short, stop fucking about": if you're faced with a difficult choice and you cannot decide,
把这个稍微接回到"人生苦短，别瞎折腾"那点上：如果你面临一个艰难的选择，迟迟无法决定，

决策 · 该走的三件大事与三条决策启发式

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
The answer is no. And the reason is modern society is full of options. Knowing this rationally sounds great, but having the courage to commit to it in reality, I think, is a different task. And cutting your losses quickly in the big three—relationships, jobs, and locations—is hard.
答案是「不」。原因在于现代社会选项太多。理性上知道这个道理听起来很棒，但要在现实中拿出勇气去践行，我觉得是另一回事。而在三件大事——关系、工作、所在地——上迅速止损，是很难的。
Chris（克里斯）：
What would you say to someone who may cerebrally be able to agree with you and say, "I understand the answer"? My cousin said this about me. He said what he's really noticed about me is my ability to walk away from situations that are just not great enough or not good enough for me.
对那种在理智上能认同你、会说「我懂这个答案」的人，你会怎么说？我表弟就这样说过我。他说他真正注意到我身上的一点，是我有能力从那些对我来说还不够好、不够棒的处境里抽身离开。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yes. And I think that is a characteristic that I have—I will not accept second-best outcomes in my life. Ultimately you will end up wherever is acceptable to you; you will get out of life whatever is acceptable to you. And there are certain things to me that are very, very important where I will not settle for second best. But then there are a lot of other things I just don't care about, because if I spend all my time caring about those things, I don't have the energy for the few things that matter. And so in decision-making, I have a few heuristics for myself. Other people can use their own, but mine are: if you can't decide, the answer is no. If you're offered an opportunity, if you have a new thing that you're saying yes or no to that is a change from where you're starting, the answer is by default always no. Secondly, if you have two decisions—if you have A or B and both seem very equal—take the path that's more painful in the short term, the one that's going to be painful immediately, because your brain is always trying to avoid pain. Any pain that is imminent, it is going to treat as much larger than it actually is. This is kind of like a decision-making equivalent of Taleb's surgeon—Taleb's surgeon, where the surgeon who doesn't look as good is more likely
对。我觉得这就是我身上的一个特质——我不接受人生里的次优结果。归根结底，你最终会停在你能接受的地方；你这辈子能得到什么，取决于你愿意接受什么。对我来说有些事极其极其重要，我绝不退而求其次。但还有很多别的事我根本不在乎，因为如果我把所有时间都花在在乎那些事上，就没有精力留给真正要紧的少数几件事。所以在做决策时，我给自己定了几条启发式（heuristics）。别人可以用自己的，但我的是：第一，如果你拿不定主意，答案就是「不」。如果有人给你一个机会，如果你面前有一个要选「是」还是「否」的新事物、它会让你偏离当下的起点，那么默认答案永远是「不」。第二，如果你有两个选择——A 或 B、两者看起来势均力敌——那就选短期内更痛苦的那条路，就是马上会让你难受的那条，因为你的大脑总在逃避痛苦。任何迫在眉睫的痛苦，它都会当成比实际更巨大。这有点像决策版的塔勒布的外科医生（Taleb's surgeon）——那个看上去不太体面的外科医生，反而更可能
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
to be a good surgeon.
是个好医生。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah.
对。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It's similar in that appearances are deceiving, because you're avoiding conflict, you're avoiding pain. So take the path that's more painful in the short term, because your brain is creating this illusion that the short-term pain is greater than the long-term pain.
道理类似——表象会骗人，因为你在逃避冲突、逃避痛苦。所以选短期更痛的那条路，因为你的大脑在制造一种错觉，让短期痛苦显得比长期痛苦还大。
Chris（克里斯）：
Because long-term, yeah, you'll commit your future self to all kinds of long-term...
因为从长期看，对，你会让未来的自己背上各种长期的……
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Exactly. So take the more short-term painful one. And then finally, the last one, which I would try to couple Gupta with, is that you want to take the choice that will leave you more equanimous in the long term.
没错。所以选短期更痛的那个。最后还有一条，我会把它和 Gupta（存疑，疑为人名或概念）连在一起讲：你要选那个长期来看让你更心平气和（equanimous）的选项。
Chris（克里斯）：
By "equanimous" he means like more peace, more mental peace in the long term.
他说的「equanimous」是指长期更平静、内心更安宁。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So whatever clears your mind more and will have you having less self-talk in the future—if you can model that out, that is probably the better route to go. And then I would focus decision-making down on the three things that really matter, because everything else is downstream of these three decisions, especially these early-life decisions. Later in life, you have different things to optimize for, but early in life you're trying to figure out who you're with, what you're doing, and where you live. And I think on all three of those you want to think pretty hard about it. And people do some of these unconsciously. You know, who you're with—very often it's like, "Well, we were in a relationship, we stumbled along, it felt okay, it had been enough time, so we got married." Right? Not great reasons. Maybe not terrible reasons either. I mean, people who overthink these things sometimes don't get the right answer. But maybe here, if you are the kind of person that's not going to settle for second best, you iterate. You iterate on a closed time frame so you don't run out the clock, and then you decide. On what you do, you try a whole bunch of different things until you find the one that feels like play to you, looks like work to others. You can't lose at it. Get some leverage, try to find some practical application of it, and go into that. And then where you live—where you live is really important. I don't think people spend enough time on that one. I think people pick cities randomly based on "where I went to school" or "where my family happened to be" or "where my friend was" or "I visited one weekend, I really liked it." You really want to think it through, because where you live really constrains and
所以凡是能让你头脑更清明、未来内心独白更少的选项——如果你能把它推演出来，那大概就是更好的路。然后我会把决策聚焦到真正要紧的三件事上，因为其他一切都是这三个决策的下游，尤其是这些人生早期的决策。到了人生后期，你要优化的东西不一样；但在早期，你要弄清楚的是和谁在一起、做什么、住在哪。这三件事我觉得都得好好想清楚。而人们有些是无意识地在做。比如和谁在一起——常常是「我们处了一段，磕磕绊绊就这么过来了，感觉还行，时间也够久了，于是就结婚了」。对吧？算不上好理由，但也未必是糟糕的理由。我是说，把这些事想太多的人有时反而得不到对的答案。不过在这件事上，如果你是那种不肯将就次优的人，你就会去迭代。你在一个封闭的时间框架内迭代，免得把时间耗光，然后做决定。至于做什么，你就多尝试各种不同的事，直到找到对你而言像玩、在别人看来却像工作的那件事。你在那件事上不会输。再加上一点杠杆（leverage），试着找到它的某种实际应用，然后投身进去。然后是住在哪——住在哪真的很重要。我觉得人们在这件事上花的心思不够。人们挑城市挑得很随意，无非是「我在哪上的学」「我家碰巧在哪」「我朋友在哪」「我有个周末去过、挺喜欢」。你真得把它想透，因为你住在哪会实实在在地限制并

关系 · 住在哪、基因与价值观

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
defines your opportunities. It's going to determine your friend circle, it's going to determine your dating pool, it's going to determine your job opportunities, it's going to determine the food and air and water quality that you receive, it's going to determine your proximity to your family—which might be important as you get older and have kids. So a very, very, very important decision—you know, quality of life, how much you stay inside or outside, and how long you'll live based on that. And I think people choose that one probably more poorly—they put a lot less thought into that one than the other two.
决定你的机会。它会决定你的朋友圈，会决定你的约会人选池，会决定你的工作机会，会决定你能享受到的食物、空气和水的质量，会决定你离家人的远近——这一点在你年纪渐长、有了孩子后可能很重要。所以这是一个极其极其极其重要的决定——关系到生活质量、你待在室内还是室外的多少，乃至你能活多久。可我觉得人们恰恰在这件事上选得更糟——他们在这上面花的心思远不如另外两件。
Chris（克里斯）：
In some ways, yeah. But also—you're so right—how many people fall backward into a relationship and, before they know it, "We're living together, we got a daughter, we got a kid, we were married."
某种程度上，对。但还有——你说得太对了——有多少人是稀里糊涂跌进一段关系里，还没回过神就「我们同居了、有了个女儿、有了孩子、结了婚」。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. And then when you have kids—because then that's half of you and half of them running around—you're never going to separate yourself from that. So once you have a child with somebody, then the most important thing in the world to you is half that other person, whether you like them or not.
对。而一旦你有了孩子——因为那是一半的你和一半的对方在跑来跑去——你就再也甩不掉这层关系了。所以一旦你和某人有了孩子，这世上对你最重要的东西，就有一半是那个人，不管你喜不喜欢他。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah. Jeffrey Miller had a tweet a long time ago that I always think about. He said every parenting book in the world could be replaced with one book on behavioral genetics.
对。杰弗里·米勒（Jeffrey Miller）很久以前发过一条推文，我一直记着。他说全世界所有的育儿书，都可以用一本讲行为遗传学（behavioral genetics）的书来取代。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I'm a big believer in genetics, yes. I do think a lot of behavior is downstream of genetics, and I think we underplay that. We like to overplay nurture and underplay nature for societal reasons. But nature is a big deal. The temperament of the person you marry is probably going to be reflected in your child by default. People can change. A securely attached kid—pick a securely attached partner. Well, the secret to a happy relationship is two happy people, right? So I would say if you want to be happy, then be with a happy person. Don't think you're going to be with someone who's unhappy and then make them happy down the road. Or if you're okay with them being unhappy but there are other things you like about them, that's fine. But this goes back to not trying to change—compensate for their unhappiness with other things. And actually, we talked a little bit about how people do connect successfully on spirit and those things, but that's maybe a little too abstract. If you want to get a little more practical, it could be based on values. And values are a set of things you won't compromise on.
我很信基因，是的。我确实认为很多行为都是基因的下游产物，而我们低估了这一点。出于社会层面的原因，我们爱夸大后天教养（nurture）、贬低先天天性（nature）。可天性才是大事。你娶（嫁）的那个人的脾性，默认很可能会在你孩子身上体现出来。人是能改变的。想要一个安全型依恋的孩子，就挑一个安全型依恋的伴侣。幸福关系的秘诀，就是两个幸福的人，对吧？所以我会说，你想幸福，就和一个幸福的人在一起。别以为你能和一个不幸福的人在一起、然后把对方变幸福。或者，如果你能接受对方不幸福、但你喜欢他身上别的东西，那也行。但这又回到那一点：别试图改变——别想用别的东西去弥补对方的不幸福。其实我们之前稍微聊过，人也确实能在精神（spirit）这类层面上成功地连结，但那也许太抽象了。要更实际一点，可以基于价值观（values）。而价值观，就是一套你不肯妥协的东西。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Values are the tough decisions of: "Oh, my parent got sick—do they move in with us, or do we put them in a nursing home?" "Do we give the children money, or do we not?" "Do we move across the country to be closer to our family, or do we stay put where we are?" "Do we argue about politics? Do we care, or do we not?" Right? Values are way more important than checklist items. And I think if people were to align much more on their values, they would have much more successful relationships.
价值观就是那些难做的决定：「哎，我父母病了——是搬来跟我们住，还是把他们送养老院？」「我们要不要给孩子钱？」「我们要不要搬到全国另一头去离家人近些，还是就守在原地？」「我们要不要为政治争吵？我们在不在乎这个？」对吧？价值观远比那些清单条目重要。我觉得如果人们能在价值观上对齐得更多，他们的关系会成功得多。

变化 · 损失厌恶、归零重来与别太把自己当回事

Chris（克里斯）：
The emotional pain of fearing change. I have this thing—the job, the location, the partner—I'm going to enter or not enter this thing; for the most part, it's leaving. I think we have this sort of loss aversion that we really feel—evolved loss aversion. It's just painful, separating yourself in front of your friends. It's embarrassing. How would you advise people to get past themselves with that loss aversion, that fear of change? "Oh my God, am I going to...?"
害怕改变带来的那种情绪上的痛苦。我面对这件事——工作、所在地、伴侣——要不要踏进去或不踏进去；而大多数时候，难的其实是离开。我觉得我们身上有一种真切能感受到的损失厌恶（loss aversion）——进化而来的损失厌恶。当着朋友的面把自己抽离出来，就是很痛苦、很难堪。你会怎么建议人们越过自己心里那道坎，越过那种损失厌恶、那种对改变的恐惧？「天哪，我真要……吗？」
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Oh my God, it's the hardest thing in the world, starting over. It's back to the zero-to-one thing. It's the mountain-climbing thing. You're not going to find your path to the top of the mountain in the first go-around. Sometimes you go up there, you get stuck, and you come back down. And the difference between all the successful people and the ones who are not is: the ones who are successful wanted it so badly they're willing to go back and start over again and again, whether in their career or in their relationships or in anything else. The more seriously you take yourself, the unhappier you're going to be.
天哪，重新开始是世上最难的事。这又回到「从零到一」（zero to one）那回事，回到爬山那回事。你不可能第一次就找到通往山顶的路。有时你爬上去，卡住了，又退回来。而所有成功者和不成功者的区别在于：成功的人太想要了，以至于愿意一次又一次回头重来——无论在事业上、关系上还是别的任何事上。你越把自己当回事，你就越不快乐。
Chris（克里斯）：
You learned how to take yourself less seriously?
你学会了别太把自己当回事？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Well, fame doesn't help on that one, because that is one of the traps of fame. People are always talking about you, they have a certain view of you, and you start believing that, and then you take yourself seriously, and then that limits your own actions. You can't look like a fool anymore, you can't do new things anymore. You know, tomorrow I announce I'm a breakdancer, right—that's going to be met with a lot of scorn and ridicule. But what if I want to be a breakdancer?
唉，名气在这件事上帮不上忙，因为这正是名气的陷阱之一。人们老在议论你，对你有某种看法，你开始信了那一套，于是你就把自己当回事了，而这反过来限制了你自己的行动。你不能再出洋相了，不能再做新事情了。比如明天我宣布我要当个霹雳舞者（breakdancer），对吧——那肯定招来一堆嘲讽和奚落。可如果我就是想当个霹雳舞者呢？
Chris（克里斯）：
I'd back you if you want to make that, yeah.
你要想去搞这个，我支持你，真的。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
The truth is, if I wanted to be a breakdancer, I'd be breakdancing. But, you know, like, I'm starting a new company—zero to one again, from scratch. Let's do it, you
实话说，我要真想当霹雳舞者，我早就在跳了。不过呢，比如说，我正在创办一家新公司——又一次从零到一、从头来过。来吧，
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
know, one more time. And not just going and raising a big VC fund, or retiring, or what have you—but that's because I want to build the product, I want to see it exist. So I think that you constantly just have to force yourself, you have to remind yourself: look, deep down you're still the same Chris you were when you were nine years old. Deep down you're still a kid. You're still curious about the world, you still have a lot of the same predilections and desires and wants—you've got a nice veneer on it. But one of the nice things when you have kids is you realize how much closer they are to you in personality and knowledge and know-how. Like, I look at my son who's, you know, eight, and I just noticed, like, wow, he probably has 60 to 80 percent of my knowledge and development and wisdom, and he has a lot more freedom and he has a lot more spontaneity. In some ways he's smarter, and there's not a big gap left here to close. This kid's going to be, you know, done very soon—and it caught up to me. And so, to the extent that I think I know better, or that I'm somewhere, or that I'm someone, it's just an illusion. It's just a belief.
再来一次。不是去募一支大型风投基金（VC fund），也不是退休或诸如此类——之所以做，是因为我想造出那个产品，我想亲眼看到它存在。所以我觉得你得不断地逼自己、提醒自己：瞧，骨子里你还是九岁时那个克里斯。骨子里你还是个孩子。你依旧对世界好奇，依旧有很多一样的偏好、欲望和渴求——只不过外面包了一层好看的漆。但有了孩子的好处之一，就是你会意识到他们在性格、知识和门道上离你有多近。比如我看着我那个八岁的儿子，我突然注意到，哇，他大概已经有了我六到八成的知识、成长和智慧，而且他自由得多、自发得多。某些方面他更聪明，剩下要补的差距已经不大了。这孩子很快就要「追平」了——他已经赶上我了。所以，凡是我自以为更懂、自以为身居某处、自以为是个人物的地方，那都不过是一种错觉，只是一个念头。
Chris（克里斯）：
What's the lineage between that and taking yourself too seriously?
这一点跟「太把自己当回事」之间，有什么传承（脉络）关系？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I shouldn't take myself too seriously, because there's nothing here to take that seriously. And if I take myself too seriously, then I'm going to get trapped—I'm going to circumscribe myself again into a limited set of behaviors and outcomes that keep me from being free, keep me from being spontaneous, keep me from being happy. So it just goes back to: don't think about yourself too much.
我不该太把自己当回事，因为这里根本没什么值得那么当回事的东西。而如果我太把自己当回事，我就会被困住——我会再次把自己圈进一套有限的行为和结果里，让我没法自由、没法自发、没法快乐。所以归根结底就是：别太想着自己。

智慧 · 无法言传的功课与「我是例外」

Chris（克里斯）：
There's a special type of pain in realizing that the advice you need to hear right now is something that almost always you learned a long time ago—and that, you know, you're basically the same person you were when you were nine. And a lot of the time people ask questions like "What advice do you wish you would give yourself 10 years ago?" People ask themselves that question. Almost invariably, the advice you would give yourself 10 years ago is still the advice you need to hear today.
有一种特别的痛，是当你意识到：你此刻最需要听的忠告，几乎总是你很久以前就学过的东西——而且，你基本上还是九岁时的那个人。很多时候人们会问「你希望十年前会给自己什么忠告？」人们这样问自己。而几乎无一例外，你十年前会给自己的那条忠告，恰恰也是你今天仍需要听的那条。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Absolutely.
完全正确。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's why I did that exercise of thinking back, you know, 10 years, 20 years, 30 years ago—what advice would I give myself? For me it's just: be less emotional, don't take everything so seriously. Do the same things, but do them without all the emotional turbulence. And so that's the advice I'm giving myself going forward.
所以我才做了那个回望的练习，回望十年、二十年、三十年前——我会给自己什么忠告？对我来说就是：少点情绪，别什么都看得那么重。做同样的事，但去掉那些情绪上的动荡。所以这也是我接下来要给自己的忠告。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah.
嗯。
Chris（克里斯）：
It's funny how we need that distance to be able to be a little bit more objective, to have a little bit more perspective. And it's almost a little bit of a trick, right? Because typically when you do that, you say, "What would you tell a friend that was going through this?" and then you try and turn the advice to the friend around onto yourself. You always think, "Well, I'm not the friend." You 10 years ago—there's enough distance in that. "Oh, I actually am still that person. There's just a single line between that and me."
很有意思，我们居然需要那段距离，才能稍微客观一点、稍微多一点视角。而这几乎像个小把戏，对吧？因为通常你这么做时，你会说「如果是朋友经历这事，你会怎么跟他说？」然后你试着把给朋友的忠告反过来用在自己身上。你总会想「可我又不是那个朋友」。但十年前的你——这里就有了足够的距离。「哦，原来我其实还是那个人，那个人和我之间只隔着一条线而已。」
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And related to this story is: I think understanding is way more important than discipline. Now, Jocko would have a fit, but on physical things, discipline is important. If I want to build a good body, I've got to work out on a regular basis. But on mental things, I think understanding is way more important. Once you see the truth of something, you cannot unsee it. All of us have had experiences where we've seen a behavior in a person, and then it just changes what we think about that person—we no longer want to be friends with them, or we deeply respect them if it was, you know, really good behavior that maybe was observed unintentionally. So when we really do see something clearly, it changes our behavior immediately, and that is far more efficient than trying to change your behavior through repetition.
跟这个说法相关的是：我认为理解（understanding）远比纪律（discipline）重要。乔可（Jocko，指 Jocko Willink）大概会跳脚，但在身体层面，纪律很重要。我想练出好身材，就得规律地锻炼。可在心智层面，我觉得理解重要得多。你一旦看清了某件事的真相，就再也无法「看不见」它了。我们都有过这样的经历：在一个人身上看到某种行为，然后它就彻底改变了我们对这个人的看法——我们不再想跟他做朋友；或者，要是无意中看到的是非常好的行为，我们就会深深敬重他。所以当我们真正看清一件事时，它会立刻改变我们的行为，而这比靠重复去改变行为高效得多。
Chris（克里斯）：
Could you give me an example?
能给我举个例子吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Let's say you have a friend, and that person turns out to be a thief. You see that person stealing something—you're done with them. The smoking lung-cancer example is a good one, right? Or anytime someone close to you dies, or you even hear about someone dying—you hear about someone—what's the first thing you do? The first thing—assuming you weren't that close to them—
假设你有个朋友，结果那人是个贼。你亲眼看到他偷东西——你跟他就到此为止了。抽烟与肺癌那个例子也很贴切，对吧？再比如，任何时候你身边亲近的人去世了，甚至只是听说某人死了——你听说某人死了——你做的第一件事是什么？第一件事——假设你跟他没那么亲近——
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
obviously if you're close to them it's different. But if you weren't that close to them—you know, you hear about someone in your extended social circle dying—you immediately start trying to distinguish yourself from them. You're like, "Oh, well, how old was this person? Did they—were they a smoker? Did they have an issue? Do I have that issue?" Right? You immediately start comparing. And what you're doing there is you're sort of just trying to see if there's an overlap here. But if you see the truth in something—if you're like, "Oh my God, this person was the same age as me, and they died"—and that's starting to happen at my age, where I'm starting to hear about, you know, extended-circle people—it just reminds you time is really short. There's a truth there, a truth there that you cannot actually distance yourself from, a truth there that you cannot unsee. Or, for example—were you into bodybuilding or something back when?
当然，要是你跟他很亲近，那就不一样了。但要是你跟他没那么亲近——比如你听说你外围社交圈里某个人去世了——你会立刻开始试图把自己跟他区分开。你会想「哦，这人多大年纪？他是不是抽烟？他是不是有什么毛病？我有那毛病吗？」对吧？你立刻开始比对。你这么做，其实是在看自己跟他有没有重叠之处。但如果你看到了其中的真相——如果你想「天哪，这人跟我同龄，他却死了」——而这正开始发生在我这个年纪，我开始听到外围圈子里的人离世——它就提醒你时间真的很短。那里有一个真相，一个你其实无法把自己撇清的真相，一个你再也「看不见」不了的真相。再举个例子——你以前是不是搞过健美之类的？
Chris（克里斯）：
Just like bro-lifting stuff.
也就是那种「兄弟撸铁」的玩意儿。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Right. But there probably was a point where you were being really aggro in the gym and you injured yourself.
对。但大概有过那么一刻，你在健身房里特别上头（aggro），结果把自己弄伤了。
Chris（克里斯）：
Many times.
好多次。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Right. And each one of those was a deep understanding of "Don't go beyond this point." There was a truth there. So again, when you see these things in such a way that you can't unsee them, that changes your behavior instantly. And I would argue that that introspection to find those truths is actually very useful.
对。而每一次受伤，都是一次深刻的领悟——「别越过这个界限」。那里有一个真相。所以再说一遍，当你以一种再也无法「看不见」的方式看清这些事，它会即刻改变你的行为。而我会说，那种为了找出这些真相而做的内省，其实非常有用。
Chris（克里斯）：
Is that a justification for more experimentation, exploration, experience in life—sort of trying to find serendipity—because all of these experiences are going to teach you an inescapable lesson?
那这是不是在为「人生里多去实验、多去探索、多去经历」——某种去寻找机缘巧合（serendipity）——找理由？因为所有这些经历都会教给你一个躲不开的功课？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
You're going to do what you're going to do. I mean, your level of exploration, I think, is sort of up to you. But life is always throwing truth back at you. It's about whether you choose to see it, whether you choose to acknowledge it—even if it's painful. Truth is often painful, right? If it wasn't, we'd all be seeing truth all the time. Reality is always reflecting truth—that's all it is.
你该怎么做就会怎么做。我是说，你探索的程度，某种意义上由你自己决定。但生活总在不断把真相抛回给你。关键在于你是否选择去看见它、是否选择去承认它——哪怕它令人痛苦。真相往往是痛苦的，对吧？要不痛苦，我们就会一直在看见真相了。现实总在映照真相——它就只是这么回事。
Chris（克里斯）：
Why would you not have accessed it already?
那你为什么没早就接触到它呢？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Exactly. You know, all the philosophy that's out there, for example—it's almost trite, like most people, until they discover it for themselves. Because wisdom is the
正是如此。比如说，外头所有那些哲学——它几乎是老生常谈，对大多数人来说都是，直到他们自己亲身发现它。因为智慧是
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
set of things that cannot be transmitted. If they could be transmitted, you know, we'd read the same five philosophy books, it would all be done, we'd all be wise. You have to learn it for yourself. It has to be rediscovered for yourself, in your own context. You have to have specific experiences that then allow you to generalize and see the truth in those things in such a way that you're not going to unsee them. But each person is going to see them in a different way. I can tell you the Socrates' story, and it's not going to resonate until there's something that other people desire that you realize you yourself don't want. And the moment that happens, then you'll see the truth in the general statement.
那一套无法被传递的东西。如果它们能被传递，那我们读同样的五本哲学书就完事了，我们就都成智者了。你必须自己去学。它必须在你自己的处境里、由你自己重新发现。你必须拥有一些特定的经历（specific experiences），它们才能让你归纳概括、并以一种再也无法「看不见」的方式看清这些事的真相。但每个人看清它们的方式都会不同。我可以给你讲苏格拉底（Socrates）的故事，可它不会引起你的共鸣，除非有一天，别人渴求的某样东西，你忽然意识到自己根本不想要。那一刻一旦到来，你就会看见那句笼统说法里的真相。
Chris（克里斯）：
I want to just read you a two-minute essay that I wrote a couple of weeks ago. It's called "Unteachable Lessons." I've been thinking about a special category of lesson—one which you cannot discover without experiencing it firsthand. There is a certain subset of advice that, for some reason, we all refuse to learn through instruction. These are unteachable lessons. No matter how arduous or costly or effortful it is going to be for us to find out ourselves, we prefer to disregard the mountains of warnings from our elders, songs, literature, historical catastrophes, public scandals, and instead think some version of "Yeah, that might be true for them, but not for me." We decide to learn the hard lessons the hard way, over and over again. Unfortunately, they all seem to be the big things, too. It's never new insights about how to put up level shelves or charmingly introduce yourself at a cocktail party. Instead, we spend most of our lives learning firsthand the most important lessons that the previous generations already warned us about. Things like: money won't make you happy; fame won't fix your self-worth; you don't love that pretty girl, she's just hot and difficult to get; nothing is as important as you think it is when you're thinking about it; you will regret working too much; worrying is not improving your performance; all your fears are a waste of time; you should see your parents more; you'll be fine after the breakup and will be grateful that you did it; it's perfectly okay to cut toxic people out of your life. And even reading this list back, I'm rolling my eyes at how fucking trite it is.
我想给你读一篇我几周前写的两分钟短文。题目叫《无法言传的功课》（Unteachable Lessons）。我一直在琢磨一类特别的功课——一种不亲身经历就无法发现的功课。有那么一类忠告，不知为何，我们都拒绝通过别人的教导去学会。这就是无法言传的功课。不管亲自去弄明白要付出多艰难、多高昂、多费劲的代价，我们都宁愿无视来自长辈、歌曲、文学、历史灾难、公众丑闻的如山警告，转而想着某种版本的「嗯，那对他们也许成立，但对我不然」。我们一次又一次地决定用最难的方式去学那些最难的功课。不幸的是，它们似乎全是大事。从来不是关于怎么把架子装平、怎么在鸡尾酒会上风度翩翩地自我介绍这类新洞见。相反，我们这辈子大部分时间，都在亲身去学那些前几代人早就警告过我们的、最重要的功课。比如：钱不会让你快乐；名气修不好你的自我价值感；你并不爱那个漂亮姑娘，她只是又辣又难追；当你正惦记某件事时，没有什么事真有你以为的那么重要；你会后悔工作太拼；担忧并没有提升你的表现；你所有的恐惧都是在浪费时间；你该多去看看父母；分手后你会没事的，而且会庆幸自己分了；把有毒的人从你生活里清出去完全没问题。可即便我把这份清单再读一遍，我也在翻白眼，嫌它老套（trite）得他妈要命。
Chris（克里斯）：
These are all basic-bitch obvious insights that everybody has heard before. But if they're so basic, why does everyone so reliably fall prey to them throughout our lives? And if they're so obvious, why do people who have recently become famous or wealthy or lost a parent or gone through a breakup start to proclaim these facts with the renewed grandiose ceremony of someone who's just gone through religious revelation? It's also a very contentious list of points to say on the internet. If you interview a billionaire who says that all of his money didn't make him happy, or a movie star who says that her fame felt like a prison, the internet will tear them apart for being ungrateful and out of touch. So not only do we refuse to learn these lessons, we even refuse to hear the message from those warning us about them. And even more than that, I think for every one of these, if I consider a bit deeper, I can recall a time—including right now—where I convinced myself that I am the exception to the rule. That my particular mental makeup, or life situation, or historical wounds, or dreams for the future render me immune to these lessons being applicable. "No, no, no, my inner landscape would be solved by skirting around the most well-known wisdom of the ages. No, no, no, I can thread this needle properly. Watch me dance through the minefield and avoid all of the tripwires that everyone else kicks." And then you kick one, and you share a knowing look—the kind that can only occur between two people who've been hurt in the exact same way. And a voice in the back of your mind will say, "I told you so." That's an unteachable lesson.
这些都是「俗到掉渣」、人人早就听过的明摆着的道理。可它们要真这么基本，为什么我们一辈子又都这么稳定地栽在上面？它们要真这么明摆着，为什么那些刚成名、刚发财、刚失去至亲、刚经历分手的人，会带着像刚经历宗教顿悟般又一次的隆重仪式感去宣讲这些事实？这也是一份在网上说出来会非常招骂的清单。如果你采访一个亿万富翁，他说他所有的钱都没让他快乐；或者一个电影明星，她说她的名气像座牢笼——网民会把他们撕碎，骂他们身在福中不知福、不食人间烟火。所以我们不光拒绝学这些功课，连那些警告我们的人传来的讯息，我们都拒绝去听。更甚于此，我觉得这每一条，只要我再往深想一点，我都能想起某个时刻——包括此刻——我说服自己「我是规律的例外」。说服自己：我那独特的心理构成、人生处境、过往伤痕，或对未来的梦想，让我对这些功课免疫、它们不适用于我。「不不不，我内心的困局，靠绕开这世上最广为人知的古老智慧就能解决。不不不，我能稳稳地穿过这根针眼。瞧我跳着舞穿过雷区，避开所有别人都踩到的绊线。」然后你踩中了一根，于是你和对方交换一个心照不宣的眼神——那种只会发生在两个以一模一样的方式受过伤的人之间的眼神。而你脑后会有个声音说：「我早跟你说过吧。」这就是一堂无法言传的功课。

语境 · 智慧高度依赖语境与「重新言说」

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It's a good essay. I think one of the reasons why these lessons are unteachable is because they're too broad—they have to be applied in context. A number of the ones that you laid out contradict each other, like "spend more time with your parents" and "don't work so hard." But at the same time, you do want to be successful. I think a lot of these lessons come down from on high, from—as you said—like the famous movie star or the billionaire saying "Oh, you don't need to be happy." It's like, "Well, okay, then give it up, bitch." In reality, I think many of these
这是篇好文章。我觉得这些功课之所以无法言传，原因之一是它们太宽泛了——它们必须放进具体语境里去应用。你列出的好几条彼此矛盾，比如「多花时间陪父母」和「别那么拼命工作」。可与此同时，你又确实想成功。我觉得很多这类功课是从高处俯传下来的，来自——就像你说的——名声在外的电影明星或亿万富翁说「哦，你不需要快乐」。这就让人想说「行啊，那你把它让出来啊，贱人。」现实里，我觉得这些当中有很多
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
contradict each other. It's like, if you went to school and you just studied philosophy for four years, you would not know how to live life, because you wouldn't know which philosophical doctrine to apply in which circumstance. You have to actually live life, go through all of the issues, to figure out what it is that you want—what's the context in which some of these things apply and some of them don't. Yes, you want to visit your parents more often, but you don't want to live with your parents, and you don't want to necessarily see them every day or every weekend—depending on the parent, you might not get along with one of them. So I think it is highly contextual. That said, I would argue that once you figure it out for yourself, you can kind of carve these variations on these maxims that apply to you, and then you'll have a specific experience that helps you remember it and actually execute on it. And you can also phrase it in a way where it's not trite anymore. So, like, my personal—yeah, so a lot of my maxims and notes-to-self are carved in a way that they're modernized. They're saying something true, which might be trite if I didn't say it in a new way, or in an interesting way that is more relevant to me today. There was a Nobel Prize winner who said something to the effect of, "Everything worth saying may have been said before, but given that nobody was listening, it must be said again."
是彼此矛盾的。这就好比，如果你上学只学了四年哲学，你照样不会过日子，因为你不知道在哪种处境下该套用哪条哲学教义。你必须真去过日子、把所有难题都趟一遍，才能搞清楚你到底想要什么——搞清楚在什么语境里某些道理成立、某些不成立。是的，你想更常去看父母，但你并不想跟父母同住，也未必想天天或每个周末都见他们——视父母而定，你可能跟其中一位还合不来。所以我觉得这高度依赖语境（contextual）。话虽如此，我会说，一旦你为自己琢磨明白了，你就能把这些格言（maxims）刻出适用于你自己的变体，然后你会有一段特定的经历帮你记住它、并真正把它执行出来。而你还能用一种不再老套的方式把它说出来。所以，像我个人的——对，我很多格言和写给自己的笔记，都被我刻成了一种现代化的样子。它们说的是某个真理，而这真理若不被我用一种新颖的、或者对今天的我更有意义的有趣方式说出来，可能就显得老套。有位诺贝尔奖得主说过大意如此的话：「值得说的话或许都已经被人说过了，但鉴于当时没人在听，它就必须再说一遍。」
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, it has to be said again, has to be recontextualized for the modern age. Things do change—technology changes things, culture changes, people change. On that—I've heard you say—you talk about the difference between seeming wise and being wise: that you tried to appear smart as a kid by sort of rote memorization, masquerading as insight and wisdom. And I certainly feel that. A lot of the show, for me, I think has been—was, and still is—a redemption arc from this decade of my life where I completely suppressed any intellectual curiosity. It's like, "Okay, I'll be a professional party boy for 10 years, stand on the front door of a nightclub and give out VIP wristbands and have access to all of the pretty girls or, you know, the cool parties," or whatever it might be.
对，它必须再说一遍，必须为现代重新放进语境。事物确实在变——技术改变事物，文化在变，人也在变。说到这个——我听你讲过——你谈到「看起来有智慧」和「真有智慧」的区别：说你小时候靠死记硬背（rote memorization）来显得聪明，把它伪装成洞见和智慧。这我深有体会。对我来说，这档节目很大程度上一直是——曾经是、现在仍是——一段救赎弧线，来弥补我人生中那十年——那十年我彻底压抑了自己一切的求知欲。那感觉就像「行吧，我就当十年职业派对男孩，守在夜店门口发 VIP 手环，能接触到所有漂亮姑娘，或者那些很酷的派对」，诸如此类。

表演与真实 · 播客脑：我们都在「表演自己」

Chris（克里斯）：
Seems like it worked out okay. It did in some ways. I mean, it's not fun. It was—look, it was a good way to spend my 20s. But to sort of come back, put your head above water, two degrees, one of which was a master's, and then just shut down any of that learning—I mean, I did that while I was at uni. While I was at uni, I was running the events. So it was actually a decade and a half. And I think there was a big redemption arc within this show. And I constantly have to kind of wipe the slime off me of this sense that I need to prove myself. And so much of it—it's why it really resonates with me. When you're memorizing things, it indicates that you don't understand them—that sort of rote memorization and regurgitation masquerading as wisdom. Because people use fluency as a proxy for truthfulness and insight. They use the complexity of your language and your communication.
看起来结果还不错。某些方面确实如此。我是说，那并不有趣。当年——你瞧，那是度过我二十多岁的好方式。但要重新冒头、把脑袋探出水面，拿两个学位、其中一个是硕士，然后又把那些学到的东西全部搁置——我是说，那是我念大学时干的。读大学时我还在张罗各种活动。所以前后其实折腾了十五年。我觉得这档节目里有一段很大的「救赎弧线」。我得不断地把那种「我需要证明自己」的黏液从身上擦掉。而其中很多东西——这正是它如此打动我的原因。当你在死记硬背时，恰恰说明你并不理解它——那种死记硬背、照本宣科冒充智慧的东西。因为人们把「说得流利」当成「说得真实、有洞见」的替身。他们拿你语言和表达的复杂程度来做评判标准。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. There's a lot of jargon out there. I think it's the mark of a charlatan to explain a simple thing in a complex way. And so when you see people using very complicated language to explain simple things, they're either trying to impress you and obfuscate, or they don't understand it themselves.
是啊，外面满是行话黑话。我认为，把一件简单的事用复杂的方式去解释，正是骗子（charlatan）的标志。所以当你看到有人用极其复杂的语言去解释简单的事，他要么是想唬住你、故意把水搅浑，要么是他自己也没搞懂。
Chris（克里斯）：
But there's an allure in that, though. This was one of the things I had to do when I went to therapy. It's kind of interesting—I think I've talked about this before. I needed to turn off "Podcast Chris" when I stepped into therapy. Because most of the time that I spend one-on-one in a deep conversation that's undistracted throughout the week—I trained myself over, you know, 700 episodes now, 900 and whatever. And I knew what I could say to this therapist, you know, to just sort of veer off a little and create some nice story, put a bow on it, push it across the table and watch your eyes light up a little bit—like a little grin or a self-deprecating joke or whatever. And I'm like: you're not here. You're performing. You're doing this. You're doing the "Chris Williamson thing" with the sort of jazz hands. So I have my own version.
不过那里面是有种诱惑力的。这是我去做心理治疗时不得不面对的一件事。挺有意思的——我想我以前讲过。走进治疗室的那一刻，我得把「播客版克里斯」关掉。因为我一周里大部分时间，都泡在一对一、不受打扰的深度对话里——你知道，到现在七百集、九百多集，我已经把自己训练成这样了。我清楚地知道能对这位治疗师说些什么，稍微岔开一点、编出个漂亮的故事，打上蝴蝶结，推过桌子，然后看着你眼睛一亮——一个小小的咧嘴笑、一句自嘲的玩笑之类。我就想：你根本不在场，你是在表演，你在干这套，你在使出「克里斯·威廉森那一套」、摊着手卖花样。所以我有我自己的那个版本。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Okay, tell me. Okay, so you have "Podcast Chris," I have "Podcast Guest Naval."
好，跟我说说。好——所以你有「播客版克里斯」，我有「播客嘉宾版纳瓦尔」。
Chris（克里斯）：
Precisely.
一点没错。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So very often I'll think of something—I'll have what I think is an insight—and I want to tweet it or write it down, but in my mind I'm talking about it on a podcast. That's kind of how my mind registers it. And for a while I thought this was a bad thing, and I tried to eradicate "Podcast Naval." And then I just realized that's just how it comes out, so I might as well just be okay with it.
所以我经常会想到点什么——觉得自己有了个洞见——想发条推特或记下来，可在我脑子里，我是在某档播客上讲它。我的大脑就是这么登记这件事的。有一阵子我觉得这是坏事，试图把「播客版纳瓦尔」根除掉。后来我才意识到，它本来就是这么冒出来的，那我索性就接受它好了。

为何上这档节目 · 从罗根之后的「金库播客」
