But it's so ludicrous that I should probably do two or three levels of confirmation before I talk about it. But there are definitely some people who believe that —
对，我不是说这百分百是真的——我是读到的……我曾狠狠捏了一下我家孩子的脸颊（来验证），这属于「我在推特上看到的、只做了一级核实」那一类。但它实在太荒谬了，我大概应该做两三级核实之后再拿出来讲。不过确实有些人相信这个——

科学怀疑论 · 半瓶水的「砖家信仰」与SIDS的迷思

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
— enough that it was a thing in certain circles for a while. But I think we just go through these, you know, these IYI beliefs — these "intellectual" beliefs come from people who take a little bit of knowledge and extrapolate it too far. They think we know more than we know, due to recent scientific studies — and these are junk science, these are low-power studies in very certain contexts that then get over-applied. Behavioral psych is very guilty of this, but it's true across a lot of science. So even with science, you have to be skeptical. You have to look very carefully at: does it apply in the right context or not? Does it come from good sources? Did they run enough high-powered studies? Is it widely accepted?
——相信的人足够多，以至于这在某些圈子里风行过一阵。但我觉得我们就是会反复陷入这些 IYI 信念（IYI 即 intellectual yet idiot，「看似有学问的蠢货」）——这些「知识分子式」的信念，来自那些只掌握了一点皮毛却把它过度外推的人。他们因为一些晚近的科学研究，就以为我们懂得比实际更多——而这些是垃圾科学，是在非常特定情境下做的低统计功效（low-power）研究，然后被过度套用。行为心理学在这点上特别有罪，但放眼很多科学领域都如此。所以哪怕面对科学，你也得保持怀疑。你得非常仔细地看：它适用于正确的情境吗？它出自可靠的来源吗？他们做了足够多高功效的研究吗？它被广泛接受吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And there are a whole bunch of things you're just not supposed to talk about. You can't say anything negative about a vaccine because, God forbid, what if they don't get the polio vaccine, right? And that's part of the reason for the recent vaccine debate — because we've taken our worship for vaccines too far, because we don't want people to not take the essential vaccines. So it gets overdone. The same way, there's this whole SIDS thing — sudden infant death syndrome, right? And I was like, no, kids don't suddenly, mysteriously die. More likely there was neglect or there was a problem, and then whoever was the caretaker doesn't want to admit to the problem, or didn't recognize the problem. But kids don't just spontaneously die in the crib, right? So they talk about swaddling babies — you swaddle babies, basically tie them up, mummify them, so you constrict them, [supposedly so] they don't die of SIDS where they roll over and can't get back. It's just all this craziness around child-raising. It's a real minefield.
而且有一大堆事情是你压根不该谈的。你不能说任何关于疫苗的负面话，因为天哪，万一他们不去打脊髓灰质炎（polio）疫苗怎么办，对吧？这也是近来疫苗争论的部分起因——因为我们把对疫苗的崇拜推得太过了，因为我们不希望人们连必要的疫苗都不打。于是就矫枉过正了。同样地，还有 SIDS 这一整套说法——婴儿猝死综合征（sudden infant death syndrome），对吧？我就想，不对，孩子不会突然、莫名其妙地死掉。更可能是有疏忽，或者出了什么问题，然后那个照看人不愿承认问题，或者没意识到问题。但孩子不会就那么在婴儿床里自发地死掉，对吧？于是他们又讲给婴儿打襁褓（swaddling）——把婴儿裹起来，基本上就是捆住、像木乃伊一样缠住，把他们束缚起来，（据说这样）他们就不会因为翻身翻不回来而死于 SIDS。围绕养孩子的就是这一堆疯话。这真是个雷区。
Chris（克里斯）：
It's a minefield.
是个雷区。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And you have these scared parents having a kid for the first time, and they open a book and start reading about how to raise children. I would argue that your natural instincts on what to do with your child are actually pretty good. It's funny — when my wife and I had our first baby, I remember at the hospital... sorry, the first one was a natural birth, at the birthing center. We went home and it was like, "There you go, that's it." And we're like, "What do we do? Where's the instruction manual?" You take him —
然后这些第一次当父母、惊慌失措的人，翻开一本书，开始读该怎么养孩子。我倒要说，你对待孩子该怎么做的天然本能其实相当靠谱。说来好笑——我太太和我有第一个宝宝时，我记得在医院……抱歉，第一个是自然分娩，在生育中心。我们回到家，那感觉就像「好了，就这样」。我们当时想：「我们该干嘛？说明书呢？」你把他抱——

育儿哲学 · 本能很好、孩子也很好：用「解释性理论」代替死记硬背

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
— home, and then you relax and you realize: actually, instincts are pretty good. If the kid cries, check to see [if it's] clean, feed him, all that. Your basic instincts are actually very, very good. And kids' instincts are actually very, very good — they know what they want, and they want things for a reason.
——回家，然后你放松下来，你会意识到：其实本能挺好用的。孩子哭了，就检查一下是不是要换尿布（看看干不干净）、喂喂他，诸如此类。你的基本本能其实非常非常好。而孩子的本能其实也非常非常好——他们知道自己想要什么，而且他们想要某样东西是有理由的。
Chris（克里斯）：
And they can encourage you to give it to them.
而且他们有办法促使你把它给他们。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yes. Children are not deficient adults who can't reason. To some extent that's true, but mostly it's not true. Mostly they have very good reasons for what they want, and you as a parent mostly have communication problems with them. They can't yet communicate to you, and you can't communicate to them. So early on with my kids, I tried to focus on teaching them basic explanatory theories, as opposed to having them memorize things.
没错。孩子并不是「不会推理的、有缺陷的成年人」。某种程度上这话有点道理，但大体上是不对的。大多数时候，他们想要什么是有非常充分的理由的，而你作为家长，大多数时候是跟他们有沟通障碍。他们还不能向你表达，你也没法向他们表达。所以在我孩子很小的时候，我就尽量把重点放在教他们基本的「解释性理论」（explanatory theories）上，而不是让他们死记硬背。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's just the most Naval solution. Okay, so this is Twitter, and this is the "How to Get Rich Without Getting Lucky" thread. So the first one —
这真是最「纳瓦尔」式的解法了。好，这就是推特，这是那条《不靠运气如何致富》（How to Get Rich Without Getting Lucky）的推文串。那么第一条——
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Well, a simple one is: how does knowledge get created? If you follow the critical rationalism / David Deutsch philosophy, then it's by guessing and then testing your guesses. So whenever they ask me something like "Well, why do you think that is?" — well, how would we figure out if that's true, right? So that's a basic game you can play, involving them. But another one is that a lot of the rules you teach kids have to do with hygiene, right? "You must brush your teeth." "Cover your mouth when you cough." "Clean up after yourself." "Don't touch that." "Wash your hands after you do this." "Don't eat food off the floor." But all of these are subsumed under the germ theory of disease, right? So if you instead go on YouTube and show them videos of germs, or have them look under a microscope at anything, they go "Ah" — they can infer what's going on.
一个简单的例子是：知识是如何被创造出来的？如果你认同批判理性主义（critical rationalism）／大卫·多伊奇（David Deutsch）的那套哲学，那答案就是：通过猜测，然后检验你的猜测。所以每当他们问我「你为什么会这么想？」——那好，我们要怎么弄清楚这是不是真的呢，对吧？这就是个可以陪他们玩的基本游戏，把他们牵扯进来。另一个例子是：你教孩子的很多规矩都跟卫生有关，对吧？「你必须刷牙。」「咳嗽要捂嘴。」「自己收拾自己的东西。」「别碰那个。」「做完这个要洗手。」「别捡地上的东西吃。」但这一切都可以归到疾病的细菌理论（germ theory of disease）之下，对吧？所以你与其讲规矩，不如上 YouTube 给他们看细菌的视频，或者让他们用显微镜随便看点什么，他们就会「啊」地一下——他们能自己推断出是怎么回事。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah — there's creepy crawlies everywhere and I've got to watch out for them.
对——到处都是爬来爬去的小东西，我得当心它们。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And then you can talk about how, if you look at humans, our real enemy are pathogens. I think a lot of aging and disease are actually downstream of our competition —
然后你就可以聊到：你看人类，我们真正的敌人是病原体（pathogens）。我认为很多衰老和疾病其实都是我们这场竞争的下游产物——

生物学 · 红皇后赛跑：病原体才是我们真正的天敌

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
— with pathogens over time, to a point that people still don't fully appreciate. There's a Red Queen hypothesis, which is that we undergo sexual selection to mix up our genes — so every 20 years, every generation, you mix up your genes. But if you look at how bacteria and viruses mutate through just random mutations, their mix-up rate and evolution rate is roughly the same as ours, even though they go through thousands of generations in those 20 years, because they're not doing sexual selection — they're doing asexual replication mutation. Their evolutionary rate is roughly equivalent to ours. So we're in a Red Queen race where we're both running at roughly the same speed, using very different strategies.
——也就是我们和病原体长期竞争的下游产物，其重要程度人们至今还没充分领会。有一个「红皇后假说」（Red Queen hypothesis），意思是：我们通过有性选择（sexual selection）来打乱重组自己的基因——所以每隔 20 年、每一代，你都重新洗牌一次基因。但你看细菌和病毒，它们仅靠随机突变来变异，它们的洗牌速率和进化速率却跟我们大致相当，尽管在这 20 年里它们经历了成千上万代——因为它们不靠有性选择，而是靠无性复制突变。它们的进化速率跟我们大致相当。所以我们处在一场「红皇后赛跑」里，双方用着截然不同的策略，却跑得差不多一样快。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
A lot of how we're evolved is around pathogens. Our immune system is one of the most expensive things to run; so much of the body is about immune-system optimization, and that's about pathogens. Junk DNA, bacteria — and CRISPR was discovered because in bacteria, their DNA is evolved to fight viruses. The way it does that is by taking viral DNA and snipping it up every time there's a viral attack, and storing it in their own DNA, so they have a copy and can recognize it next time it attacks. And a lot of the population structure of a species determines how long their lifespans are. So if in a given species there's a very high rate of infection, then the older members of the population are carrying diseases that will then infect the young — so it's important for that species to get rid of the old faster. So the higher the disease rate in a given population, the less long-lived the entire population, so the older ones don't infect the younger ones. That's a hypothesis, and I think it's pretty interesting.
我们进化方式的很大一部分都是围绕病原体的。我们的免疫系统是运行成本最高的东西之一；身体的好大一部分都在于免疫系统优化，而那是冲着病原体去的。垃圾 DNA、细菌——而 CRISPR 之所以被发现，正是因为在细菌体内，它们的 DNA 进化出了对抗病毒的能力。其做法是：每次遭到病毒攻击，就把病毒的 DNA 截取下来剪碎，存进自己的 DNA 里，这样就留了个副本，下次再被攻击时就能认出来。还有，一个物种的种群结构在很大程度上决定了它们的寿命。所以如果某个物种的感染率非常高，那么种群里年老的成员就会携带疾病、进而传染给年轻的——于是对这个物种来说，更快地淘汰掉老者就很重要。所以一个种群里疾病率越高，整个种群就越短寿，好让年老者别去传染年幼者。这是个假说，我觉得相当有意思。
Chris（克里斯）：
It's an interesting hypothesis.
这是个有意思的假说。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Homeostasis within the human body — how we're always returning to a given level of things — that's a fundamental part of our makeup. Our temperature, pH, blood pressure, and so on are under homeostasis. But if you engage in any kind of signaling — you take a peptide, for example, that's a signaling molecule; you take a hormone externally — the body will counteract it. You take testosterone —
人体内的稳态（homeostasis）——也就是我们总会回到某个既定水平——这是我们构造的一个根本部分。我们的体温、酸碱度（pH）、血压等等都处在稳态调控之下。但你只要进行任何形式的「信号传递」——比方说你服用一种肽（peptide），那是一种信号分子；或者你从外部摄入一种激素——身体就会去对抗它。你摄入睾酮（testosterone）——

生物学 · 稳态是防身术：身体为什么用「脉冲」释放激素

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
— the body will counteract it; it will downregulate its own production very fast. And the body releases its own hormones in pulses rather than at steady state. Why is that? Well, that's because bacteria and viruses can infect your body and trick your body — they can take it over, like toxoplasmosis does, like rabies does. They take over macroscopic structural bodies. Small bacteria and viruses would hack our bodies and literally take them over if we didn't have defense mechanisms. And one of those defense mechanisms is homeostasis: anytime you see something getting out of whack, you immediately push back really hard on it, because — did I just get infected? Is something trying to take me over? It's also why hormones get released in pulses at night rather than at steady, low levels — because enemy bacteria can release toxins, or the same signaling molecules, in small quantities, but they can't pulse. They can't coordinate to pulse. So your body can coordinate to pulse as a macroscopic object, but microscopic objects can't coordinate to create the same pulses.
——身体就会去对抗它；它会非常快地下调自身的产量。而且身体释放自身激素是以「脉冲」（pulses）方式、而不是稳定恒定地释放。为什么呢？因为细菌和病毒能感染你的身体、欺骗你的身体——它们能把身体接管过去，就像弓形虫病（toxoplasmosis）那样、像狂犬病（rabies）那样。它们接管宏观的结构性躯体。如果我们没有防御机制，小小的细菌和病毒就会黑进我们的身体、真的把它整个接管。而其中一种防御机制就是稳态：任何时候你一发现什么东西失衡了，就立刻狠狠地把它压回去，因为——我是不是刚被感染了？是不是有东西想接管我？这也是为什么激素在夜里是以脉冲方式释放，而不是稳定的低水平释放——因为敌方细菌能小剂量地释放毒素，或者释放同样的信号分子，但它们没法打出脉冲，没法协同地形成脉冲。所以你的身体作为一个宏观物体能协同地打出脉冲，而微观物体没法协同造出同样的脉冲。
Chris（克里斯）：
Oh, that's cool. Is that why? Because it's coming from you?
哦，这太妙了。所以才是这样吗？因为它是从你自身发出的？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Correct. It's endogenous rather than exogenous.
对。它是内源的（endogenous），而非外源的（exogenous）。
Chris（克里斯）：
I never knew that.
我从来都不知道这个。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And that's why we resist a lot of exogenous treatments — a lot of our medical treatments don't work. Anyway, there's a bunch more I could go on about, but I think a lot of — you see this in cancers, where the Epstein-Barr virus shows up in a lot of cancers. And now it seems like the gut microbiome influences so many things. Basically, bacteria and viruses are at the top of the food chain compared to us. We are at the top of the well-known food chain, but bacteria and viruses eat us, fungus eats us. So these microscopic predators are our natural predators. And so a lot of aging, societal structure, hygiene, religious strictures against pork, circumcision — all of these things are designed to resist bacteria and viruses. So if you can teach children this philosophy at an early age, you shortcut all the debates.
这也是为什么我们会抵抗很多外源性的治疗——我们很多医疗手段不起作用。总之，我还能往下讲一大堆，但我想很多——你在癌症里就能看到这一点，EB 病毒（Epstein-Barr virus）会出现在很多癌症中。如今看来肠道微生物群（gut microbiome）也影响着太多东西了。基本上，相对于我们，细菌和病毒才处在食物链的顶端。我们处在「已知食物链」的顶端，但细菌和病毒在吃我们，真菌在吃我们。所以这些微观掠食者才是我们真正的天敌。于是，很多衰老、社会结构、卫生习惯、宗教中对猪肉的禁忌、割礼（circumcision）——所有这些东西，都是为了抵御细菌和病毒而设计的。所以如果你能在孩子很小的时候就教会他们这套哲学，你就把所有的争论都抄了近道、一步跳过去了。
Chris（克里斯）：
How effective have you been at teaching that philosophy to children?
你把这套哲学教给孩子，效果怎么样？

育儿哲学 · 父子之间的哲学游戏：「虚无能存在吗？」与不当说教

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That one, I think I've been pretty effective. I've drilled that one at home. The one I haven't quite gotten around to yet is evolution. I'm starting to do little bits of that — like "we came from monkeys, what does that mean?" I've already got them thinking about some of the deeper questions. I did ask my young son, "Can nothing exist?" I thought that was a fun question. So I like to throw out a fun question.
那一套，我觉得我教得相当成功。这个我在家反复操练过。我还没怎么腾出手来教的是进化论。我开始一点点地讲了——比如「我们是从猴子来的，这是什么意思？」我已经在让他们思考一些更深的问题了。我确实问过我的小儿子：「虚无能存在吗？（虚无本身能不能存在？）」我觉得这是个有趣的问题。我就喜欢抛出一个好玩的问题。
Chris（克里斯）：
How old is he now? Like three?
他现在多大了？三岁左右？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
No, no, he's eight. An eight-year-old and a six-year-old. So I asked them both, "Can nothing exist?" And they had pretty good answers, right? Another one we played with the other day was, "What is the Matrix? What is this? What is all this?" I just find it entertaining — it's just fun to talk about, to talk about these questions with your kids. I'm not saying that's a good way of child-raising, that it's leading to any deeper learning — other than maybe just having them start, or continue, to question the basic structure of reality and not move past it so quickly.
不不，他八岁了。一个八岁、一个六岁。所以我问了他们俩：「虚无能存在吗？」他们的答案都挺不错，对吧？前几天我们还玩了另一个：「《黑客帝国》（the Matrix）是什么？这是什么？这一切是什么？」我就是觉得有意思——跟孩子聊这些问题，纯粹好玩。我并不是说这是一种好的育儿方式、说它能带来什么更深的学习——也许它的唯一作用，就是让他们开始、或者继续去质疑现实的基本结构，而不要那么快就略过去。
Chris（克里斯）：
Also, to take joy — what's the meta-lesson being taught there? Dad spends time asking questions to which there are not necessarily answers, because there is something enjoyable in the process of learning and trying to decipher what's happening.
还有，去享受其中——这里教的那层「元课程」（meta-lesson）是什么？是「爸爸会花时间去问那些不一定有答案的问题，因为在学习、在试图破解眼前发生之事的过程本身里，有某种令人愉悦的东西」。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Possibly. Also, Dad tries not too hard to teach people things — I don't want to be didactic.
有可能。还有，爸爸不会太用力地去「教」人东西——我不想搞说教（didactic）。
Chris（克里斯）：
He helps them to arrive at it.
他是帮他们自己抵达答案。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, correct. Dad is here to help you solve problems when you have problems — and you constantly have problems. So if you come to Dad, Dad can help explain to you how he would solve the problem. But most of the time, they don't want that —
对，没错。爸爸在这儿，是当你遇到问题时帮你解决问题——而你会不断遇到问题。所以如果你来找爸爸，爸爸可以帮你讲讲他会怎么解这个问题。但大多数时候，他们并不想要这个——
Chris（克里斯）：
Agency.
那是能动性（agency／自主能动性）。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Most of the time, they just want me to solve the problem. So sometimes you have to play dumb. It's like, "Why is my Wi-Fi not working on my computer?" And I'm like, "I don't know — did you click on that thing?"
大多数时候，他们只是想让我替他们把问题解决掉。所以有时候你得装傻。比如「我电脑的 Wi-Fi 为什么连不上？」我就说「我不知道啊——你点过那个东西了吗？」
Chris（克里斯）：
You've got like a rebellious sovereign child — sovereign as they may be, but sometimes they still need Dad to step in.
你养出来的是个叛逆的、有主权的孩子——尽管他们再怎么有主权（sovereign），有时候还是需要爸爸出手介入。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So in addition to feeling loved and having high self-esteem, I think the most important trait that would be nice to not rob them of is agency. I want them to preserve their agency. They're born naturally agentic and willful, but a lot of child-raising can beat that out of them, by essentially domesticating them.
所以，除了感受到被爱、拥有高自尊之外，我觉得最重要的、最好别从他们身上剥夺掉的特质，就是能动性（agency）。我希望他们保住自己的能动性。他们生来就天然地富有能动性、充满意志，但很多育儿方式会把这一点从他们身上打掉——本质上就是在驯化（domesticating）他们。

育儿哲学 · 宁要狼，不要乖巧的狗：「学得快、杀不死」

Chris（克里斯）：
That's right.
没错。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And I would rather have wild animals and wolves than have well-trained dogs, because I'm not going to be around to take care of them.
我宁愿要野生动物、要狼，也不要训练有素的狗，因为我不会一直在身边照顾他们。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, so they're going to have to be able to look after themselves.
对，所以他们将来必须能照顾好自己。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Exactly. A friend of mine, Parsa, on AirChat — he had a great saying. He said he wants his children to be quick to learn and hard to kill. So that was pretty good.
正是。我有个朋友帕尔萨（Parsa），在 AirChat 上——他有句很棒的话。他说他希望自己的孩子「学得快、杀不死」（quick to learn and hard to kill）。这话挺到位的。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, that was cool. I remember you saying — just thinking about sort of the future and culture and stuff like that — I remember you saying that the left had won the culture war, and now they're just driving around shooting the survivors. After the last six months of change that we've seen, and sort of where we're at at the moment, what do you think the future of the culture war looks like?
嗯，那话很酷。我记得你说过——就着对未来、对文化之类的思考——我记得你说过，左派已经赢得了这场文化战争（culture war），现在他们只是开着车四处补枪、扫射幸存者。在我们刚刚见证的过去这六个月的变化、以及当下我们所处的位置之后，你觉得文化战争的未来会是什么样子？

文化与政治 · 文化战争没结束：伟人史观 vs 大势史观

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It's not over yet. They definitely won earlier rounds — they took over institutions. I think now it's much more of a fair fight, where you have people like Elon kind of supporting [the other side]. So there are these different forces through history, right? Historians will argue about this. There's a "great man" theory of history — like, you have the Einsteins, you have the Teslas, you have the Genghis Khans and the Caesars, right? They determine the flow of history. And then there's the other point of view: that no, there are these massive forces at play — demographics and geography and so on — and then the particular great man doesn't matter; they just come and go. Napoleon doesn't matter; it would have been somebody else. The specific names are not important.
还没结束。他们早几个回合肯定是赢了——他们接管了各种机构。我觉得现在这更像是一场公平得多的较量了，因为有埃隆（Elon，指马斯克）这样的人在某种程度上支持（另一方）。所以纵观历史，有这么几股不同的力量，对吧？历史学家会为此争论不休。有一种「伟人史观」（great man theory of history）——比如有爱因斯坦们、有特斯拉们（指尼古拉·特斯拉）、有成吉思汗们和凯撒们（Caesars），对吧？是他们决定了历史的走向。然后还有另一种观点：不，是有这些巨大的力量在起作用——人口结构、地理等等——而具体是哪个伟人无所谓；他们来了又走。拿破仑（Napoleon）无关紧要；换个别人也一样。具体的名字并不重要。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And because of the kind of leftist turn that our institutions took in the last few decades, they now only subscribe to the "great forces" theory of history, not the "great man" theory of history. But I think now we're seeing the two play out, where you're seeing Trump and Elon and other individuals rising up and saying, "No, we resist."
而正因为我们的各种机构在过去几十年里发生的那种「左转」，他们如今只认「大势史观」（great forces theory），不认「伟人史观」。但我觉得现在我们正看到这两者同时上演，因为你看到特朗普（Trump）、埃隆以及其他个人挺身而出，说：「不，我们要反抗。」
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, that's interesting.
嗯，这很有意思。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And the battle between these collectivists and "great forces" versus individuals — it's as old as humanity itself, and it is fundamental to the species. We are not a completely individualistic species.
而这些集体主义者、「大势论」与「个人」之间的战斗——它跟人类本身一样古老，而且对这个物种是根本性的。我们并不是一个完全个人主义的物种。

政治哲学 · 在蜂群与孤岛之间：马克思主义与资本主义的钟摆

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
No man is an island. A single person can't do anything by themselves. But we're also not a Borg — we're not a beehive, we're not an ant colony, we're not all just drones marching along. So which is it? We're somewhere in the middle. And the human race is always kind of bouncing between the two. We like strong leaders, we like to be led, we like to coordinate our forces en masse and do things. But at the same time, we're also all individuals, willing to break away and do our own thing. And everyone's always fighting to be a leader, and there are always status games going on. So there's a pendulum that's always swinging back and forth.
没有人是一座孤岛（No man is an island）。一个人单枪匹马什么都干不成。但我们也不是「博格人」（Borg，《星际迷航》中的集体意识种族）——我们不是蜂巢，不是蚁群，不是一群只会齐步前进的工蜂。那到底是哪一种？我们处在中间的某个位置。而人类这个物种总是在两极之间来回弹跳。我们喜欢强势的领袖，我们喜欢被领导，我们喜欢成群结队地协调力量去做事。但与此同时，我们又都是一个个的个体，随时愿意脱队、去干自己的事。而且每个人都在争着当领袖，地位游戏（status games）也无时无刻不在上演。所以有一座钟摆，永远在来回摆动。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And in modern economics, the way that manifests is between Marxism and capitalism, right? Marxism is "from each according to his ability, to each according to his needs" — we're all equal, there's a millenarian project, we're all going to be equal in the end, don't try to stand out, but do what's good for everybody. And there's a religious aspect to it. And then the capitalist, individualist [view] is libertarian — every man for himself, you each do what you want and it'll work out for the greater good. That's Adam Smith — the invisible hand of the market will feed you. The baker should bake, the butcher should butcher, the candlestick maker should make candlesticks, and it'll all work out: each person does their best, and they trade. So which is it? Which theory is correct? And I think there's always going to be a battle between the two.
而在现代经济学里，这种摆动表现为马克思主义（Marxism）与资本主义（capitalism）之间的对立，对吧？马克思主义讲的是「各尽所能，按需分配」——我们人人平等，有一个千禧（救世）式的工程，最终我们都将平等，别想着出人头地，而要做对大家都好的事。它还带有一种宗教色彩。然后资本主义、个人主义的那一派则是自由意志主义（libertarian）——人人为己，各干各想干的事，最终会成就更大的公共利益。这就是亚当·斯密（Adam Smith）——市场那只看不见的手会喂饱你。面包师就该烤面包，屠夫就该宰肉，做蜡烛的就该做蜡烛，一切自会理顺：人人各尽其力，然后彼此交易。那到底是哪一种？哪套理论才对？我觉得这两者之间永远都会有一场战斗。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And I think the interesting thing that's going on is there's a modern flavor to it, which changes it. The modern flavor is that the individual is getting more powerful, because they're getting more leverage. So someone like an Elon Musk can have the leverage of tens of thousands of brilliant engineers and producers working for him. He can have factories of robots manufacturing things. He can have hundreds of billions of dollars of capital behind him, and he can project himself through media to hundreds of millions of people. That is more power than any individual could have had historically. So the great men of history are becoming greater. That said, that same leverage is increasing the gap between the haves and have-nots.
而我觉得当下正在发生的、有意思的地方在于：这事带上了一种现代的味道，从而改变了它。这股现代的味道就是：个人正变得越来越强大，因为他们获得了越来越多的杠杆（leverage）。所以像埃隆·马斯克（Elon Musk）这样的人，可以拥有数以万计的杰出工程师和生产者为他效力的杠杆。他可以拥有一座座由机器人组成的工厂去制造东西。他背后可以有数千亿美元的资本，他还能通过媒体把自己投射给数以亿计的人。这是历史上任何个人都不曾拥有过的权力。所以历史上的伟人正变得更加伟大。话虽如此，正是这同一种杠杆，也在拉大「有产者」与「无产者」之间的差距——

社会与权力 · 赢家与隐形人：相对性动物与多数人的暴政

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
— so in the wealth game, more people are winning overall, and the average is going up. But in the status game, there are essentially more losers — there are more invisible men and women who are getting nothing out of life and have no leverage. Relatively speaking. Objectively speaking, they might be better off; they still have phones and TVs, and they're not —
——所以在财富游戏里，整体上有更多的人在赢，平均水平在上升。但在地位游戏（status game）里，本质上输家更多了——有更多隐形的男男女女，他们从人生里什么都没得到、毫无杠杆可言。这是就相对而言的。客观地说，他们的处境也许更好了；他们还是有手机、有电视，他们并不——
Chris（克里斯）：
They're not absolute creatures, they're relative creatures.
他们不是「绝对性的生物」，他们是「相对性的生物」（relative creatures）。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Correct. And to the extent that we're relative creatures, there are more losers than winners. And in a democracy, those people will outnumber the winners, and they will vote the winners down. So that's the battle that goes on. And the democracy has gotten very broad. So one of my other quips is that it's not the right to vote that gives you power — it's power that gives you the right to vote. So we've confused the two.
对。在我们是「相对性生物」这一点上，输家比赢家多。而在民主制（democracy）里，这些人在数量上会压倒赢家，他们会投票把赢家压下去。这就是正在进行的那场战斗。而民主已经变得非常宽泛了。所以我另一句俏皮话是：不是投票权赋予你权力——而是权力赋予你投票权。我们把这两者搞混了。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
What happened was, voting started as a way for people who had power to divide up the power and not fight amongst themselves. The winners of the revolution, the winners of the war, the people in the House of Lords and the House of Commons — they divide up power amongst themselves. They say, "Hey, we have all the money, we have the power, we are the knights, we have the swords, we have the warriors, we could kill everybody — but we don't want to just fight each other all day long. We don't have to be Game of Thrones forever. So we're going to divide up power by voting amongst ourselves." But then, as society goes on and becomes more and more peaceful, that franchise for voting gets spread. It gets spread to people who don't have land, who don't have power, who may not be able to inflict physical violence. And then eventually you get to the point where everybody's voting — and everybody's voting for candy and fairies and all the free things in life.
事情的经过是这样：投票一开始，是有权力的人用来瓜分权力、免得彼此火并的一种方式。革命的胜利者、战争的胜利者、上议院（House of Lords）和下议院（House of Commons）里的那些人——他们在自己之间瓜分权力。他们说：「嘿，钱都在我们手里，权力在我们手里，我们是骑士，我们有刀剑，我们有战士，我们能把所有人都杀光——但我们不想整天就这么自相残杀。我们不必永远活在《权力的游戏》（Game of Thrones）里。所以我们要靠在我们内部投票来瓜分权力。」但随后，社会不断发展、变得越来越和平，这投票的「选举权」（franchise）就开始扩散。它扩散到那些没有土地、没有权力、可能根本施加不了肉体暴力的人身上。然后你最终走到这一步：所有人都在投票——而所有人都在投票要糖果、要仙女、要人生里一切免费的东西。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And then eventually people start voting to oppress each other. The 51 percent, in any domain, vote to suppress the 49. It's a tyranny of the majority. But not all of them are willing to back that up with physical power. And so you can end up in a situation where people who don't have physical power are using the institutions of the state to control the people who do have physical power. As a simple example, taking the United States: the people who don't have guns voting to disarm the people that do have guns, right? Well, if the people who do have guns get coordinated —
再然后，人们终于开始投票去压迫彼此。那 51% 的人，在任何领域，都会投票去压制那 49%。这就是「多数人的暴政」（tyranny of the majority）。但他们并不是所有人都愿意用肉体力量去给这投票撑腰。于是你可能落到这样一种局面：没有肉体力量的人，正在动用国家机器去控制那些拥有肉体力量的人。举个简单的例子，拿美国来说：没有枪的人投票去解除有枪之人的武装，对吧？可是，要是那些有枪的人协调串联起来——

社会与权力 · 一切之下是暴力：不稳定的社会结构与新闻的洪流

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
— and care enough, you can't do that, right? So I think eventually these societal structures are unstable. They break down. And they break down because eventually the people who have the power say, "No, wait a minute — you don't get to vote. You only got to vote because you had power, and now you don't have power, and you're somehow trying to vote." All of nature, all of society, all of capitalism, all of human endeavor is underpinned by physical violence. And that is a very hard truth to swallow, and hard to get away from. Nature is red in tooth and claw. If you don't fight, you don't survive, you don't live — you die. And that's true of everything alive today, and humans are no different.
——并且足够在乎，你就办不成那件事，对吧？所以我认为，这些社会结构终归是不稳定的，它们会崩塌。它们之所以崩塌，是因为最终拥有权力的人会说：「不，等一下——你没资格投票。你当初能投票，只是因为你那时有权力；现在你没有权力了，却还想着投票。」整个自然、整个社会、整个资本主义、人类所有的奋斗，底下都垫着肉体暴力。而这是一个极难下咽、极难回避的真相。自然界是「血染獠牙与利爪」（red in tooth and claw）。你若不战斗，你就活不下来、活不下去——你就会死。这对当今一切活着的东西都成立，人类也不例外。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So giving up physical power and then thinking you can exercise political power — that fails. Which is why every communist revolution, which is all about equality and Kumbaya and brothers and sisters, ends up being run by a bunch of thugs. Because if you don't have a way to divide up the wealth based on merit, then it's always going to be based on power and influence. The thugs with the guns always win in the end. So the question is just: can you keep the thugs with the guns paid and happy, in a successful society where you're allocating based on merit? Because if you can't, then you're going to do it based on power. So I do think that this battle is not over — but that's because it never stopped. It's always been there from day one. It will continue.
所以，放弃了肉体力量，却以为自己还能行使政治权力——这是行不通的。这也是为什么，每一场共产主义革命——明明满口都是平等、是「Kumbaya」（一团和气的口号歌）、是兄弟姐妹——最后都沦为被一帮暴徒（thugs）掌控。因为如果你没有一套按功绩（merit）分配财富的办法，那分配最终就总会按权力和影响力来。手里有枪的暴徒，最后总是赢家。所以问题不过是：在一个按功绩分配的、运转良好的社会里，你能不能把那些有枪的暴徒喂饱、哄好、让他们成功？因为要是你做不到，你就只能改按权力来分配了。所以我确实认为，这场战斗没有结束——但那是因为它从未停止过。它从第一天起就一直在那儿。它还会继续下去。
Chris（克里斯）：
Is it a battle to not care about the news in an age of news saturation? All of this stuff — headlines 24 hours a day, streaming directly into your consciousness through a device in your pocket. A lot of what we've spoken about today is freedom — freedom from having to think about, or care about, things that you don't have control over, or that you shouldn't, or that you don't want to. And yet people are just submerged up to the bottom of their nostrils, basically drowning in worry. So, yeah — is it a battle to stay out of the news when you're saturated in it?
在这个新闻过载的时代，「不去在意新闻」本身是不是一场战斗？所有这些东西——一天 24 小时的头条，通过你口袋里的一台设备，直接灌进你的意识里。我们今天聊的很多内容都是关于自由——也就是从「不得不去想、去在意那些你掌控不了的、或你不该在意的、或你压根不想在意的事」当中解脱出来的自由。可人们偏偏被淹到了鼻孔底下，基本上是在忧虑里溺水。所以，是啊——当你已经被新闻泡透了，「躲开新闻」是不是一场战斗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah. I mean, as you're saying, the human brain is not evolved to handle all the world's emergencies breaking in real time. You can't care about everything, and you'll go insane if you try. Doesn't mean you shouldn't care at all — there's no "should." If you want to care, go ahead and care. I would just say that you're probably —
是啊。我是说，正如你讲的，人脑并没有进化到能去应对全世界所有的紧急事件实时爆发。你没法在意所有事，要是硬试，你会发疯。这并不意味着你就该完全不在意——根本没有「应该」这回事。你要想在意，那就尽管去在意。我只想说，你大概是——

欲望与新闻 · 只关心你能影响的事

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
...better off only caring about things that are local or things that you can affect. So if you really care about something that's in the news, then by all means care about it, but make a difference. Go do something about it, and make sure that it's your overwhelming desire and you don't have five other desires at the same time. Also, just realize the consequences of it: you're going to be unhappy until that thing gets fixed, and that thing will often be out of your control. Desire is a contract to be unhappy until you get what you want. But for the most part, that's something that is in your life. It's like, "till I lose the weight," "until I get the job." You can be outside too, yeah. If it's "until the carbon dioxide parts per million are below this particular number," that's a tough one. Or all the people with Trump derangement syndrome, right? He's living rent-free in their heads and driving them insane. And I get it—I mean, there are politicians who have definitely driven me insane as well. But it comes at a very high cost. It's something that is out of your control that you cannot really influence. So it's probably good to at least be conscious of it.
……最好只关心那些就在身边、或你能影响的事。所以如果你真的在意某件新闻里的事，那就尽管去在意，但要真正带来改变——去为它做点什么，并确保它是你压倒一切的渴望，而不是同时还有另外五个渴望。同时也要看清它的后果：在那件事被解决之前你都会不快乐，而那件事往往不在你的掌控之中。欲望，就是一纸"在得到想要的之前都不快乐"的契约。但大多数情况下，那应该是你生活里的事，比如"等我减下肥来""等我拿到那份工作"。也可以是身外之事，对。但如果是"等二氧化碳浓度降到某个特定数值以下"，那就难办了。又或者那些得了"特朗普综合征"的人，对吧？他白住在人家脑子里，把他们逼疯。我能理解——我是说，确实也有政客把我逼疯过。但这代价非常高。那是你掌控不了、也根本影响不了的东西。所以至少对此有所自觉，大概是好事。

一百、时事 vs 永恒 · 被媒体忽视、却会被史家研究的事

Chris（克里斯）：
You mentioned historians before. One of my friends has a question—his equivalent of Peter Thiel's question of "What is it that you believe that most people would disagree with?" His is: "What do you think is currently ignored by the media but will be studied by historians?"
你刚才提到了史学家。我有个朋友有个问题——相当于他版本的彼得·蒂尔之问"有什么是你相信、但大多数人会反对的？"。他的问题是："你认为有什么是当下被媒体忽视、却终将被史家研究的？"
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
You're asking me that question right now? What do I think is ignored by the media but will be studied by historians? Well, I mean, the media is only focused on very timely things, right? So it depends if you want to talk about timely or timeless. But as a simple example, if I just look at things that maybe in the next five or ten years are going to make a massive difference that people are not focused enough on—and I think within two years this will be obvious—so I'll make a prediction. And predictions are tough, but you're gonna have to eat it in a few years.
你现在就要我回答这个问题？我觉得有什么被媒体忽视、却会被史家研究的？嗯，媒体只盯着非常应时的东西，对吧？所以要看你想谈的是"应时的"还是"永恒的"。举个简单例子吧，我就看那些在未来五到十年里会产生巨大影响、而人们关注得不够的事——我觉得两年之内这会变得显而易见——那我就做个预测。预测很难，但过几年你就得为它埋单。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, you're gonna have to eat this in a few years.
是啊，过几年你就得为这个埋单。

一百零一、预测一 · 现代医学还停留在石器时代

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So I'm probably wrong, but two things that I pay attention to that I don't think a lot of people do—well, there's a couple. One is, I think, just how bad modern medicine is. I think people just put a lot more faith in modern medicine than is warranted. Like, our best ideas for a lot of things are surgery—just cutting things out, right? Treating things that are extraneous, like, "Oh, you don't really need a gallbladder, you don't really need an appendix, you don't really need tonsils." Oh, that's false. The human body is very, very efficient. All those things are needed. So I think the state of modern medicine is still pretty bad. We don't have many good explanatory theories in biology. We have germ theory of disease, we have evolution, we have cell theory, we have DNA, genetics, morphogenesis, embryogenesis—and not much else. There's not much else. Everything else is rules of thumb, memorization: A affects B because A affects... C affects D, but we don't understand the underlying explanation. It's all just words pointing to words pointing to words. So biology is still in a very sorry state. And because we are not allowed to take risks that might kill people, we just don't experiment enough in biology, so a lot of treatments are just outright banned by large regulatory bodies. So we just don't have the innovation. So I think we're still in the Stone Age when it comes to biology, and we've got a long way to go. And I think people will look back aghast at this. And I think this is Brian Johnson's point—he's like, "Let's be more extreme, let's try to live forever, it must be more experimental, and I'll start as an end-of-one and start experimenting on myself." But even there, I disagree with Brian on many things, like taking huge amounts of supplements. I think we just don't know if supplements outside of the natural context—like, just eat liver, man. But it's fine. And I wouldn't be vegan either. But I really appreciate that he's experimenting, he's good-natured about it, he shares everything. So we need more people like that. So I think the state of biology, people will look back and say, "Wow, that was in the dark ages."
所以我很可能是错的，但有两件事是我关注、而我觉得很多人都没关注的——其实有好几件。第一件，我觉得就是现代医学有多糟糕。我认为人们对现代医学的信心，远超它实际配得上的程度。很多问题，我们最好的办法就是手术——直接切掉，对吧？还把一些器官当成多余的来对待，比如"哦，你其实不需要胆囊，你其实不需要阑尾，你其实不需要扁桃体"。哦，那是错的。人体非常非常高效，那些东西全都是有用的。所以我觉得现代医学的状态还是相当差。生物学里我们没什么好的解释性理论。我们有疾病的细菌理论、有进化论、有细胞学说、有DNA、遗传学、形态发生、胚胎发生——再没多少了。其余几乎全是经验法则、死记硬背：A影响B，因为A影响……C影响D，但我们并不理解背后的解释。全都是词指向词、词再指向词。所以生物学还处在很可悲的状态。又因为我们不被允许冒可能致人死亡的风险，我们在生物学里根本实验得不够，许多疗法直接被大监管机构彻底禁掉了。所以我们就是没有创新。所以我觉得在生物学这块我们还停留在石器时代，路还长得很。我觉得后人回头看会震惊不已。我想这也是布莱恩·约翰逊（Brian Johnson）的观点——他说："咱们激进一点，努力争取长生不老，必须更敢于实验，我就拿自己当样本量为一的实验对象，从我自己身上开始试。"不过即便如此，我在很多事上跟布莱恩看法不同，比如吃大量补剂。我觉得我们根本不知道脱离了天然情境的补剂行不行——老兄，直接吃点肝脏就行了嘛。不过那也没关系。我也不会去当素食者。但我真的很欣赏他在做实验，态度也好，什么都分享出来。我们需要更多这样的人。所以我觉得生物学的现状，后人回头会说："哇，那时候简直是黑暗时代。"

一百零二、预测二 · 无人机将统治未来一切战争

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I think another thing that we'll look back on is, I think we still continue to underestimate how important drones are going to be in warfare. The future of all warfare is drones. There will be nothing else on the battlefield. Because I think of the end state of drones as autonomous bullets—not even guided, autonomous, like they're self-directed. And so if that's the future we're headed towards, that's it.
我觉得另一件后人会回头看的事是，我们仍然持续低估了无人机在战争中将有多重要。一切战争的未来都是无人机。战场上将不会再有别的东西。因为我把无人机的终极形态想象成"自主子弹"——甚至不是制导，而是自主的，它们能自己导向目标。所以如果那就是我们正奔向的未来，那就是这样。
Chris（克里斯）：
Why would you have an armed force that's—
那你为什么还要养一支军队来——
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
There's going to be no aircraft carriers, there's going to be no tanks, there's going to be no infantrymen. They're just going to be autonomous bullets—your autonomous bullets against their autonomous bullets. Whichever ones win, the other side just surrenders, because it's over. I think that's the second piece of it.
将不会有航空母舰，不会有坦克，不会有步兵。就只剩自主子弹——你的自主子弹对抗对方的自主子弹。哪一方赢了，另一方就直接投降，因为大局已定。我觉得这是第二点。

一百零三、预测三 · GLP-1 是青霉素以来最具突破性的药

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I think a third piece that is going to be kind of unexpected is the GLP-1s, which I know you and I have privately discussed before. I think these are the most breakthrough drugs since antibiotics. They're probably more important than statins. They're sort of miracle drugs. There are downsides, but the downsides and side effects are so minor compared to the upsides—beyond just weight loss. They also seem to be addiction breakers. They seem to lower many kinds of cancer. They almost metabolically reverse aging up to a certain point. And I think they're going to bend the curve on healthcare costs. And the big question people are going to be asking over the next five years is: why are Americans paying thousands of dollars a month for this when people overseas are getting them for free, or I can order from China for free or whatever? And maybe—if I were Bernie Sanders, the platform I would be running on, I would say, "Okay, we're gonna pay hundreds of billions of dollars to Novo and Eli Lilly, and we're just gonna make these free." And there are hundreds of analogs of these things that work—these are not going to be limited to just the few that are being used today. Just take one of them or two of them, make them free, and I think it'll make a big difference. And, as you and I were discussing earlier, this does bend a lot of people out of shape who got there the old-fashioned way and they want to see obesity as a moral failing on people's parts. And it lowers their status—if suddenly the signal is less of a signal.
我觉得第三点会有点出人意料，就是 GLP-1 类药物，我知道你我之前私下聊过。我认为这是自抗生素以来最具突破性的药。它们大概比他汀类（statins）还重要，算是某种神药。它们有副作用，但与好处相比，那些缺点和副作用太微不足道了——而且好处远不止减重。它们似乎还能戒断成瘾，似乎能降低多种癌症的风险，在某个程度上几乎能在代谢层面逆转衰老。我觉得它们会把医疗成本的曲线掰弯。未来五年人们会问的大问题是：为什么美国人每月要花数千美元买这个，而海外的人能免费拿到，或者我从中国免费订就行？也许——如果我是伯尼·桑德斯（Bernie Sanders），我会拿这个当竞选纲领，我会说："好，我们出几千亿美元给诺和（Novo）和礼来（Eli Lilly），然后就把这些药做成免费的。"而且这类药有上百种有效的类似物——不会只局限于今天在用的那少数几种。随便挑一两种，做成免费，我觉得就会带来很大改变。还有，就像你我之前聊的，这确实让很多人很不爽——那些靠老办法瘦下来的人，他们想把肥胖看成是人的道德缺陷。这降低了他们的地位——如果那个"信号"突然不那么算信号了。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, absolutely.
对，完全是这样。

一百零四、反对声背后 · GLP-1 戳破了多少"神圣的奶牛"

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So they're incentivized to say, "Oh, well, you don't know the downsides, it's irresponsible to suggest it, it's going to cause cancer, have fun losing bone and muscle mass." By the way, none of that stuff is really true. The cancer stuff is actually beneficial. I know people who are now taking these things for anti-aging reasons—they're already fit, but they just want to age better and have a stronger insulin metabolism. And there's evidence now that these things put off dementia, Alzheimer's, colon cancer—it's insane—cardiovascular disease. Like, the list of benefits is insane. There's no free lunch, but this is a class of drugs that prevents you from taking other drugs into your body. It prevents you from taking too much sugar, too many calories in an era of abundance, prevents you from smoking, prevents you even—there's an organization called Casper that is now doing a study on heroin addictions, and they're showing that it's going to lower opioid overdoses and heroin addictions. So there's a lot of overwhelming medical evidence coming out. And I think—I don't know the exact number, but I think something like 10% of the population might have tried these things.
所以他们就有动机去说："哦，你不知道它的坏处，建议别人用是不负责任的，它会致癌，等着丢骨量和肌肉量吧。"顺便说一句，这些说法基本都不成立。关于癌症的部分实际上是有益的。我认识一些人，他们现在吃这些药是出于抗衰老的目的——他们本来就很健美，只是想老得更好、让胰岛素代谢更强。而且现在有证据表明，这些药能推迟痴呆、阿尔茨海默症、结肠癌——简直离谱——还有心血管疾病。好处的清单长得离谱。天下没有免费午餐，但这是一类能阻止你往体内摄入其他东西的药。它让你在这个物质过剩的时代不会摄入太多糖、太多热量，让你戒烟，甚至——有个叫 Casper 的机构正在做海洛因成瘾的研究，他们的数据显示它能降低阿片类药物过量和海洛因成瘾。所以现在涌现出大量压倒性的医学证据。我觉得——我不知道确切数字，但大概有 10% 的人口可能已经试过这些药。【存疑：原文 "might not have tried" 疑为口误，按上下文应为 "might have tried"，已据语境校订】
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, I think that's the number that I'd seen as well. Yeah, it's massive. I think it's about 50% of the population say that they would like to try it.
对，我看到的也是这个数字。是啊，规模很大。我记得大概有 50% 的人口说他们想试试。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Exactly. So I think the body positivity movement is dead. And we always kind of knew it was a scam—I mean, it's dying very, very quickly.
正是。所以我觉得"身体自爱"（body positivity）运动已经死了。我们其实一直隐约知道那是个骗局——我是说，它正在非常非常快地消亡。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah.
是啊。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I quipped, like, "You can never be too rich, too thin, or too clean." And immediately a whole bunch of people went nonlinear, mentioning like, "What do you mean too thin? And what about the hygiene hypothesis?" And obviously there's always exceptions, but people want to be thin and fit, and people want to be clean—back to the pathogen discussion that we had. So I think overall there's going to be huge demand for these things, and our modern medical system is not built to supply these well. I don't hold it against the pharma; I think the pharma did their job by creating the thing. But I think next we need to step up and figure out how to make it broadly and cheaply available, as opposed to just milking it only for people on obesity who can get Medicare to sign off for it, or people paying out of pocket at very, very high prices.
我打趣说："人永远不会太有钱、太瘦或太干净。"结果一大堆人立刻就炸了，跑来说："'太瘦'是什么意思？那卫生假说（hygiene hypothesis）怎么算？"显然总会有例外，但人们就是想瘦、想健美，人们就是想干净——又回到我们之前聊的病原体话题。所以我觉得总体来看，这类药会有巨大的需求，而我们的现代医疗体系并不是为高效供应这些药而建的。我不怪药厂——我觉得药厂把东西造出来，已经尽了本分。但接下来我觉得我们得挺身而出，想办法让它廉价、广泛地普及，而不是只盯着那些能让医保（Medicare）批准的肥胖患者去榨利润，或者让自费的人付极高的价钱。

一百零五、分配的政治 · 谁普及它，谁就将获重赏

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
The benefits of societal distribution of the safer GLP-1s is so large that whichever politician tackles that is going to be richly rewarded.
把更安全的 GLP-1 类药物在全社会铺开，好处大到——哪个政客来啃下这块，谁就会获得丰厚的回报。
Chris（克里斯）：
Well, obesity is the number one source of malnutrition worldwide. There's twice as many people that are obese than are starving. So about half a billion people are starving, and so many problems are downstream of that. Like, look at how much of the federal budget goes into dialysis because of kidney failure. And why is that? It's because of diabetes, right?
要知道，肥胖是全球营养不良的头号来源。肥胖的人数是饥饿人数的两倍。大约有五亿人在挨饿，而许许多多问题都是从这往下衍生出来的。比如，看看联邦预算里有多少花在了因肾衰竭而做的透析上。为什么会这样？就是因为糖尿病，对吧？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
So, so many of the problems that we have in modern society are downstream of obesity. And you know this—fitness is so important. And yes, in some people these things cause muscle and bone loss, but not in the people who are eating high protein and working out hard. So they can be taken in a way that's safer. And some versions of these, like liraglutide, the original one—they've been around for decades, and the others have been around for about a decade. And we already have, as you said, 10% of the population taking them. So they're already quite widely distributed. It's a good sample size.
所以现代社会里我们碰到的太多问题，都是从肥胖往下衍生的。你也知道——健康体能太重要了。是的，在某些人身上这些药确实会造成肌肉和骨量流失，但在那些高蛋白饮食、努力锻炼的人身上不会。所以它们可以用更安全的方式来服用。其中一些版本，比如最早的利拉鲁肽（liraglutide），已经存在几十年了，其他的也存在大约十年了。而且就像你说的，已经有 10% 的人口在用它们。所以它们其实已经相当广泛地铺开了。这个样本量很不错。
Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, it's a great sample size. What more do you need?
是啊，是个很棒的样本量。你还想要什么？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Like, if you have a bacterial infection that's eating you, I don't say, "Oh, I have this antibiotic, but it's going to raise your blood pressure." It's like—no, take the antibiotic. If you're going to kill yourself, I say, "Take this antipsychotic and stay alive a little longer and solve it." I don't say, "Oh, it's going to cause your heart rate to go up by three beats a minute"—I don't worry about that. So similarly, if you're poisoning yourself with toxins and overuse of substances that you shouldn't be using—either heroin, alcohol, cigarettes, sugar, or just sheer calories—take this GLP-1. They also improve digestion; you just have less food matter going through your stomach. They lower cancer risks across the board—there's quite a few cancers that they lower. Cardiovascular—I mean, I don't know what else to tell you.
打个比方，如果你身上有个正在吞噬你的细菌感染，我不会说："哦，我这儿有抗生素，但它会升高你的血压。"——不，吃下那个抗生素。如果你要自杀，我会说："吃下这个抗精神病药，多活一会儿，把问题解决了。"我不会说："哦，它会让你心率每分钟多跳三下"——我才不操这个心。同理，如果你正用毒素和过度使用那些不该用的东西在毒害自己——不管是海洛因、酒精、香烟、糖，还是单纯的热量——那就吃这个 GLP-1。它们还能改善消化，因为经过你胃里的食物残渣更少了。它们全面降低各类癌症的风险——能降低相当多种癌症。还有心血管——我是说，我真不知道还能跟你说什么了。
Chris（克里斯）：
I've been very surprised by the negative reception whenever you have a conversation about GLP-1s. And I think a lot of it may be people who—
每次一聊到 GLP-1，那种负面反应都让我很意外。我觉得很大一部分可能是那些——
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Well, think about how many sacred cows are being gored, right? All the people who are basically saying, "You should work harder, you should be fit like I did," right? It's lowering their status. Think about all the nutritionists and doctors and trainers who are now being—you know, it's too easy—they're being put out of business in a way, right? It's kind of like, why does the American military keep buying aircraft carriers in the age of drones? There's an incentive bias. It's a very strong motivated reasoning. But it doesn't matter—10% of people are on it, everybody wants to be fit, it's going to spread like wildfire.
想想看，这戳破了多少头"神圣的奶牛"，对吧？所有那些基本上在说"你应该更努力，你应该像我这样靠苦练保持身材"的人，对吧？这降低了他们的地位。想想所有那些营养师、医生和教练，他们现在——你懂的，这变得太容易了——某种程度上他们要被挤出这个行当了，对吧？这有点像：在无人机时代，美军为什么还一直买航空母舰？这里有一种利益偏见。是一种非常强的"动机性推理"。但这都无所谓——10% 的人已经在用了，人人都想健美，它会像野火一样蔓延开来。

一百零六、走出过去 · 该不该被自己的历史"拥有"

Chris（克里斯）：
I was just thinking as you were talking that, when we think about health, a lot of people kind of get captured by the way that they were brought up—the habits that they had from their childhood, or what mom and dad did, or genetic predisposition and stuff like that. I think you have as many reasons as many people to sort of feel hard done by—challenges that you had earlier on in your life. Is getting past your past a skill—sort of not being owned today by your history, sort of not having that victimhood mentality?
你刚才说的时候我就在想，当我们谈健康时，很多人多少被自己的成长方式给框住了——童年养成的习惯，或者爸妈当年怎么做的，又或者遗传倾向之类的。我觉得你和很多人一样，有同样多的理由觉得自己受了委屈——你人生早年遭遇的种种困境。那么，"走出自己的过去"是一种技能吗——也就是今天不被自己的历史所"拥有"，不抱那种受害者心态？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yeah, I did have a tough childhood, but I don't think about it. I think there are a couple of things going on there. One is, I did process it quite a bit. I thought about it—but I thought about it to get rid of it. I didn't think about it to dwell on it. I wanted to be successful, I wanted more than anything else to rise past that, and so I couldn't have that as a burden on me. So I had to get rid of it. So to the extent that I dealt with it, it was for the express purpose of getting rid of it—not to create an identity or story, or to reflect upon it, or to say, "Look at me, look at what I've accomplished and look how great I am and what I've done." So I got rid of it. And I think at some point you wrestle with that thing, and then you just realize you're never going to untangle the whole thing. It's a Gordian knot problem. Like Alexander—you know, he found that tangled knot in India, and it said, "Oh, the famous conqueror will come and will untie this knot, nobody else can untie the knot." And he took one look at it, pulled out a sword, and just cut it. So at some point you just have to cut your past. If your past is bothering you, you will eventually get tired of trying to untangle that knot, and you will just drop it, because you will realize life is short.
是的，我童年确实过得很苦，但我不去想它。我觉得这里有几层。第一，我确实把它消化了相当多。我想过它——但我想它是为了摆脱它，不是为了沉溺其中。我想成功，我比什么都更想超越那段过去，所以我不能让它成为我的负担。所以我必须摆脱它。所以我处理它的程度，全是出于一个明确目的：摆脱它——而不是去打造一种身份或故事，不是去反复回味，也不是为了说"看我，看我成就了什么，看我多了不起、我做了什么"。所以我把它甩掉了。我觉得到某个时刻，你会跟那东西较劲，然后你会意识到，你永远没法把整团结解开。这是个"戈耳狄俄斯之结"（Gordian knot）的难题。就像亚历山大——你知道，他在印度发现了那个缠死的结，传说写着："那位著名的征服者会来解开这个结，别人谁都解不开。"他看了一眼，抽出剑，一刀就把它砍断了。所以到某个时刻，你就得把自己的过去一刀斩断。如果你的过去在困扰你，你最终会厌倦去解那个结，然后干脆把它扔掉，因为你会意识到人生苦短。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And the more you have, the more you want to accomplish in this life—actually the less time you have to unravel that thing. So I just wanted to actually get things done, so I had no time to deal with it, so I just cut it. It's like a really bad relationship—but in this case it's a bad relationship with your own history. So you just drop it.
而你拥有的越多、这辈子越想成就更多，你实际上能用来解开那团东西的时间反而越少。所以我只是真的想把事情做成，根本没空去搭理它，于是我就把它斩断了。这就像一段糟糕透顶的关系——只不过这次是你跟自己历史的糟糕关系。所以你直接把它扔了。

一百零七、人生真正的货币 · 不是金钱，也不是时间，而是注意力

Chris（克里斯）：
Yeah, I think so much of what we've spoken about today is on the shortness of life and the fact that every moment is precious. You had a take about—is it that the most fundamental resource in your life is not time, it's attention?
是啊，我觉得我们今天聊的很多内容，都落在人生之短、以及每一刻都很珍贵这件事上。你有个说法是——你人生中最根本的资源不是时间，而是注意力（attention），对吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
That's right. I used to think, you know, the currency of life—people think it's money. And yes, money is important, and it does let you trade certain things for time, but it doesn't really buy your time. Ask Warren Buffett how much time money can buy you, or Michael Bloomberg. They're as rich as Scrooge McDuck, but they can't buy more time, right? Brian Johnson notwithstanding. So you can't trade money for time. Money is not the real currency of life. And time itself doesn't even mean that much, because, as we talked about before, a lot of time can be wasted because you're not really present for it, you're not paying attention. So the real currency of life is attention—it's what you choose to pay attention to and what you do about it. And so, back to the point about the news media: you can put your attention on the news, but that's how you're spending the real currency of life. So just be aware of that. If you want to, that's fine—there's no right or wrong here. Maybe it is your destiny to pick something in the news, learn about that problem, adopt that problem, and solve it. But just be careful, because your attention is the only thing that you have.
没错。我以前以为，你知道，生命的货币——人们以为是金钱。是的，钱很重要，它确实能让你用某些东西换来时间，但它并不能真正买到你的时间。去问问沃伦·巴菲特（Warren Buffett）钱能给你买来多少时间，或者迈克尔·布隆伯格（Michael Bloomberg）。他们富得像史高治·麦克老鸭（Scrooge McDuck），可还是买不到更多时间，对吧？布莱恩·约翰逊另当别论。所以你没法用钱去换时间。钱不是生命真正的货币。而时间本身其实也没那么重要，因为就像我们之前聊的，很多时间会被浪费掉——因为你并没有真正身处当下，你没在用心留意。所以生命真正的货币是注意力——是你选择把注意力放在什么上，以及你为此做了什么。所以回到新闻媒体那个点：你可以把注意力放在新闻上，但那就是你在花费生命真正的货币。所以对此心里有数就好。你要愿意，那也行——这里没有对错。也许把新闻里的某件事挑出来、去了解那个问题、把它揽下来并解决掉，正是你的天命。但要当心，因为注意力是你唯一拥有的东西。
Chris（克里斯）：
And that can also be captured by your own past?
而注意力也会被你自己的过去给俘获，对吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Yes, you can fritter it away on anything you like.
对，你可以把它挥霍在任何你愿意的东西上。

一百零八、起点是输家的好处 · 以及无法复制给孩子的东西

Chris（克里斯）：
Is there an advantage to starting out as a loser?
从一个"输家"起步，有好处吗？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Absolutely, yeah. Because if you're a loser, then you'll want to be a winner, and then you'll develop all the characteristics that'll help you be a, you know, quote-unquote winner in life. That said, I wouldn't sentence my kids to it. I don't think you can artificially do that. It's sort of like—imagine that 300 years ago you were born a serf, and then somehow you managed to escape off the farm and you become a landowner, and then eventually you become minor nobility and an aristocrat. Are you going to put your kids back on the farm and say, "You're going to be a serf again"? I know they all like those stories—the kids themselves like those stories, because it says, "I came from the school of hard knocks, my dad made me go shovel hay for a summer." It's not real. I mean, you're not going to trick them. I think all you can do is kind of cultivate an appreciation and gratitude for what you have. And the only way to do that is just to evidence it yourself, right? Just show yourself—how you spend money, how you respect it, what you do with it, how you take care of people, who you're responsible for.
当然有，是的。因为如果你是个输家，你就会想成为赢家，然后你会培养出所有那些能帮你成为——你懂的——所谓"赢家"的特质。话虽如此，我不会判我的孩子去受这个罪。我觉得你没法人为地制造它。这有点像——想象三百年前你生为农奴，后来你设法逃出了农庄，成了地主，最终又成了小贵族、上流人士。你会把孩子们重新塞回农庄，说"你们要重新当农奴"吗？我知道大家都喜欢那种故事——孩子们自己也喜欢那种故事，因为那意味着"我是从苦难学校里熬出来的，我爸让我去铲了一整个夏天的干草"。那不是真的。我是说，你骗不了他们。我觉得你能做的，就是培养他们对所拥有之物的珍惜与感恩。而做到这点的唯一办法，就是你自己亲身示范出来，对吧？把它展示给他们看——你怎么花钱、怎么尊重钱、拿它做什么、怎么照顾别人、你对谁负责。

一百零九、部落的责任 · 能者多担，社会以"地位"回报

Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And the more resources you have, the greater the tribe you can take care of—the more of the tribe you can take care of. So when you have no resources, you're struggling to take care of yourself, and at that point it's good to be selfish, because you can't save somebody else if you can't even save yourself. So you take care of yourself, and you become the best version of yourself. But there are too many men who are able, fit, and have some money who are doing nothing with their lives. They're just sitting at home doing nothing, just indulging themselves. Maybe they go on dates and they get DoorDash. Like, I have no respect for that. I think there's nothing worse in society than a lazy man, because he's sort of leaving it all on the table—he's leaving his potential on the table. It's bad for him. So the next thing you do is you go and you have a family and you take care of your family, take care of that tribe. Then you take care of your extended family—you take care of your cousins, brothers, uncles, grandmothers, aunts, sisters, everybody that you can. And then if you have more resources beyond that, then you go take care of your local tribe—you take care of your people, you start trying to do some good for the world. And if you have more resources than that, you go take care of an even bigger tribe. And that's how you earn both respect and self-confidence, and you live up to your potential. So the more you have, the more is rightfully expected of you. And I think it's a good compact with society when highly capable people express and flex that capability by giving more and more and by doing more and more.
而你拥有的资源越多，你能照顾的部落就越大——你能照顾的部落成员就越多。所以当你毫无资源时，你光是养活自己都很吃力，那个阶段自私是好事，因为你连自己都救不了，就救不了别人。所以你先照顾好自己，把自己变成最好的版本。但有太多身强体健、又有点钱的男人，正虚度此生。他们就窝在家里无所事事，只顾享乐。也许约约会、点点 DoorDash 外卖。这种人我一点都看不起。我觉得社会上没有比一个懒汉更糟糕的了，因为他等于把一切都白白浪费了——他把自己的潜能扔在桌上没用。这对他自己是坏事。所以接下来你要做的，是去成家、照顾你的家庭、照顾那个部落。然后照顾你的大家族——照顾你的表亲、兄弟、叔伯、祖母、姑姨、姐妹，所有你照顾得过来的人。如果之后你还有更多资源，就去照顾你身边的部落——照顾你的同胞，开始试着为世界做点好事。如果你的资源还更多，就去照顾一个更大的部落。这就是你同时赢得尊重与自信、并不辜负自己潜能的方式。所以你拥有得越多，社会对你正当的期待也越高。我觉得，当能力很强的人通过越来越多地给予、越来越多地行动来施展和发挥这份能力时，这就是与社会达成的一份好约定。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
And society rewards them with the one thing they can't get otherwise, which is status. So society should give you status in exchange for it. They should say, "Okay, you did a good job. You took care of more people than just yourself and just the people immediately around you." And that's what an alpha male is, to me. An alpha male is not the one who gets to eat first—the alpha male eats last. The alpha male feeds everybody else first and then gets to eat last. And they do that out of their own self-respect and pride. And society rewards them by calling them an alpha and giving them status.
而社会会用一样他们靠别的方式得不到的东西来回报他们，那就是地位（status）。所以社会应当以地位作为交换给你。社会应该说："好，你干得不错。你照顾的人，不只是你自己、也不只是你身边那一圈人。"在我看来，这才是真正的"阿尔法男（alpha male）"。阿尔法男不是那个抢先吃饭的人——阿尔法男最后吃。阿尔法男先让所有人都吃上，自己最后才吃。他们这么做是出于自己的自尊和骄傲。而社会回报他们的方式，就是称他们为阿尔法、并给予他们地位。

一百一十、别打击行善者 · 对慈善的反弹是在帮倒忙

Chris（克里斯）：
I wonder whether some of the pushback that we've got against rich, wealthy, powerful people is disincentivizing.
我在想，我们对富人、有钱有势之人的那些反弹，是不是在打消他们行善的积极性。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
It is.
确实是。
Chris（克里斯）：
Who is it—Zuck, who donated money to Zuckerberg General Hospital, and they wanted to pull his name off of it?
是谁来着——小扎（Zuck），他给"扎克伯格综合医院"（Zuckerberg General Hospital）捐了钱，结果有人想把他的名字从医院上摘掉？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I didn't see that, but that's ridiculous. That kind of stuff backfires. You should reward people for doing good things. What were you saying before? You don't just need to—in fact, actually actively avoid castigating people if you want their behavior to change. When they get something wrong, reinforce it when they get something right. It's happening at a societal level as well.
这事我没看到，但那太荒唐了。这种做法会适得其反。你应该奖励做好事的人。你之前说什么来着？你不仅要——其实，如果你想改变某人的行为，就应当主动避免去斥责他。他做错时，要在他做对时去强化它。这种道理在整个社会层面同样成立。
Chris（克里斯）：
Correct.
没错。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
I mean, like, the guys who make a lot of money and go out and buy sports teams—I wouldn't do that, right? But the one who goes out and builds a hospital, or builds a rocket to take people to the moon, rescues some astronauts—you should be rewarding him for that.
我是说，比如那些赚了大钱跑去买体育球队的人——我不会那么做，对吧？但那个跑去建医院、或者造火箭把人送上月球、去救了几个宇航员的人——你应该为这些事去奖励他。

一百一十一、节目收尾 · "别期待什么"——最纳瓦尔的告别

Chris（克里斯）：
Naval, I really appreciate you. I hope that this has lived up to whatever weird daydreams you've been having. What have you got coming up? What can people expect from you over the next however long?
纳瓦尔，我真的很感激你。希望这场对谈没有辜负你之前那些古怪的白日梦。你接下来有什么计划？在往后不论多长的时间里，大家能从你这儿期待些什么？
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Expect nothing.
别期待什么。
Chris（克里斯）：
That's the most Naval way that we could have finished this. Dude, it's been a long time coming, and I really do appreciate you for being here today.
这是我们能给这场对谈画上句号的、最"纳瓦尔"的方式了。老兄，这一天等了很久，我真的很感激你今天能来。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
But I do hope to deliver something.
不过我确实希望能拿出点东西来。
Chris（克里斯）：
Oh, I think you have. Thank you.
哦，我觉得你已经做到了。谢谢你。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
Thanks for having me.
谢谢你请我来。
Chris（克里斯）：
Thank you too. Thanks for getting in my mind—and hopefully now you're out.
也谢谢你。谢谢你钻进了我的脑子里——希望现在你已经出来了。
Naval（纳瓦尔）：
We'll see. It might be even worse now—you've got the real memories to stick. I don't know. The reason to win the game is to be free of it. The reason to do the podcast is to be done with it.
那就走着瞧吧。现在说不定更糟了——你已经有了真实的记忆，挥之不去了。我也说不准。赢得这场游戏的理由，是为了从中解脱。做这期播客的理由，是为了和它了结。
Chris（克里斯）：
All right.
好的。
Chris（克里斯）：
Wow, you made it to the end. Congratulations. Well, if you enjoyed that, you're gonna love my full-length conversation with the one and only Alain de Botton from the School of Life right here. Go on. Press it.
哇，你看到最后了，恭喜。那么，如果你喜欢这一期，你一定会爱上我和"人生学校"（School of Life）独一无二的阿兰·德波顿（Alain de Botton）的完整对谈，就在这儿。来吧，点下去。
